1.	Introduction

Big Five Profile is designed to measure general personality traits based on the “BIG FIVE” construct of personality. This construct purports the existence of 5 essential traits viz. Openness, Conscientiousness, Extraversion, Agreeableness and Neuroticism, collectively referred as "OCEAN", as the building blocks of personality.

The questionnaire consists of 15 sets of 4 statements in forced choice format, on a 4 points rating scale - 1 for most and 4 for least. The estimated time to complete the test online is 5-7 minutes.


This test gives a global personality profile of the test taker by assessing the relative strength on each of the five major traits. It is recommended for entrance examinations in schools, colleges and institutions; as a screening tool to shortlist candidates for interviews; and as a prelude to feedback sessions in personality development programs.



1.1	What the test taker/test administrator would get?

•	A graphical representation indicating the candidate’s personality on the five behavioral traits.

•	A	detailed	description	of	the	person’s	personality	on	each	trait	with personalised comments.

•	A tabular representation of the result that describes the candidate’s main personality factors and opposing factors.

•	A detailed description including particular strength and weakness of the test taker on the basis of his/her strongest personality trait.












User Manual – Big Five Profile © Central Test 2016	p. 3
 
1.2	What it measures? Specificity and theoretical background of the test

The Big Five Profile is based on the 'Big Five' theory of personality, pioneered in a popular study in the year 1963 by psychologist Warren Norman. The term "Big Five" was coined by Lewis R. Goldberg and was originally associated with studies of personality traits used in a natural language. The test measures what many psychologists consider to be the five fundamental dimensions of personality. However, the test is designed to be used in corporate settings and certain expressions are modified accordingly.


Personality psychologists are primarily interested in knowing two things: what differentiates one person from another, and why we behave the way we do. The Big Five model of personality tries to answer the first question.


The Big Five was originally derived in the 1970's by two independent teams of researchers - Paul Costa and Robert McCrae (at the National Institute of Health), and Warren Norman (at the University of Michigan)/Lewis R. Goldberg (at the University of Oregon) - who took slightly different routes at arriving at the same results: most human personality traits can be boiled down to five broad dimensions of personality, regardless of language or culture. These five dimensions were derived by asking thousands of people, hundreds of questions and then analyzing the data with a statistical procedure known as factor analysis.


It is important to realize that the researchers did not set out to find five dimensions, but that five dimensions emerged from their analyses of data. In scientific circles, the Big Five is now the most widely accepted and used model of personality. The Big Five Profile has gained extreme importance in the corporate world in identifying and in making the best use of a person's skills and inherent qualities.



















User Manual – Big Five Profile © Central Test 2016	p. 4
 
2.	Factor Descriptions


Consciousness of others
This is the tendency to be pleasant and accommodating, gracious and cooperative in social interactions.
High Score
Sensitive, cooperative, empathetic and altruistic, consensual. Influenced by feelings.	Low score
Demanding, Pragmatic, dominant, tenacious, non-accommodating. May lack sensitivity.
Sociability/Dynamism
This is the tendency to be concerned with the outside world and to constantly look for gratification. It deals with enjoying human interactions, being enthusiastic, talkative, confident and sociable. It also deals with taking pleasure in activities that involve large social groups.
High Score
Looks for thrills, energetic and enthusiastic. Likes the company of others and to communicate.	Low Score
Remains calm, focused, subjective and individualistic. A deep thinker and likes quietness.
Emotional Balance
This is the tendency to remain calm and composed and face stressful situations with confidence.
High Score
Lethargic, faces situations with confidence, thoughtful & optimistic. Has a balanced mood and composed disposition.	Low Score
Strong emotional reactivity, inconsistent and extra sensitive to criticism. Has a tendency to be anxious or even depressed.
Meticulousness
This is the tendency to show self-discipline, act dutifully, to aspire achievement and success. It also deals with planning things rather than behaving spontaneously.
High Score
Self-disciplined, critical, methodical, conscious of everything. Prefers planned rather than spontaneous behaviour.	Low Score
Improvises processes, although he/she may be a little disorganized. Impulsive and likes to be guided by his/her intuition.
Openness/Imagination
This is a general appreciation for art, emotion, adventure, original ideas, curiosity and diversity of experiences. It is a tendency to fluctuate with new thoughts, ideas and changes.

High Score
Likes variety, imaginative, sensitive to aesthetics. Has a great curiosity, both for the internal and external world.	
Low Score
Likes what is established and familiar, needs stability. Does not prefer to leave a job unfinished or put off making a decision.




User Manual – Big Five Profile © Central Test 2016	p. 5
 
3.	Psychometric properties

Periodic assessment of sensitivity and fine-tuning the test are crucial for long- term survival and effectiveness of any psychometric test. Like all the other tests developed by Central Test, the Big 5 Profile-English Edition has also been subject to a series of validation studies since its first online publication in May, 2008.

It can be seen from this table that mean scores of all the five factors is between
4.	5 and 5.5 and standard deviations is between 1.5 to 2.0; it indicates that findings are close to a normal distribution and thus support the empirical evidence that Big Five factor scores are normally distributed in the population. From an assessment point of view, it provides good evidence that the tool (Big Five Profile-English Edition) is corresponding well with its theoretical assumptions.


# Mean and standard deviation together provide the estimate of a candidate’s position in the population of reference.
# Standard error of measurement provides an estimate of error that can be involved in measurement. Most standard psychometric tests have a standard error of 1.0 to 1.50; the lesser the standard error in comparison to mean score, the greater the accuracy of the test to assess that particular characteristic.

Factor	No. of	Alpha
	items	Reliability
Consciousness of Others	12	0.74
Sociability/Dynamism	12	0.66
Emotional Balance	12	0.70
Meticulousness	12	0.76

*n=550


In behavioral sciences, an alpha reliability of over .65 is considered to be very good. As we can see from this table, the alpha reliability for all the factors is ranging from 0.66 (Sociability/Dynamism) to 0.76 (Meticulousness). It can further be observed that constructs that are wide and diverse in their conceptualization like sociability/dynamism and openness/imagination (as the meaning from sociability/dynamism and openness/imagination changes to some degree in different contexts) have lower alpha and constructs with relatively narrow definition (e.g. Meticulousness) have a higher alpha. This supports the construct validity of the test.

# Alpha reliability provides an estimate of the variance among the scores on items that can be explained by dimension score.


3.3	Validity

Validity of a psychometric tool may be assessed in a variety of ways and it is always recommended to use multiple methods to examine validity, in order to have a psychometrically sound and practically useful test. In its essence, validity refers to the extent a tool is able to demonstrate that it is measuring what it is supposed to. For example, if a tool claims to measure extraversion it should measure all the facets of extraversion and should not measure anything other than extraversion (to take an analogy from natural science, a thermometer should measure only temperature and not pressure or humidity).










User Manual – Big Five Profile © Central Test 2016	p. 7
 
In the field of psychology, validity of a tool is assessed by various methods depending upon the construct under assessment. Normally, these methods revolve around assessing three distinct qualities:

	Representation of the concept:

It refers to the extent the test items are in agreement with their underlying construct. It was examined by giving the preliminary test items to a group of five experts with factor descriptions of all the five factors. They were asked to put each item in one of the five factors as per their agreement with the factor description. Only items that yielded high consistency across experts were selected for the final questionnaire.

	Criterion validity:

Criterion Validity is the extent to which factor scores indicate correspondence with identified empirical criteria. For example, it is observed that high performing sales people show a high level of sociability and emotional balance and people in maintenance planning have a high level of meticulousness and a low level of openness/imagination.


Empirically verifiable criteria like these were identified and the relationship between these criteria and factor scores was examined. A detailed account of these analyses is beyond the scope of this document, however here is a description of the process followed to examine criterion validity:


A.	Five distinct occupational groups were identified as criterion groups and hypotheses were formed regarding the expected difference among different groups on each factor.


To test these hypotheses, a one-way ANOVA was performed. Most of the groups were having significantly high scores on one or two factors that are thought to be important in performing duties associated with that particular occupation (p<0.005). For example, most successful salespeople were found to have a high score on sociability/dynamism and emotional balance.

Introduction
VOCATION is a career interest assessment tool designed to analyse the occupational and vocational preferences of individuals. Based on John Holland’s (1959) model of occupational interests (Holland Occupational Themes), VOCATION provides the user with an informative evaluation of the occupational areas that suit their interests.
VOCATION is an updated version of Central Test’s Occupational Interest Inventory-R. This new version includes various benefits such as:
●	More contemporary questions reflecting modern day vocations and fields
●	Short and easy-to-understand questionnaire
●	More situational questions
●	Average completion time of 8 minutes
●	Ipsative scaling to guard against social desirability and impression management
●	Developed using state-of-the-art psychometric techniques such as Thurstonian Item Response Theory
●	Practical and easy-to understand reports
●	Contemporary 3 factor combination profiles
In this manual and user guide you will find useful information about the questionnaire development, theoretical background, psychometric properties and practical applications.

Chapters 1, 2, 3 and 8 are specifically tailored for HR practitioners, while Chapters 4, 5, 6 and 7 comprise of detailed technical and psychometric information.
 
1.	General Description and Applicability
VOCATION maps out the test-taker’s career interests across six major factors. Given below are the
definitions for each factor.
Realistic
Individuals with "realistic" interests tend to like physical and/or manual activities. Driven by concrete results, they are drawn to tasks that require great dexterity and physical endurance, and as a result are likely to enjoy outdoor activities or those that involve working manually with their hands, such as fixing and assembling objects.
Investigative
This factor corresponds to individuals that enjoy investigating theoretical knowledge. They like to solve problems, conduct surveys and research information in order to better understand a subject or the environment around them.
Artistic
Those with an interest in “artistic” activities are likely to want to express themselves through creative means such as music, drawing and art. They tend to be interested in activities that allow them to be intuitive and imaginative, and may not enjoy being part of projects that are too conventional, with strict methods that allow for little improvisation.
Social
Individuals with an interest in “social” activities like to be around people, communicating with and/or helping them wherever possible. They may have a preference for activities that allow them to meet new people and to have a certain sense of community or of being part of a group.
Enterprising
Individuals with an interest in "enterprising" activities tend to enjoy tasks that involve an aspect of risk-taking and influencing others. They may enjoy having to persuade others of their ideas and/or leading or managing people. They are also likely to be dynamic and take initiatives.
Conventional
Individuals with an interest in “conventional” activities have a preference for conducting work in an orderly and meticulous fashion. They are likely to want to stick to a certain structure in the tasks that they undertake and to be organised. They are likely to enjoy tasks that require precision and attention to detail and that relate to dealing with numerical data.

1.2.	Questionnaire Structure
Format
Unlike traditional questionnaire-based career interest inventories, questions in VOCATION are presented in a forced choice format whereby respondents select the answer that best represents their preference from two listed options. This forces respondents to select the option that is most appealing to them. Forced-choice formats reduce response bias (Chan, 2003; ten Holt, van Duijn & Boomsma, 2010), susceptibility to impression management (Baron, 1996; Bartram, 2007) and are generally more difficult to fake (Brown & Maydeu-Olivares, 2011). Forced-choice formats make it impossible to provide the same response on all items thereby resulting in greater differentiation of scores within a profile (Cruise, 2011/2012). Recruiters, career counsellors and HR practitioners can therefore be confident that responses are more accurate representations of individual work preferences.
 
Questions
The questionnaire consists of 60 questions that measure 6 factors and 12 facets. All questions must be answered in order for respondents to progress to the next stage of the assessment. Shown below is a sample question.


1.3.	Mode of Administration, Languages and Special Requirements
VOCATION is administered online and works with all modern computer operating systems including Windows, MAC-OS and Linux. A PDF version of the questionnaire is available, if required.

Like other modern questionnaires, the assessment can be administered remotely and without supervision. However, should standardised group assessments be required, this can easily be accommodated. Reports are solely generated online. Therefore, if the test has been administered in paper-format, an administrator must manually enter candidate responses into the online platform of the questionnaire to generate candidate reports.
The questionnaire is currently available in English and will soon be available in other languages. For more information on language availability please contact the Central Test Consulting Service (consulting@centraltest.com).
VOCATION was designed to be easily used by individuals from diverse backgrounds and by those with a range of special needs. All test-takers are able to adjust visual screen specifications based on individual requirements. For example, respondents with visual impairments or those requiring a larger font size for comfort, may increase the font size by enlarging the computer screen‘s display (by pressing "CTRL" and "+"). Similarly, respondents are able to access test instructions throughout the course of the assessment. It is also possible to navigate through questions or change answers as desired by selecting on-screen prompts. Alternatively, for blind candidates or those with severe visual disabilities, items may be read aloud by a trained administrator. Individuals with hearing impairments are not excluded from taking the questionnaire since all content is presented visually. Although the average time for completion is 8 minutes, VOCATION has no time limit. Therefore, individuals with learning disabilities are able to take the time they need. Administrators are encouraged to consider whether a candidate who needed an unusual amount of time to take the test may have special needs. Please contact the test publisher for guidance on administering the questionnaire to individuals or groups with special requirements.
1.4.	Social Desirability
Social desirability is the tendency to present oneself in a manner that will be viewed favourably by others. When done unintentionally, it is referred to as self-deception. When done intentionally, it is referred to as impression management (Tett & Simonet, 2011). As people are social by nature, they
 
are generally inclined to seek some degree of social acceptance. Unlike aptitude tests, the results of questionnaires like VOCATION are affected by impression management. An individual‘s attempt to present himself/herself in a favourable light negatively impacts test results, whether the questionnaire is completed online or in paper-based format (Dodou & de Winter, 2014).

Candidates are usually tempted to answer in a way that would impress the recruiter even though there is no right or wrong answer. Candidates may provide responses according to what they feel others may expect to fulfil a role. As a result, social desirability is a significant risk to the validity of assessments (Mueller-Hanson, Heggestad, & Thornton III, 2003). If social desirability was equivalent across all candidates, then test administrators would be able to factor it into the scoring and interpretation of results. However, faking differs across individuals, resulting in distortion of scores.
VOCATION actively guards against social desirability with the use of an ipsative (forced choice) questionnaire format. Research has found ipsative measures to be more resistant to distorted responding compared to more commonly used Likert-scales (Bowen, Martin, & Hunt, 2002; Kluger, Reilly, & Russell, 1991). Not only does the ipsative format reduce the candidate's’ ability to inflate their scores, but items are also combined in such a manner that makes it difficult for candidates to conceive a response strategy, as items are paired with equally appealing response options.
1.5.	Administration Instructions
In most cases the assessment will be taken remotely through online access, and without supervision. However, in the case of taking the assessment within a supervised setting, the following guidelines should be applied.
Although instructions for completing VOCATION are presented on-screen, it is important that administrators communicate with the candidates. Prior to starting the assessment, instructions should be read aloud by the administrator, and candidates should be given the opportunity to ask questions before taking the assessment. Administrators should verify that candidates understand what they are required to do prior to starting the assessment.
Test instructions are the same for every individual. Standardised instructions ensure that all participants are exposed to similar conditions during the assessment process, to ensure that it is fair and objective, and that assessment conditions do not directly or indirectly give any individual or group an advantage.
Further tips:
●	If candidates have questions after the assessment session has begun, the administrator should try to answer them without disturbing other candidates.
●	If a candidate does not understand a question, the administrator should answer carefully and avoid explaining the meaning of words or questions in his/her own words in order to not influence the candidate.
●	If an administrator suspects that a candidate‘s lack of understanding is due to their
education level, the assessment should be stopped.
●	If an administrator suspects that a candidate‘s lack of understanding is due to language
proficiency, the administrator should ask the candidate to take the assessment in the
 
language they are most comfortable with.
●	If an administrator suspects that a candidate is being over cautious when responding, the candidate should be encouraged to endorse their most truthful response.

Candidates receive a notification to take the questionnaire via e-mail with an embedded web link and login details. Web links to the questionnaire are password protected and unique to the candidate‘s account. It is possible to set expiration dates in order to ensure that candidates complete the questionnaire within the specified time-frame. It is possible to configure the invitation in the way that the assessment must be completed at the same IP address once the questionnaire has been accessed from a particular IP address.
VOCATION has no time restriction. However, candidates generally take about 8 minutes to complete the questionnaire. For monitoring purposes, completion times are recorded to allow administrators to see if a candidate rushed through the questionnaire or needed an unusual amount of time to complete the test. Candidates are encouraged to answer questions spontaneously, without rushing, in order to avoid spending too much time thinking about each statement; there are is right or wrong answer.
1.6.	Technical and Security Issues
This chapter details guidelines governing the security of administering VOCATION. These guidelines ensure that every administration of VOCATION is a pleasant, objective and fair process. Security specifications ensure that candidate information is protected both during administration and after assessment completion.
1.6.1.	Respecting the Ethical Charter
As a publisher of online assessment tools, Central Test respects the legal conventions of French Labour Law and the regulatory provisions resulting from the law of 6 January 1978 pertaining to information technology, files and freedoms: Declaration N°1834067.
Test results may not be forwarded to a third person without the explicit consent of the test candidate. A candidate has the right to access their results.
The assessor must grant them access automatically or when the candidate requests it.
The candidate has the right to request that all his/her personal data be permanently deleted. To do this, s/he must contact either the organisation or Central Test directly, who will delete his/her data.
1.6.2.	Confidentiality and Security of User Data
Central Test guarantees the security and confidentiality of data generated during the administration of online tests. Access codes are encrypted in the database and cannot be accessed by anyone.
Passwords are computationally generated and are not visible to companies. A URL rewriting module (secure link generator) is installed and has transparent parameter passing for the user.
Browsing and data exchange via the Central Test application can be secured and encrypted using SSL. Secure browsing is guaranteed by the https protocol, which is represented by an icon in the
 
form of a padlock in the internet browser. The Central Test application has a « GeoTrust SSL True BusinessID » digital signature, issued and certified by GeoTrust©. This certificate is irrefutable evidence of our serious attitude towards security and protects all Central Test assessments from phishing attacks. Invitations to take tests are made via secure links that are of limited use (in terms of numbers and duration). The tests are timed and IP addresses are tracked. Access to questionnaires may be limited in terms of numbers and duration.
1.6.3.	Hardware Architecture and Server Availability
Central Test’s architecture consists of a network of 10 physical dedicated servers providing high- quality service.
Each server is housed in a specialised data centre featuring:
●	Dedicated lines
●	A guaranteed bandwidth of 100Mbps (per server)
●	Redundant energy sources
●	4-hour Equipment Restoration time
●	2-hour Incident Intervention time
●	Monitoring and assistance 24/7/365
Access to the server administration is restricted to an IP address and is password-secured. Data exchanges are secure.

●	Internet Explorer 8 or above

Reports can be generated within minutes of candidates completing the questionnaire. VOCATION report is user friendly and customisable. Report content can be tailored depending on the needs of the administrator. Separate reports may be configured for the candidate and the company, recruiter, coach or psychologist. Reports are available in PDF format.

2.	Conceptual Framework and Development
2.1.	Theoretical Background
John Holland’s Model
The Holland Typology, also known as the RIASEC Model, was developed by Holland (1959) and is based on his theory that a person’s vocational interest or career choice is determined by underlying “personality types”. The model is popular because of its practical applicability, simple structure, and availability in order to assess career interests.
Although every individual is complex, Holland (1977) believes that everyone can be easily categorised into 6 basic personality types or a combination of the 6 types – Doers, Thinkers, Creators, Helpers, Persuaders, and Organizers. Holland (1997) hypothesised that an individual’s career choice is an expression of his personality, and that the RIASEC Model can also be used to identify appropriate work environments (Gottfredson, 1999).
Holland (1959) developed his theory throughout his career, starting in the 1950’s when he enrolled in the army to serve as a psychological assistant. Nauta (2010) proposed that many of Holland’s ideas for his theory are a result of his graduate training and the frustrations he experienced when providing career counselling services. Nauta (2010) also states that when Holland was serving in the army, he realised that people fall into categorical vocational personalitytypes. Holland observed army recruits being classified based on the results of an interview, and this is when the idea to sort individuals into set psychological types struck him (Nauta, 2010).
Holland, being experienced in the field of career counselling, began to notice the setbacks of the career tests available at that time. Most of these tests consisted of long scoring items and there was no set list of occupations that could be matched with the results of the assessments. This led Holland (1985) to design the Vocational Preference Inventory (VPI).
Holland continued to work on his theory for the next few years. He also published the Self-Directed Search in 1970, which has since undergone four revisions. Holland also published the Occupations Finder (1977), which is an occupational classification system. Although Holland retired in 1980, he continued to work on his theory. In 1997, he revised it again and added an environmental identity
 
aspect to it (Staff, 2008).
Based on six personality types, Holland developed the RIASEC Model. RIASEC is an acronym for Realistic (Doers), Investigative (Thinkers), Artistic (Creators), Social (Helpers), Enterprising (Persuaders) and Conventional (Organizers). Each type is characterised by a constellation of interests, preferred activities, beliefs, abilities, values, and characteristics (Nauta, 2010).
According to Nauta (2010), Holland stressed upon the fact that most people look for a work environment that allows them to use the skills and abilities that they possess, and take on roles that they are comfortable with.
Holland’s theory is widely accepted and is instrumental in bringing about a radical paradigm shift in the measurement of career-related decisions, satisfaction and stability of work (Bullock, Andrews and Braud, 2010).



Links between the RIASEC dimensions and personality traits
Figure 1. The structural framework of Holland’s (1977) RIASEC Modelof Occupational Themes
represented as a hexagon.
A significant amount of research has been conducted on the possible relationship between the RIASEC dimensions, and personality traits. Larson, Rottinghaus and Borgen (2002) conducted a meta- analysis on the relationship between Holland’s RIASEC dimensions and the Big Five Personality factors (consisting of Openness, Conscientiousness, Extraversion, Agreeableness and Neuroticism), (Costa & McCrae, 1992). They found that there were significant correlations for men and women between the Artistic dimension and ‘Openness’ from the Big Five, Enterprising and ‘Extraversion,’ Social and ‘Extraversion,’ Investigative and ‘Openness,’ as well as Social and ‘Agreeableness.’ Barrick
 
et al (2003) also found support for these relationships, as well as a further overlap between Conventional and ‘Conscientiousness’ and Social and ‘Agreeableness.’ However, research does not appear to show any correlation between the RIASEC Model and Neuroticism (Schinka, Dye, & Curtiss, 1997).
This research suggests that it is possible to infer occupational interests from the personality traits of an individual, and therefore that it may be possible to assume personality traits from one’s occupational interests. However, research would suggest that occupational interests are more predictive of choice of employment than personality is (Fruyt & Mervielde, 1999), which can in turn be an accurate predictor of workplace success (Iddekinge, Putka, Roth, & Lanivich, 2011).
Sub-division of the RIASEC factors
Research has found that there are considerable differences between aspects within each dimension of Holland’s RIASEC model (1958). The six factors; Realistic, Investigative, Artistic, Social, Enterprising and Conventional are broad, and considering the complexity of modern day occupational fields, perhaps should be simplified or subdivided in order to represent a larger range of individual differences. Dingemanse, Van Amstel, De Fruyt, & Wille (2007) further explored the idea that there should be more separation within RIASEC types rather than just between them. They created the Career Insight Questionnaire (CIQ), in which they constructed 15 “building blocks of the Holl and
types” (Dingemanse, Van Amstel, De Fruyt & Wille, 2015, p237) from the original six factor RIASEC
model. For example, Realistic was sub-divided into ‘Handcraft,’ ‘Outdoor,’ and ‘Mechanics/Construction,’ and Investigative was sub-divided into ‘Science’ and ‘Theory.’ Then
comparing their factors with Costa and McCrae’s Big Five personality traits, Dingemanse et al (2015) found that correlations between personality traits from the Big Five were not consistent across aspects of each of the RIASEC factors, when sub-divided. For example, a negative correlation was found between Agreeableness and Holland’s Investigative factor, but only for the scientific component of this factor, suggesting that an individual who scores highly on agreeableness, may have an interest in only part of Holland’s ‘I’ interest.
Based on this research, it was decided that splitting each of the RIASEC factors into two parts for VOCATION would yield a more accurate representation of the range of existing occupational interests. Further details of these subdivisions can be found in Chapter 2.2.3. ‘Factor Definitions’.
2.2.	Development of the Test
VOCATION was developed from a thorough multi-stage process. The precursor to the questionnaire (Occupational Interest Inventory-R) was first developed in 2006. Questionnaire items and factors have been updated in order to ensure a) that items reflect contemporary ways of working, b) effective prevention of faking and social bias, c) that practitioners have a tool that improves the accuracy of assessment decisions and d) that individuals are clearly guided when assessing fit with desired roles and careers. As well as these aspects, throughout the revision of this assessment, it was made sure that the structure of factors and items met the highest standards of reliability and validity.
2.2.1.	Factors and Item Construction
 
VOCATION was developed from the bank of items used from the original Occupational Interest Inventory-R questionnaire. The original questionnaire was reviewed in three major steps:
1.	Feedback was collected from client interviews and training workshop feedback forms in order to be able to ensure that the questionnaire and the factors measured meet their needs.
2.	A panel of experts consisting of psychometricians and researchers then reviewed the items
for face validity purposes and to give their expert opinion.
3.	The questionnaire was pretested six times with a large and representative sample of people. Results were analysed and items were removed and/or changed based on their psychometric qualities, in order to achieve the best reliability and validity.
All changes were made in order to ensure that all aspects of VOCATION accurately measure contemporary occupational domains and interests. Items were checked for cultural bias, clarity and accuracy. Two examples of changed items resulting from this process are depicted in Table 3 below.


Table 3. Examples of changed items resulting from review and feedback.

Factor, Facet	Original Item	VOCATION
Artistic, A1	Assist with writing the script.	Express yourself through the
writing of a script.
Enterprising, E2	Floor manager, ensuring the
smooth running of the
channel.	Ensuring that the teams are
working efficiently.


2.2.2.	Pretests
Pretests were conducted with a range of male and female professionals across the globe to ensure that items were accurate, relevant, clear, concise, culture-free, bias-free and reflected targeted work behaviours. After six English pretests, the final questionnaire was piloted with 309 English-speaking professionals from the United Kingdom. Pretest samples comprised of native English speakers from different continents in order to establish the questionnaire as an international measure of occupational interests.
Upon collection of pretest data, composite reliabilities for each dimension were calculated from generated item factor loadings. Factors were flagged if reliabilities were less than 0.55. Factors that did not converge were inspected for poorly-performing items which were then reviewed, modified or changed and then subjected to another round of pretest.
2.2.3.	Factor Definitions
In light of the findings that the RIASEC factors are rather broad and should be further split up, as discussed in Chapter 2.1., VOCATION comprises of the original six factors, but each divided into two
 
further facets.
On the test user’s report, each factor is presented with a short indication of the personality traits, values, potential aptitudes, learning style and environment that may be endorsed for the individual, based on his/her significant interest in the factor. The personality aspects were developed using Holland’s (1985) research, as well as further research conducted on the relationship between personality traits and the RIASEC model, as discussed in Chapter 2.1. The values associated with each factor signify the values which may be of importance to the individual. The potential aptitudes are the possible skills that the individual may hold. Learning style relates to the method of learning which may be most beneficial to the individual when taking in and comprehending new information. The environment then relates to the style of work environment that is likely to suit the individual, based on his/her interests.
The definitions for each factor, as well as their facet definitions and the additional information related to personality aspects, values, potential aptitudes, learning style and environment, are presented below.



Realistic
Individuals with "realistic" interests tend to like physical and/or manual activities. Driven by concrete results, they are drawn to tasks that require great dexterity and physical endurance, and as a result are likely to enjoy outdoor activities or those that involve working manually with the hands, such as fixing and assembling objects. This factor is divided into R1 and R2, as below:

(R1) Physical & Outdoor Activities: Refers to the desire to engage in physical activities and work outside of an office space and/or confined area.

(R2) Manual & Technical Interests: Refers to the desire to make concrete objects, and to engage in activities that involve manual work and/or the use of machinery.

Personality - Tough, genuine, natural and practical.

Values - Common sense, pragmatism and effort.

Potential aptitudes - Dexterity, comfortable with technical tasks, mechanical intelligence and physical stamina.

Learning style - A concrete and practical approach, where information is learnt through applying examples.
Environment - Most comfortable in environments which allow them to obtain concrete results, to use technical equipment or to work on-site. They tend to dislike being confined to indoor areas such as an office.
 
 

Investigative
This factor corresponds to individuals that enjoy investigating theoretical knowledge. They like to solve problems, conduct surveys and research information in order to better understand a subject or the environment around them. This factor encompasses both I1 and I2, as shown below:


(I1) Intellectual Curiosity & Learning: Refers to the preference for activities that require critical thinking and the desire to gain knowledge.

(I2) Science & Technology: Refers to an interest towards science and new technologies, as well as the desire to investigate and research such topics.

Personality - Curious about everything, and constantly seeking to learn about and understand their environment. They are often precise, intellectual and have a scientific mindset.

Values - Intellectual curiosity, critical thinking and logic.

Potential aptitudes - Research, logical reasoning, learning and analytical capabilities.

Learning style - Best suited to an approach that uses theoretical and systematic research. Environment - Most comfortable in thought-oriented environments that favour expertise over productivity.



Artistic
Those with an interest in "artistic" activities are likely to want to express themselves through creative means such as music, drawing and art. They may feel the need to express their ideas and feelings in this way, and want the independence to do so. They tend to be interested in activities that permit them to be intuitive and imaginative, and may not have a desire to be a part of projects that are too conventional, with strict methods that allow for little improvisation. This factor is divided into A1 and A2, as below:

(A1) Aesthetic Sense & Expression: Refers to an interest in activities that require artistic sensitivity
and expressing one’s emotions in different forms.

(A2) Creativity & Design: Refers to a preference to conceive, imagine and innovate.

Personality - Intuitive, creative, idealistic and independent. Sensitive and attuned to art and aesthetics.

Values - An eye for beauty, originality, imagination and freedom.
 
Potential aptitudes - Originality, imagination and the ability to 'think outside of the box'/innovate.

Learning style - Best suited to a method that involves creativity, intuition, communicating through art, words, speech and visualising/conceptualising ideas.

Environment - Comfortable in environments that promote the expression of ideas and creativity, and where methods are not overly defined or structured.



Social
Individuals with an interest in "social" activities like to be around people, communicating with and/or helping them wherever possible. They may have a preference for meeting new people, as well as feeling a sense of co-operation or being part of a group. Furthermore, they are likely to want to be able to express themselves, as well as patiently listen to, and be attentive to, the needs of others. This factor encompasses both S1 and S2, as below:

(S1) Dedication to Others: Refers to a desire to help others, listen, inform, train, give advice and be helpful to the community.

(S2) Personal Relationships: Refers to a preference to be in contact with other people, explain, counsel and communicate with clients or co-workers.

Personality - Patient, understanding and attentive to others. They also tend to be kind, warm and welcoming.
Values - Altruism, cooperation and generosity.

Potential aptitudes - Empathy, listening and communication.

Learning style - Learn by sharing with others and by working as a team.

Environment - Comfortable in social environments that encourage personal contact, collaboration and communication.


Enterprising
Individuals with an interest in "enterprising" activities tend to enjoy tasks that involve an aspect of risk-taking and influencing others. They may enjoy selling and persuading others of their ideas and/or leading or managing others. They are also likely to enjoy communicating with others and taking initiatives. This factor is divided into E1 and E2, as below:

(E1) Enterprising: Refers to an inclination to develop new activities, materialise ideas or projects, take risks and manage difficult situations.

(E2) Leadership: Refers to the desire to encourage, motivate and manage or lead a team/other
 
people.

Personality - Open, dynamic, assertive and entrepreneurial.

Values - Taking risks, status and competition.

Potential aptitudes - Take initiatives, lead and motivate others.

Learning style - Learn by doing and physically taking part in the type of situation that they are learning about.

Environment - Comfortable in competitive environments where they can take risks and push themselves out of their comfort zone in order to move up quickly.



Conventional
Individuals with an interest in "conventional" activities have a preference for conducting work in an orderly and meticulous fashion. They are likely to want to stick to a certain structure in the tasks that they undertake, to be organised and efficient with their time. They may as a result pay attention to small details, and be inclined towards projects involving numerical aspects, which would require such a precise approach. This factor is divided into C1 and C2, as below:

(C1) Methodical: Refers to the preference to organise, plan tasks, rigorously apply methods or rules, and control quality or structure.

(C2) Interest in Data and Numbers: Refers to the interest that an individual has to reason with numbers, analyse statistical data and informatics, as well as understand the laws of mathematics. Personality - Meticulous, self-controlled, careful and stable.

Values - Accuracy, stability and efficiency.

Potential aptitudes - Organisation, data analysis, attention to detail and ability to work with numbers.

Learning style - Learn by following rules, instructions and established procedures.

Environment - Comfortable in structured and hierarchical environments where rules and procedures are clearly defined.


2.2.4.	Combined Profiles
To date, most RIASEC tools derive individuals’ occupational interest profiles by identifying their two or three main interests out of the 6 RIASEC factors, and combining them to explain which field or occupation is likely to interest the person. Holland himself noted the importance of using factor
 
combinations, stating in his Self Directed Search tool (1979), which uses a three factor combination, that it is vital for the user to consider all the possible variations of the three factor combination. This suggests that an individual’s occupational interests profile should not be inferred from their level of interest on one of the RIASEC factors alone.
In support of using the combination of several RIASEC factors to derive an individual's occupational interests profile, research has found that there is a large amount of overlap between the six RIASEC factors and personality. For example, Barrick et al (2003) and Larson, Rottinghaus and Borgen (2002) found a significant relationship between extraversion and both the Enterprising and Social factors of Holland’s model. This suggests that, due to the complexity of personality, individuals are likely to be interested in multiple RIASEC factors.
In light of this, VOCATION reports derive an individual’s occupational interests profile from the combination of their two highest interests (calculated at an equal level of significance) and their third highest interest (calculated at a slightly lower level of significance than the first two), and in turn present a list of possible appropriate trades. Using a combination of the three main factors allows for a more accurate and precise measurement of the occupations that individuals are likely to be interested in. Furthermore, keeping the third highest interest at a slightly lower level of significance allows for a more realistic view of the individual’s occupational profiles, and leaves room for individuals to focus on their two main interests alone, in case they do not identify well with their third highest interest.
2.2.5.	Ipsative Response Format
Response options for the items in VOCATION are written in binary forced-choice format (FC). FC format items ask that respondents make comparative judgements by endorsing statements as ‘most like me’ or ‘least like me’, and have been shown to overcome many of the limitations associated with Likert-scale items (Brown & Maydeu-Olivares, 2011a; Chan, 2003; Saville & Willson, 1991; ten Holt, van Duijn, & Boomsma, 2010). The advantages of using FC items include:
•	Removal of response biases, such as the tendency to agree with all items (acquiescence bias)
•	Reduced impression management and faking
•	More powerful intra-person comparisons
Despite the advantages of using forced choice items in questionnaires, they have been underutilised due to the nature of the data such questionnaires produce. These data are ‘ipsative’, meaning that the sum of individuals’ scores remains constant. Historically, Classical Test Theory (CTT) has been considered inadequate for the scoring and validation of ipsative data (Cornwell & Dunlap, 1994; Loo, 1999; Meade, 2004), because:
•	The relative nature of scores means that comparisons between individuals can be difficult.
•	The linear dependency of scores distorts the construct validity of the scales.
•	The lack of variance distorts correlations with external criteria.
•	Assumptions required for traditional reliability estimates are violated.
 
2.2.6.	Thurstonian Item Response Theory (IRT) and Ipsative Responses
Contemporary analysis techniques, developed by Brown and Maydeu-Olivares (2011, 2013), have allowed for the reliable use of FC items in questionnaire development. Using a combination of Item Response Theory (IRT) methodologies and Thurstonian comparison models (Thursto ne, 1927; 1931), Brown & Maydeu-Olivares showed that the previous issues in the interpretation of ipsative data were able to be overcome, for example:

•	‘Absolute’ values could be obtained, allowing for meaningful comparisons between individuals.
•	Distortions that lead to issues in validity can be corrected.
Despite their potential benefits, Thurstonian models were not previously used in the analysis of ipsative data for the following reasons (Brown, 2010):

•	There was no efficient estimation method available.
•	Computer technology was not powerful enough to facilitate analysis.
•	Previous statistical programmes did not facilitate the calculation of scores because the variance of
error for each question is 0.
For VOCATION, Thurstonian IRT was used to revise, rewrite and validate both items and factors. Figure 2 displays the model of Thurstonian IRT used to analyse items and factors on the questionnaire.



Figure 2. Modelof Thurstonian IRT analyses utilised in the development of VOCATION.


2.2.6.a.	Item Facility
Item facility refers to an item’s ability to differentiate between respondents and identify floor or ceiling effects. An item has floor or ceiling effects when the probability of endorsing a response option is much lower or greater than the probability of endorsing the alternate option. As items for VOCATION were binary, item facility was examined from the proportion of responses for each option. The more equivalent the proportion the more balanced the item. For VOCATION items with floor or ceiling effects were discarded or modified. The facility of changed or new items was then examined with subsequent pretests.
2.2.6.b.	Item Difficulty and Discrimination
Here, an ‘item’ refers to the possible answer(s) for each question on VOCATION. Item difficulty is the
 
first parameter of an IRT model. It is defined as the location on the ability continuum, where the probability of endorsing the item is equal to 50% of the participants. In an interest based assessment, the difficulty parameter reflects the level of interest needed for the participant to have a 50% chance of choosing the item. An item is said to be ‘easy’’ when the probability that 50% of individuals who endorsed it only have a low level on the interest measured by the item. (e.g. an “easy” item measuring Enterprising might be chosen by 50% of participants having only a low level of trait for Enterprising). On the contrary, an item is said to be “difficult”, when the probability that 50% of individuals who endorsed it have a high level of trait (e.g. a “difficult” item measuring Realistic might reach the probability of being chosen by 50% of the participants only if they have a high level of trait for Realistic).
2.2.7.	Pilot Study
This section is only about the final pilot study with the data used (sample) for the stats part of manual).
The final questionnaire was piloted with 309 English-speaking professionals from the United Kingdom. Composite reliabilities for each factor were calculated from generated item factor loadings. Factors were flagged if reliabilities were less than 0.55. Factors that did not converge were inspected for poorly-performing items which were then reviewed, modified or changed and then subjected to another round of pretest. The average scale composite reliability for the Pilot study was 0.83.
 
3.	Scoring
A raw score is the total number of responses endorsed by a candidate for each factor measured. In the context of career-interest assessment, raw scores mean little as they do not provide effective or appropriate comparisons between candidates across a specific interest. Norms enable HR
practitioners, managers and recruiters to evaluate an individual’s score relative to the score of other
delegates who completed the same assessment.

3.1.	The relationship between Raw Scores and Norms
The best norm group is one that is most representative of the characteristics of the individuals taking a test. Applied assessment assumes personality traits, interests, motivations, values, attitudes and perceptions are distributed along a normal probability or bell-shaped curve. Therefore, norm groups effectively identify individual scores across this distribution. In any given population, levels of a target attribute may be found. Therefore, for a given attribute or trait, the normal curve represents the norm or average performance of a population and the scores that are above and below the average for that population (Kaplan & Saccuzzo, 2008). For norms to be meaningful, the sample used must reflect the intended population of test takers. If a test is developed for engineers, the norm sample must consist of engineers in the population. Similarly if an assessment was developed for nurses, it is not appropriate for use in the selection of chemists. Norm groups can range in size from a few hundred to a hundred thousand. However the larger the norm group sample size the closer the approximation to a normal population distribution. Figure 3 shows the normal bell curve.



Figure 3. Normal Bell-shaped curve showing the distribution of percentages, standard deviations and standard ten (Sten).

Key to the creation of norms is the conversion of raw scores into derived scores. This process provides a system of common metrics that readily facilitate the interpretation of test scores. For VOCATION, the forms of score standardization used were: 1) Mean; 2) Median; 3) Standard deviation; 4) Standard score; 5) Standard ten (sten); and 6) Standard error of measurement. Table 4 describes the various types of derived scores used in the validation of VOCATION.
 
Table 4. Types of derived scores used to standardize VOCATION.

	Derived Score	Symbol	Definition and Characteristics
 	 
1	 
Mean	 
µ	 
The average score of all participants who complete an assessment. The mean is calculated by dividing the sum of all scores by the number of participants. The formula for calculating the Mean is:

μ = ( Σ Xi ) / N,

where ΣX is the sum of all the population observations, N is the number of population observations, Σx is the sum of all the sample observations, and n is the number of sample observations.
 	 
2	 
Median	 
~
𝓍	 
The median is the score that separates the highest scores from the lowest. The median can be found by arranging all scores in order from lowest to highest and selecting the score in the middle. Like the mean, median is a measure of the general tendency of a group of scores.
 	 
3	 
Standard Deviation	 
σ	 
A measure of the amount of variation across scores for candidates who take an assessment. A small standard deviation indicates scores are close to the mean while a large value tells us there is much variability across individuals. The formula for calculating the Standard Deviation is:

σ = sqrt [ Σ ( Xi - μ )2 / N ],

where μ is the population mean, Xi is the ith element from the population, and N is the number of elements in the population.
 	 
4	 
Standard Score	 
Z	 
Estimates whether an individual’s score is above average, average or below average compared to peers. Scored on a scale from -4 to 4 with zero in the middle representing average. Positive scores are above average while negative scores are below average. Z scores are calculated using the formula:

z = (x - μx) / σx,
 

			
where X is a normal random variable, μ is the
mean of X, and σ is the standard deviation of X.
 	 
5	 
Standard Ten	 
Sten	 
A standard score with scores ranging from 1 – 10. Here the mean is 5.5 and the standard deviation equals 2. The formula for calculating Sten scores is:

Sten = (𝓍 − 𝓍/𝓍 )2+5.5,

where X is the candidate’s raw score, µ is the mean of raw scores for all candidates, σ is the standard deviation of raw scores on the standardisation sample, multiplied by 2 + 5.5.

Sten scores are rounded to the nearest whole number with values less than 1 or greater than 10 rounded up or down respectively.
 	 
6	 
Standard Error of Measurement	 
SEM	 
SEM is related to test reliability and provides an indication of the dispersion of measurement errors associated with test scores from a group of candidates. It helps us to identify level of errors when estimating candidate’s true scores from their observed scores. The smaller the SEM, the more accurate are the assessments that are being made.
The formula for calculating SEM is:

𝓍𝓍𝓍 = 𝓍 × √(1−𝓍𝓍𝓍𝓍𝓍𝓍𝓍𝓍𝓍𝓍𝓍).

3.2.	Transforming Raw Scores to Standardised Scores
For the 60 items in VOCATION, the minimum and maximum overall score for each attribute is 0 and
10. The conversion of raw scores to standardised scores is achieved using a four-step process, as below.

Step 1 Ipsative scores are calculated for each scale by summing all forced-choice responses for each attribute. For each response, 1 is assigned to the response that is endorsed and 0 for the response that is not endorsed.
Step 2  Scores are repositioned on a scale from 0 to 10 by using the following formula:
 
 


Whereby max refers to the maximum possible score of each scale and min refers to the minimum possible score of each scale.



Step 3 To enable the comparisons between individuals, scores for each scale are standardised using the mean and standard deviation across respondents. This process produces a z score using the formula:

z = (score – (μ/𝓍)

Step 4	Standardised scores are then transformed into sten scores from 1 to 10 using the formula:

sten = z * 2 + 5.5

3.3.	Standard Error of Measurement
No test score is perfect. Therefore, we need an estimate of the possible amount of error present within a score. The amount of error associated with a score is known as the standard error of
measurement (SEM). SEMhelps us to understand what is a candidate’s true score and the extent to which it differs from the obtained score (Harvill, 1991). In an ideal world, the SEM would be calculated by having an individual take an assessment on many occasions over a long period of time. The extent to which there was variation across each score obtained by the candidate would be classified as the standard error of measurement.
However, as it is not feasible to assess candidates multiple times, the SEM must be calculated using a different approach.

The SEM is an estimate that is added to or subtracted from a candidate’s score in order to establish a level of confidence about a score. From this confidence band, we are able to determine the extent to which a score may vary. Standard error of measurement is related to reliability. Therefore as reliability increases, SEMdecreases. For a series of scores, approximately 68% of the time, an observed score will lie within +1.0 and -1.0 SEM of the true score. On 95% of occasions, the observed score will be between +1.96 and -1.96 SEM of the true score. Whilst 99% of the time, the observed score will lie within +2.58 and -2.58 SEM of the true score. The 95% confidence band of a score is calculated using the formula:
Confidence band =score ± 1.96 x SEM
Therefore for VOCATION, a factor with a score of 5 and SEM of 0.2, has a confidence band of 4.608 -
5.392. This means we can be 95% confident that an individual’s true score lies in this range.
 
3.4.	Choosing an appropriate Norm Group
Norms give meaning to an individual’s score. The interpretation of the same score may therefore differ depending on whether a score was obtained by a graduate, administrator or seni or manager. For accurate interpretation, it is necessary to compare an individual’s score with a group that is most similar to him/her. To choose the appropriate norm, first identify the country of the candidate. Then from that country, identify the age, gender, educational level, number of years work experience, social professional status, industry or occupational group most appropriate for the candidate.
Alternatively, it is possible to create your own internal norms, based on the candidates that have already been assessed.

3.5.	Creating In-house Norms
It is possible to generate internal organisational norms if there is a relevant sample of 50 or more individuals. In-house norms ensure that comparisons between individuals and the reference group reflect the target role, tasks and work environment as closely as possible. In-house norms are particularly valuable in situations where standard norms are not available, roles are new, the organisation has a unique client-base or business function and in cases where the applicant pool for a role is significantly higher or lower than expected.

To create in-house norms, first calculate the mean and standard deviation for each factor on
VOCATION and score the results based on the step by step guide outlined in Chap ter 3.2. Organisations will then be able to compare individual applicants or employees to a local normal group.
3.6.	Link between Scores and Report Comments
Comments on VOCATION reports reflect the scoring process. An individual receives a score of 0 to 10 (as demonstrated in Chapter 3.2.). The comments provide an extensive interpretation of all facets within the six RIASEC factors of the test. They are chosen from six levels according to the score of the candidate, and detail individual preferences and orientation towards the attributes according to the individual’s score. Higher scores are accompanied by stronger descriptors, weaker scores are accompanied by weaker descriptors, and so on. For example, a candidate with a low score on a factor may be described as ‘not appearing to have an interest’ in the relevant factor. A high score on the other hand may describe an individual as having ‘a strong interest’ in the relevant factor. Scores should be interpreted in relative terms; the order of the targeted profiles is more important than their absolute score.
Combined profiles on VOCATION reports also reflect the scoring process. These profiles represent a combination of the three highest factor scores that the individual has endorsed. They are calculated on the basis that the scores on the first two interests will be of highest, equal significance, whereas the score on third interest will be of slightly lower significance.
 
4.	Sample Descriptives
The sample comprised of 309 professionals from the UK, (142 men; 167 women) ranging in age from 18 to 72 years (µ=43.19; σ=13.92). All the participants represented a range of occupational groups, representing technical, administrative, service, managerial and non-managerial roles, and a range of industries including banking and professional services, marketing and the service sector, manufacturing and product development. Data was collected from the professional research and sampling firm named CINT, and each respondent received compensation for their participation. To ensure the quality of data obtained, participants whose answers were deemed unreliable or who completed the questionnaire in less than 8 minutes were excluded from analyses. 5.	Norms
Norms help us to compare an individual’s score on a test with the scores of others who completed the same assessment, usually under similar conditions. These individuals who took the test at the same time are representative of the population for which an assessment is intended, and are therefore referred to as the norm group. The closer the match between the characteristics of the sample and the target population, the more accurate the distribution will be as a ranking guide. With norms, test developers and psychologists are able to establish comparisons between individuals and make more objective decisions. Norm groups enable us to attribute meaning to test scores and quantify an individual’s performance, or level of an attribute, interest or trait relative to his/her peers.
Tables 8 to 13 detail the mean, standard deviation, minimum, and maximum scores for the UK sample on factors of VOCATION, for the whole sample on gender, and is organised into categories based on age and gender.

6.	Reliability
Reliability refers to the extent to which an assessment is free from error and the degree to which items on a scale are measuring the same construct. For a measure to be reliable, the same results must be consistently generated over time. Reliability is important for an assessment as it enables us to have confidence that scores on a test are really typical of an individual’s behaviour, interest, attitude or aptitude. A primary concern when developing and using tests, is the extent to which random measurement errors influence test performance. If a test is unreliable it is impossible for it to be valid, as accuracy of results (validity) depends on the consistency with which the same results are produced. High reliability of an assessment helps to guard against biases that may be caused from errors in human judgment during the recruitment and selection process.

6.1.	Internal Reliability
Internal reliability is a measure of the degree to which items on a scale are internally consistent and measure the same global construct or dimension of a construct. Composite reliability is a robust technique used to assess the internal consistency of items on a scale. It is mathematically defined as the amount of scale score variance accounted for by all underlying factors. Cronbach alpha, while a popular way of determining a measure’s internal reliability, has a major weakness in that it assumes all items on a scale are equally important and therefore weighs all items similarly. As a consequence, if there is an increase in the number of items, reliability would artificially increase even though loadings may be low and not significant.

Therefore, composite reliability is a recommended alternative as it makes use of both item loadings and measurement error (Dunn, Baguley, & Brunsden, 2014; Raykov, 1997; Schmitt, 1996). Internal reliability values vary from 0 to 1, with higher values more desirable. As a rule of thumb, a reliability of .70 or higher is recommended (Nunnally, 1978), with values greater than .9 (excellent); .7 to .9 (good); .6 to .7 (acceptable); .5 to .6 (poor) and values less than .5 (unacceptable). Composite reliability should be ≥ .7.

The formula for Composite Reliability (CR) is:


The composite reliability for VOCATION was computed on the standardised British sample (N=309). Table 14 describes the internal reliability of VOCATION. All the factors showed a composite reliability of above .7 with R and S being .90 and above. On top of this, VOCATION has an overall composite reliability of .83, indicating that the scale has a very good internal consistency and is highly reliable.

6.2.	Test-Retest

Test-retest reliability involves administering a test twice to the same respondents under similar conditions and comparing the results for each individual in order to determine the stability of the test scores over time. The more similar the scores on the first administration (Time 1) are to the scores on the second administration (Time 2), the greater the reliability. The Pearson Product Moment Correlation Coefficient gives us the correlation between the two administrations and ranges between 0 and 1. The closer the correlation is to 1, the greater the reliability. The formula for the Pearson Product Moment Correlation is:

where [x] and [y] are the two samples’ means and X and Y are the values of each of the two
variables.

For VOCATION, test-retest reliability was calculated on the UK sample (n=88) at two points in time, between 3-6 weeks apart. Participants ranged in age from 21 to 72 years (μ = 48.27, σ =12.22). Test- retest reliabilitywas computed from individual scores for all factors and was calculated using the Pearson Product Moment Correlation Coefficient. The test-retest correlations for VOCATIONshow excellent results, with the lowest correlation being at .74 and the average being .81. Therefore it can be concluded that VOCATION is a highly reliable measure overtime as a person taking the test at two different points in time, will consistently get the same results.
 
7.	Validity
Validity refers to the extent to which an assessment measures what it claims to measure, and that this is well-founded and accurately corresponds to actual behaviour. In psychometrics, validity relates to the degree to which practical evidence and scientific theory support the interpretations of test scores. Whilst the reliability of an assessment relates to the degree to which results are consistent over time, validity tells us the degree to which results of an assessment are accurate and enable us to confidently make predictions about a target outcome. Therefore, a test can be re liable but not valid (one can consistently get the wrong answer) but it cannot be valid without being reliable, as confidence in the accuracy of results depends on the consistency with which we obtain the same results. Established validity protects practitioners from faulty, costly, unethical and legally risky decisions.

Validity is derived from the Latin word ‘validus’, meaning strong. According to the Standards for Educational and Psychological Testing (Standards; APA, AERA, & NCME, 2014), validation is a process of providing evidence for the scientific basis of interpreting assessment scores. The process of validation therefore depends on evidence supporting: a) test content, b) response processes, c) internal structure, d) relations to other variables, and e) consequences of testing. VOCATION was validated following a thorough multi-stage process that ensured items and factors were accurate, representative, consistent, clear, fair and culturally free.
Face validity is a measure of whether an assessment looks like it is measuring what it claims to be measuring, by those who take it (Nevo, 1985). This is an important aspect to take into account in order to obtain the candidate’s co-operation and commitment. Face validity and attitudes towards tests influence candidates’ motivation, concentration and test-taking anxiety (McCarthy & Goffin, 2003). As a result, the question of ‘Does VOCATION look, to potential candidates, like it measures occupational and vocational preferences?’ was addressed during the creation of the assessment, by way of ensuring that aspects such as the situational questions were not misleading or confusing to the user. The following sections will present, in more detail, further aspects of validity that were taken into account for VOCATION.

7.1.	Concurrent Validity
Concurrent validity measures whether a method is concurrent with the results of another similar tried and tested method. In this case, for concurrent validity to be established for VOCATION, the components of the assessment must correlate highly with corresponding components on other established interest-based questionnaires. Concurrent validity also requires that the two tests are taken at the same time or with little time between them, so that the correlation is pure and unaltered by changes over time. Tables 16 to 18 show the results detailing the concurrent validityfor VOCATION.
 
7.1.1.	The ORVIS IPIP
The International Personality Item Pool (IPIP) is a collection of 2413 internationally recognised personality items. The project was initiated in 1996 and is available in over 25 languages.
The IPIP questionnaire for this study was extracted from the IPIP Website (Goldberg, 1999)

Out of the IPIP scales, The Oregon Vocational Interest Scales (ORVIS) was chosen to be the most adequate scale for concurrent validity as it measures the same constructs as Holland’s RIASEC (Pozzebon, 2010). For example, E1 correlated highly with creativity, this is likely due to the fact that E1 measures the enterprising mindset, to be innovative and forward thinking, which requires a great deal of creative thought.
 
7.2.	Construct Validity

Construct validity measures the degree to which a test measures what it claims to measure. It refers to the appropriateness of inferences made on the basis of observation or measurement. Construct validity is essential to the overall validity (perceived and actual) of a scale and is particularly valuable when there is much subjectivity to concepts. When a construct is not clearly or thoroughly defined, we are unable to make direct inferences between the construct and other behaviours (Cronbach & Meehl, 1955). Often there is no accepted unit of measurement for constructs; even fairly well known concepts such as intelligence are open to debate. Therefore, the construct validity of a scale may be threatened if the construct is: a) defined as too broad or too narrow, b) mislabelled as a specific dimension of or component within the construct, c) confounded by other related constructs that mask the effects of the main construct, or d) the construct was developed with insufficient research. Therefore, in order to develop a scale that measures occupational and vocational preferences, the construct must reflect known theories that define interests, identify the components that it is comprised of and pinpoints positive and negative attributes associated with each dimension.
For interest-based assessments, construct validity is important as it: a) adds legitimacy and acceptability to the standardised measurement of employee interests, b) facilitates understanding of organisational implications of findings, and c) enables organisations and individuals to tailor
interventions to meet desired targets. Construct validity addresses the question ‘Does VOCATION define and classify occupational and vocational preferences in a way that represents theoretical and practical understanding?’ For VOCATION, construct validitywas established using two approaches: inter-factor correlations and confirmatory factor analysis, as detailed in the sections below.
7.2.1.	Inter-factor Correlations
The factors in VOCATION measure occupational and vocational preferences, however they all measure different facets of interests. In other words, if someone scores highly on a factor, it does not necessarily mean that they will score highly on all factors. Table 19 shows the inter-factor correlations.
