The correlations in Table 19 demonstrate the relationships between the various aspects measured by VOCATION. As can be seen in Table 19, the two factors which correlate most highly are S and E at
.66, this is consistent with Holland’s theory as these two factors are next to each other in the RIASEC hexagon, whilst A and C are opposite of each and are highly negatively correlated at - .45. However we have observed differences, for example E and I are opposite each other in the hexagon, but are seen to be correlated in our model. Perhaps this reflects the investigative nature that is required for modern day entrepreneurship, in fact some of the greatest business leaders and entrepreneurs began as technological innovators, so it’s certainly possible that the lines dividing the hexagon becoming less rigid.
7.2.2.	Confirmatory Factor Analysis
To investigate the theoretical constructs or factors that may be represented by a set of items as well as the quality of individual items, exploratory factor analysis (EFA) and confirmatory factor analysis (CFA) are usually performed. EFA and CFA are both forms of factor analysis. Factor analysis examines the factors (hypothetical constructs) of an assessment which are developed to interpret clusters of strong inter-correlations between items in a correlation matrix. Factor analysis aims to simplify a range of items into a few common variables; these can be identified by extracting and grouping items which have high correlations on a factor to reveal the common variables.
The goal of EFA is to identify and maximise the i mportance of each dimension (percentage of variance), while CFA is a hypothesis driven approach that determines the degree to which observed indicators reflect the underlying construct by specifying the number of factors expected and then classifying the value of expected relationships (Schreiber, Nora, Stage, Barlow & King, 2006). In doing so, CFA provides a numerical estimate of the degree to which the proposed factor structure is an accurate fit of the phenomenon in the real world. CFA therefore investigates the degree to which measures of a construct are consistent with established understanding and theoretical arguments for that construct.
A confirmatory factor analysis (CFA) with continuous factor indicators was conducted in order to check the convergence of the results with the model of the assessment. CFA is a form of factor analysis used to measure that a construct is consistent with its intended meaning. There are two key approaches to confirming the factor structure of a scale. A strict confirmatory approach is one in which a model is tested using structural equation modelling (SEM) goodness of fit tests to determine if the model specified by the research is consistent with the data. On the other hand, an alternative model approach allows two or more models to be tested to identify which has the best f i t. For the development of VOCATION, a strict confirmatory approach was adopted as there is only one formulated model for VOCATION.
 
CFA is used to determine how well the model fits the data, and theref ore evaluating the goodness of fit indices is crucial. Several statistical tests are used to determine how well the model fits the data in CFA and SEM. The closer fit indices meet statistical requirements, the better the model. The Chi Square (X²) statistic is considered the primary index of goodness-of-fit. However, due to proble ms associated with reliance on the X² (sensitivity to sample sizes and inappropriateness when distributional assumptions are violated), measures of relative fit are strongly recommended. The X² is a measure of absolute fit, and provides a fundamental indication of how well the proposed theory fits the data and does not rely on comparison with a baseline model (Jöreskog & Sörbom, 1993).
For the validation of VOCATION, the Root Mean Square Error of Approximation (RMSEA, Steiger & Lind, 1980), the Bentler Comparative Fit Index (CFI), and the Tucker-Lewis Index (TLI) were chosen as additional measures of fit, as they are the least likely to be affected by sample size (Fan, Thompson & Wang, 1999). Furthermore, using a variety of indices is necessary as different indices reflect different aspects of model fit (Hooper, Coughlan & Mullen, 2008). Like the X², the RMSEA is a measure of absolute fit that assesses the amount of error associated with a proposed model. The lower the RMSEA, the more robust the model; indices of .05 or below are considered to be a good fit (Hu & Bentler, 1999). This is because RMSEA is related to the residuals in the model and assesses the degree to which the proposed model is inaccurate. Both the CFI and TLI are incremental or relative fit indices that assess overall improvement of a proposed model compared with an independence model where observed variables are uncorrelated (Hooper et al., 2008; Holtzman & Vezzu, 2011). While higher values of the CFI and TLI (.9 and above) indicate an excellent fit, it should be noted that a CFI or TLI of .90 or higher may not necessarily correspond to a RMSEA of .05 or less (Hu & Bentler, 1999).
Two CFA analyses were conducted on the 6 factor and 12 factor models. Tables 20 and 21 show the goodness of fit indices and model results.
Thurstonian IRT
“Item response theory (IRT) refers to a set of mathematical models that describe, in probabilistic terms, the relationship between a person’s response to a survey question and his or her level of the ‘latent variable’ being measured by the scale” (Hays & Fayers, 2005, p.55). In ipsative tests, a respondent must make a comparative judgement between two or more items to give an answer.
Conversely, in normative tests, judgement is measured in absolute terms for each evaluated
attribute and does not compare preferences between different attributes. Classical test scoring does not reflect this psychological process of comparison, but rather ignores the fact that respondents indicate a preference not an absolute judgement. Thurstone’s law of comparative judgements (Thurstone, 1927; 1931) generates results based on the comparative judgements of individuals. In order to analyse the data using Thurstonian IRT modelling, raw data was converted into binary outcomes following the procedure described by Brown and Maydeu-Olivares (2011) for Item response modelling of paired comparisons and ranking data. For VOCATION, the 60 questions consisted of blocks of 2 items (response options). The observed responses to the paired comparisons were coded using the formula below, where l indicates the pair {a, b};
 
 


The Thurstonian IRT model was fitted to the binary outcomes of paired comparisons with a structure of both 6 and 12 latent factors. In total, 60 binary outcomes were generated from 60 blocks of two items. Mplus version 7.1 (Muthén & Muthén, 2014) was used to estimate item parameters from tetrachoric correlations using the unweighted least squares (ulsmv) algorithm.

Table 20. Confirmatory factor analysis using Thurstonian IRT for the 6 factor model.

N	X2	df	X2/df	p	RMSEA	P <=.05	CFI	TLI

Tables 20 and 21 show the model fit results for the two models. Due to the sample size, the more robust estimators of RMSEA and CFI/TLI were used to assess model fit. From these indicators, it can be seen that both models have a good fit and the 12 factor model fits better than the 6. This confirms that VOCATION is a valid model. The fact that the 12 factor fits better than the 6 also gives more credence to the idea that each factor in the RIASEC model can be split into two facets.

7.2.3.	Consequential Validity
Consequential validity emerged from ethical concerns related to societal ramifications of assessments. It can be described as a type of validity evidence that concerns the intended and unintended, positive as well as negative, consequences related to the interpretation and use of assessments (Messick, 1995). Messick (1980, p. 1012) argued that “not only should tests be evaluated in terms of their measurement properties but that testing applications should be
evaluated in terms of their potential social consequences”. Consequential validity is considered to be an important component of the evaluation of validity, rather than an isolated conceptualisation of it (Hubley & Zumbo, 2011). Given the financial costs associated with hiring a candidate, as well as the employment legislation governing diversity and inclusion of specific demographic groups, consequential validityis important for VOCATION. Details of how consequential validity was measured are displayed below, in Chapter 8.
 
8.	VOCATION and Equal Opportunities
Organisations are increasingly under pressure to ensure that tools used to inform employee recruitment, selection, development, promotion and performance measurement decisions are fair, objective and accurate. This pressure stems from a progressively knowledgeable applicant pool, a more sophisticated labour force and stringent local and international employment legislation and testing standards. Equal opportunities therefore represent a critical consideration for any modern day work assessment, and an assessment is only considered to be truly valid when it is appropriate for use with different groups. VOCATION is a fair and accurate measure of occupational and vocational preferences, and HR practitioners and psychologists can be confident of its diverse application.
Within the context of assessment, measures used must not result in adverse impact when applied to different groups. Adverse impact occurs when there is a significant difference in the rate of selection and employment decisions for one protected group over another. This is particularly important for organisations as there is a risk of selecting the wrong candidate for a job, as well as legal and financial costs associated with discrimination claims. Adverse impact is classified as discrimination when a candidate believes (and validity evidence fails to show otherwise) that not being offered a role was based on membership to certain demographics. For most countries across Europe, North America and Australia, fairness in the selection process is subject to legislation. Specifically for the USA, the Employee Testing and other Procedures Act 1971 (US Department of Labor) protects candidates against unfair assessment procedures, whilst fair employment assessment in the UK is protected by the following:

●	Race Relations Act (1976)
●	Sex Discrimination Act (1975;1986)
●	Disability Act (1998)
●	Employment Equality Regulation (2003)
○	Sexual Orientation
○	Religion and Belief
●	Age Discrimination (2006)


8.1.	Measurement Equivalence

Measurement equivalence (ME) refers to the consistency of measurement across some specified group demarcation such as nationality, ethnicity and gender. The focus of ME is to e nsure that individuals from different groups with the same standing on an attribute have the same set of observations on an assessment (Drasgow, 1987; McLeary & Cruise, 2012). Understanding ME is important in assessments in order to establish validity, confidence and equivalence across groups. Equivalence of measurement is obtained when candidates from different cultural backgrounds, age groups, ethnicities, genders and occupational groups with the same level on a trait, have an equal
 
chance of being selected for a role that requires the desired level on this trait. ME helps to determine the extent to which respondents are interpreting items similarly, and identified differences across groups reflect actual differences in the trait being measured (Drasgow, 1984). One approach to assessing measurement equivalence is differential item functioning analysis.



8.1.1.	Differential Item Functioning (Dif)

An item functions differently, or displays Dif, if respondents from different groups have different probabilities of endorsing the item, despite having the same level of the attribute or trait. When a different probability of endorsing an item remains constant across different levels of the attribute or trait, the Dif is said to be uniform. Here, the item of interest consistently gives one group an advantage across all levels of the attribute or trait. Conversely, when a different probability of endorsing an item varies along the level of the attribute or trait, the Dif is characterised as non- uniform (Clauser & Mazor, 1998; Hanson, 1998). Rather than a consistent advantage being given to the reference group across the ability continuum, an advantage to one group over another exists at different levels of the trait. For instance, an item may give the reference group an advantage at the higher end of the continuum, while a disadvantage at the lower end (see Figure 5). Item Response Theory (IRT) facilities Dif analysis by examining the probability of endorsing items through the item characteristic curve. Here, the logistic regression approach is used, whereby separate analyses are run for each item and maximum likelihood estimation used to calculate the probability of endorsing an item. Figures 4 and 5 show an example of item characteristic curves for uniform and non-uniform Dif.
 
 
Figure 4. Item characteristic curve with uniform Dif.









Figure 5. Item characteristic curve with non-uniform DIF.

The DIF analysis provides information on whether an item is perceived in a similar way across gender, age and culture. If there is no DIF, it means that there is no bias that would favour one group over another. For example, two individuals from different countries with a similar absolute interest
 
in the Artistic factor will have the same probability of endorsing the items measuring Artistic. However, true differences between groups may exist (e.g. some cultures are more interested in Artistic, others less). Such true differences are reflected in the norms (see Chapter 4).

8.1.1.a.	DIF Analysis: Gender
DIF analysis was performed across gender in order to ensure that there was not a bias towards either males or females, and therefore that VOCATION is a fair test for both genders. For this analysis, the UK norm sample was divided according to gender; 167 female and 142 male.

8.1.1.c.	T-tests and ANOVAs
The DIF analysis demonstrates that VOCATION has high consequential validity, and although several items were flagged with DIF, there was a negligible impact on the overall assessment, meaning that the assessment does not bias towards gender or age. Therefore, any significant differences found between genders or age groups can be attributed to real differences between the groups and not biases of the assessment. To further explore the differences between the groups, t-tests and ANOVAS were performed between genders and age groups (see Tables 24 to 29).

The ANOVA for the RIASEC factors by age groups found significant differences for R, A, S and E. Post hoc analysis was conducted using pairwise t-tests (Bonferroni correction) in order to see where these differences occurred.

The R, S and E post hoc t-tests found there were no significant differences between the six age groups. However, it was found that for factor A, the age group 18 to 25 (M=6.22) was significantly higher than the age group 50 to 56 (M=4.7 , p=.009), with the age group Over 57 (M= 4.91, p=.053)
 
being close to significant. This indicates that younger people may be more interest in artistic endeavours. This may be because the artistic working field is becoming more and more popular, and accessible, in terms of work. Creative careers such as design and illustration are more desirable than ever at present, with companies realising the importance of having such elements in their work. As a result, more and more young people are surrounded by these topics and as the appeal of working in such fields increases, perhaps have more and more interest in the field.

For an in-depth look at the differences between age groups for the RIASEC factors, an ANOVA was conducted on the facets. The results are shown in Table 26 above. The differences found in A were shown to exist in only A2 and not A1, the differences in S were also found to only be in S2 and not in S1. While the differences in E was found to be in E1 and E2, this further indicates that the RIASEC factors should be broken down into facets to be properly interpreted.
The post hoc t-tests for A2 showed that once again the differences existed between the 18 to 25 group (M=6.50) and the 50 to 56 group (M=4.91, p=.013) and Over 57 group (M=5.03, p=.036). This difference could be due to the fact that A2 is more centered around creativity and thinking outside the box, both of which are traits more often seen in the younger generations, who have a fresh outlook on the world. Perhaps the reason why this difference is not also seen in A1 is that A1 is aesthetic appreciation, something which is far less likely to change with age.
 
The post hoc t-tests for E1 showed that the 18 to 25 group (M=4.84) and 26 to 33 group (M=4.81) were both significantly higher than the 42 to 49 group (M=3.71, p=.033 and p=.035) This may be because at a younger age taking risks is necessary in order to advance their careers as there is more to gain and less to lose. Whereas at 42 to 49 people are steady in their careers and ne ed stability to settle down for family reasons.
The post hoc t-tests for E2 indicated that the Over 57 group (M=5.14) was significantly higher than the 42 to 49 group (M=3.92, p=.014). This may be due to the older generation feeling that they have greater wisdom to share and direct the younger generation, while the 42 to 49 group will be as above in stable careers, most likely with higher managers.

The t-tests found significant differences between males and females on the factors of R, I, A, and S. Males were found to be significantly more interested in R (male=5.3 vs female=4.04) (t(t307)=-5.45, p=.001), across both R1 (male=5.32 vs female=4.45) (t(t307)=-5.45, p=.001) and R2 (male=5.29 vs female=3.62) (t(t307)=-6.4, p=.001). This could be due to the idea that men are generally expected to be the ones taking manual labour jobs such as mechanics or building, etc.
Males were also found to be significantly more interested in I (male=5.94 vs female=5.40) ( t(t307)=- 2.78, p=.01), however this difference can only be seen in I2 (male=6.07 vs female=5.22) (t(t307)=- 3.54, p=.001). This may be because I2 is focused on technology and research, a field of occupational interests which has been predominantly male-focused for many years, meaning that perhaps cognitive biases exist which cause females to be discouraged by such areas. This could be an interesting area of future research, as societal roles are becoming less entrenched, more schemes aimed at encouraging women into STEMfields are being implemented. We could expect these differences to reduce in younger generations and it may be possible to measure the effect of these schemes using occupational interest inventories.
Females were found to be significantly more A (male=4.79 vs female=5.59) (t(t307)=3.08, p=.001), for both A1 (male=4.55 vs female=5.43) (t(t307)=3.08, p=.001) and A2 (male=5.04 vs female=5.76) (t(t307)=2.58, p=.01). The A1 difference may be due to a tendency for women to be more in touch with their emotions and are therefore, perhaps more willing to express their emotions through artistic endeavours. The A2 difference could be down to an inclination for women to be more intuitive and therefore more interested in roles which require design and imagination.
Females were also found to be significantly more S (male=5.18 vs female=4.39) ( t(t307)=-3.22, p=.001), however this difference was only found for S1 (male=4.84 vs female=6.09) (t(t307)=4.66, p=.001). This difference may exist as a result of women being expected by society to hold more social roles (nurses, teachers, customer service, etc.) which require more dedication to others.
 
9.	Additional Information and Considerations


VOCATION was developed for use in a range of organisational settings. It is important that the appropriate norms, scores and interpretations are used in order to guide HR decisions for recruitment and selection, career development, coaching and performance management. Accurate interpretation of psychometric tests helps both administrators and candidates to: a) put the assessment into context; b) effectivelycompare candidates across target capabilities; c) accurately identify areas for development; d) maximise fit between job requirements and candidate abilities and interests; and e) promote the organisation as a fair and objective place to work. The current chapter gives guidelines for HR practitioners to understand and successfully interpret candidate scores for VOCATION for different organisational objectives.
Report generation software for VOCATION provides graphs and personalised comments for all the 6 factors and 12 facets that relate to the candidate’s score. The report should only be interpreted by professionals who have completed Central Test’s accredited training course and are familiar with this manual. Training in and knowledge of the assessment process are vital as scores should be interpreted in light of information regarding reliability, validity, standard error of measurement, and any accommodations to the assessment or assessment session that were made. Scores should be presented within the context of the assessment and in a clear, meaningful and appropriate way for the target audience.


9.1.	Interpreting Scores
It is important to note that all assessments by Central Test have been developed for HR purposes only. Therefore, no clinical conclusions can be drawn from the results – not even from extreme scores. There are six different levels of comments in total. Levels are determined by a candidate’s score. Table 30 provides an example of descriptors used to describe different levels of a factor.
Extreme scores are defined as the lowest and highest possible values on a factor. For VOCATION, the minimum score is set at 0 and the maximum score is set at 10. Given the ipsative format of the assessment, it is impossible to obtain extreme scores on all factors.
Each factor consists of two facets. High scores on a factor indicate that there is a high probability that the person who is being evaluated can be characterised as having an interest in that factor. Middle scores (i.e. 4-7) indicate a high congruence with the norm group and may be less of a preference for the individual. However, all comments should be viewed as hypotheses that need to be confirmed with other sources of data, such as interviews or further assessments, CV content and references.

9.2.	Organisational Need: Career selection, Recruitment, and Development
Through the identification of 21st century work interests, VOCATION is an effective tool for both the employer, in the selection of job candidates on the basis of the occupational areas that suit a candidate’s vocational interests, and the job candidate, in the successful selection of an appropriate career navigation.
Choosing an appropriate career path is a difficult task, and with the overwhelming number of working fields that exist in the modern-day occupational world, it can be confusing to know which to take. VOCATION helps to pinpoint a candidate’s most appropriate job as a result of their recreational interests. By choosing a career that will make use of their interests, an individual can find greater job satisfaction.
This then leads to the importance of interest assessment in recruitment. In order to successfully hire a candidate that will feel satisfied with their job and as a result want to remain with the company, job satisfaction of the employee becomes significant to the recruiter. By using VOCATION, an employer will be able to infer how appropriate a job may be for a candidate based on an objective assessment of their interests. If the endorsed interests of said candidate do not align with those of the job role in question, there may not be a good match between the two.
Recruitment and selection is an important organisational function which can be very time consuming and expensive. Furthermore, bad recruitment decisions have additional effects on organisational productivity, capability and sustainability. Whilst someone in financial and legal administration for example, may feel more comfortable if they have a preference for tasks that involve following strict rules, someone working in marketing may be better suited to have an interest in tasks that are more imaginative and creative. It is recommended that prior to administering VOCATION, recruiters have a detailed job description and person specifications based on a job analysis.
VOCATION helps recruiters to customise job profiles in their client account by specifying desired scores for each factor. The assessment allows the recruiter to easily compare the target profile with a candidate’s profile.
Furthermore, VOCATION can be used in line with performance evaluations, which are a key component of employee development and success. Performance reviews should be fair and
 
balanced assessments of an employee’s achievements and capabilities, and should give the opportunity to discuss expectations and accomplishments. VOCATION supports HR practitioners with performance reviews that can help to discuss job performance in relation to occupational and vocational interests; if an individual is not performing as they could be at work, it may be down to a lack of interest in the particular area that they are working on. Identifying this can help the recruiter to identify whether or not the employee should be transferred to another area of the company, or to a different aspect of a project.
Whilst VOCATION is effective in identifying areas for development, it should not be used to directly track an employee’s performance progress. We therefore recommend the use of objective performance assessments or other evaluations in conjunction with VOCATION.






User Manual – Big Five Profile © Central Test 2016	p. 8
 
B.	Some external criteria like the number of years of managerial experience, overall satisfaction with current occupation, accident rate, turnover of the department, promotions/letter of appreciation received in the last 3 years, etc. were identified and the relationship between factor scores and these criterion scores were examined using correlation analysis in studies conducted by the in-house research and innovation department.


	Concurrent validity:

Concurrent validity is the extent to which factors of a test display similarity with similar factors assessed by other contemporary tests. This is normally examined by performing a simple correlation analysis between factor scores across multiple tests based on the same underlying construct. Concurrent validity of this test was examined with NEO-PI-R and it was found to be satisfactory (r=0.66 to 0.90).


3.4	Socio-Cultural Considerations

A good psychometric test should demonstrate sensitivity towards social and cultural factors that are supposed to have a bearing on the construct the test is measuring. At the same time, it should not be biased towards any particular group of people (especially those protected by law). A good test should not show a significant positive or negative discrimination towards any particular group of people.

Scores against the following sociocultural variables were examined in order to identify any such bias on the part of the test:

A.	Gender

B.	Age

C.	Race

Only two such variables demonstrated some sort of association with few factors. These are summarized below:

Introduction
The Professional Profile 2© is a personality questionnaire that analyses an individual’s preferences, motivations and values at work. Consisting of 112 questions, it is a useful tool for employee recruitment, training and career development.
The assessment provides an in-depth analysis of a candidate's personality profile and job temperament based on 14 fundamental opposing dimensions of the human character (e.g. introversion - extraversion, team work - autonomy). The scale was specifically developed to evaluate personality traits in the workplace, identify a candidate's strengths, potential weaknesses and areas for development. Profiles generated by the Professional Profile 2© will help decision-makers to optimise job, team and organisational fit.
The Professional Profile 2© has been validated with more than a thousand professionals from a variety of occupations, educational levels and age groups. Due to its robust psychological approach and practitioner-based results, the assessment is utilised by HR personnel, managers, psychologists, coaches, training and recruitment consultants all over the world. There are several features that distinguish the Professional Profile 2© from other work-based personality assessments. These include:
•	More contemporary and business relevant items
•	Significantly shorter with only 112 items
•	Average completion time of 15 minutes
•	Measured dimensions based on both theory and competency requirements specified by HR practitioners, line managers, recruitment and training consultants
•	Developed in multiple languages simultaneously with professionals across the globe
•	Ipsative scaling to guard against social desirability and impression management
•	Built-in response audit to ensure face validity
•	Valid and reliable with studies conducted with professionals from 4 continents (North America, Europe, East Asia and Australasia)
•	Developed using state-of-the-art psychometric techniques such as Thurstonian Item Response Theory
•	User-friendly and customizable reports that can be easily tailored by the administrator
•	Practical and easy-to understand reports
•	Information on job fit, team fit, organisational fit, individual strengths and areas for development as standard.
In this manual & user guide you will find useful information about the questionnaire’s development, theoretical background, psychometric properties and practical applications.
Chapters 1, 2, 3 and 8 are specifically tailored for HR practitioners whilst chapters 4, 5, 6 and 7 comprise of detailed technical information and psychological information.
This test has been granted registration as a psychological test by the British Psychological Society, Psychological Testing Centre.




 
1.	General Description and Applicability
The Professional Profile 2© provides an in-depth analysis of a candidate’s personality profile and professional aspirations based on fourteen fundamental aspects of the human character. The assessment combines values, motivations and personality in order to enable recruiters, HR professionals and psychologists to accurately assess fit between a job and a candidate. This dual approach is valuable as:

•	Personality traits influence how an individual is likely to act, adapt to and function effectively in a given situation

•	Motivations and values influence choices and decisions and therefore shed light on what drives an individual to act


Personality

The Professional Profile 2© measures seven opposing pairs of personality dimensions within the context of work. The assessment is based on established models of personality – like the “Five Factor Model” and the “Theory of Psychological Types”, as well as documented evidence on the value of personality measurement to organisational effectiveness. Dimensions represent an individual’s approach to communicating, conflict handling, coping with pressure, interacting with others, managing ambiguity, decision-making and problem solving respectively. The seven paired dimensions of personality are defined as:

Persuasiveness
The ease with which an individual is able to convince and influence others using tact and adapt his/her speech for an audience, whilst Straightforwardness refers to being authentic and direct in communication, with a need to remain transparent. High persuasiveness scorers tend to be strategic and influential. High straightforwardness scorers generally thrive in settings where transparency is essential.

Flexibility
A willingness to be open to making concessions when dealing with others in order to seek consensus and avoid conflict, whilst Firmness refers to a tendency to defend one's own point of view and remain determined during exchanges with others. High flexibility scorers are generally good at taking others’ perspectives into account during discussions. High firmness scorers are well suited for roles that require bargaining and tough negotiations.

Emotional Distance
The tendency to stay calm and composed even when faced with stressful or unsettling situations, whilst Emotional Sensitivity refers to the tendency to react to stress, take things to heart and readily express emotions. High emotional distance scorers are best suited for stressful settings that require a cool head and emotional control at all times. High emotional sensitivity scorers tend to be the first in a team to react to subtleties in the environment.

Extraversion
The tendency to be outgoing and lively, seeking to develop many friendly contacts, whilst Introversion refers to being reserved and formal, choosing to maintain only a few close relationships. High extraversion scorers thrive in positions that require much relationship building and public speaking. High introversion scorers generally remain productive and motivated even without social contact.

Improvisation
Being spontaneous and acting without clear planning, and at ease when facing unexpected situations, whilst Structure refers to a preference for being organised, methodical and referring to procedures. High structure scorers are best suited for projects that have very strict deadlines and require detailed planning well in advance. High improvisation scorers tend to find solutions even in very ambiguous circumstances.




8
Professional Profile 2© Central Test 2015
 
Focus on facts
The tendency to be objective, logical, analytical and rational when evaluating information and making decisions, whilst Intuition refers to the tendency to appraise situations and make choices based on personal insights, instincts, impressions and 'gut feeling'. High intuition scorers are assets to environments that demand sharp instincts and immediate remedies. Individuals who have a high focus on facts score excel in settings where objective analysis is required and quick decisions costly.

Rule-following
The tendency to respect and comply with established guidelines and standards set by the organisation and society, whilst Free-thinking is the tendency to create or modify rules if existing ones are considered inefficient and impractical. High free-thinking scorers tend to thrive in organisations that encourage productivity without clearly-defined rules. High rule-following scorers are best suited for positions that require enforcement of regulations for quality, safety or legal reasons.

Values and Motivations

The Professional Profile 2© also measures seven opposing pairs of values and motivations within the context of work. These dimensions are based on extensive evidence highlighting the importance of values, needs and drives for employee success. Modern ways of working are more complex, fluid, integrated and demanding than roles of yesteryear. Employee engagement, work-life balance, well-being, career- management, cross-functional and cross-border team working are key issues facing the twenty-first century organisation (Cruise, 2011; Judge & Kammeyer-Mueller, 2012; Sonnentag, Kuttler, & Fritz, 2010; Stiglbauer, Selenko, Batinic, & Jodlbauer, 2012). Managers and recruiters therefore need to understand the value employees place on work, as well as their; a) commitment orientation; b) leadership orientation;
c) task preferences; d) achievement values; e) teamwork orientation; and f) adaptability orientation.

The seven paired dimensions of values and motivations are defined as:

Involvement at work
The importance given to work and an individual’s emotional bonding with an organisation, whilst Personal balance refers to a preference for separating one's personal and professional lives and balancing the two. High involvement at work scorers are well suited for organisations with a culture of full dedication and commitment. High personal balance scorers are best suited for organisations that encourage and advocate work-like balance.

Desire to lead
The inclination to take charge in situations and to seek roles that require leading people, whilst Desire for guidance refers to the preference for supervision, regular feedback, and clearly defined directives. High desire to lead scorers are likely to take the lead on projects and are eager to take up responsibilities. High desire for guidance scorers thrive in positions that offer opportunities for direct mentorship and learning.

Need for action
A tendency to be dynamic and prefer short term projects that deliver quick and concrete results, while Need for reflection refers to a preference for complex, long term projects that yield results in the future and require more strategy, patience and reflection. High need for action scorers excel in settings characterised by a fast work pace and speedy decision-making. High need for reflection scorers are assets to roles characterised by strategic tasks spread over long periods of time.

Ambition
The competitive drive towards success and achievement, setting high and risky goals, whilst Humility refers to the tendency to be satisfied with one's current status and be comfortable with professional stability. High ambition scorers tend to thrive in organisations that are highly pressurised and competitive. High humility scorers enjoy stability at work and thrive in harmonious environments.

Autonomy
A preference for independence and freedom while working. Team work refers to the tendency to enjoy group work, be cooperative and focus on team cohesiveness. High autonomy scorers are well-suited for projects that require self-reliance and individual focus. High team work scorers excel in organisations that demand idea generation through collaboration.
 
Altruism
The inclination to be concerned about people and generous with one's time and resources, wanting to contribute to the greater good. Individualism refers to being practical and realistic with a focus on one's personal objectives. High altruism scorers excel in organisations that actively promote social responsibility and people development. High individualism scorers tend to thrive in highly results-oriented settings.

Novelty seeking
Being inquisitive with an inclination to explore and experiment with new ways of doing things. Familiarity seeking refers to being more comfortable with what one is accustomed to and follow proven methods over experimental approaches. High novelty seeking scorers are committed to innovation and improving outdated practices. High familiarity seeking scorers are assets to organisations that require the use of methods that have a proven track record.

1.1.	Main areas of application
The Professional Profile 2© was specifically designed to help HR professionals, managers and business practitioners recruit, select and develop employees across the globe. It may be used throughout an employee's career life-span and is suitable for both management and non-management employees. The questionnaire can also be used in conjunction with other occupational-based assessments to acquire a more comprehensive profile of individuals in specific roles. For example, combining the Professional Profile 2© with the Sales Profile R© provides in-depth insight into how well a sales person is likely to integrate into a team, the type of sales s/he would be best suited for and the organisational setting s/he is likely to thrive in.

Applications are varied. The Professional Profile 2© is suitable for individuals, teams and organisational assessment. Specifically, it is designed for use in: a) individual recruitment, career management, career guidance, coaching and development; b) team development, profiling, conflict resolution and effectiveness; and c) organisational succession planning; performance evaluation, performance management, job analyses, culture and engagement audits.


1.2.	Question structure
The Professional Profile 2© consists of 84 core questions and 28 audit questions. Questions are presented in forced-choice format, whereby each question is comprised of two general statements that require the candidate to choose the option that best represents their preference. Each question assesses different components of either personality or motivation dimensions. This means that each dimension is measured by 16 items - 8 items for each factor. The questionnaire therefore measures 224 behaviours whilst appearing significantly shorter. Items are mirrored so that each response option assesses a different dimension – personality items are paired with personality items and motivation items are paired with motivation items. This combined approach means that both, the individual’s personality trait and motivation orientation associated with a targeted behaviour, are captured. This results in a more realistic measurement of human behaviour as personality and motivation at work have traditionally been measured with separate instruments. This item structure also reduces the likelihood of respondent fatigue and response bias. Furthermore, forcing respondents to choose one of two options safeguards against impression management and candidates’ attempts to fake results.


Example 1.
I try to find my way out of disagreements
	I know how to be inventive in situations of uncertainty

Example 2.

Being helpful is essential to me
	I enjoy being responsible for a group

Figure 2. Examples of two forced-choice items on the Professional Profile 2©.


In addition to the core 84 questions, the Professional Profile 2© has a built-in response audit scale comprised of 28 questions; the items were generated from statistical computations and are based on correlations from the trait and dimension factor structures. The total number of questions to be completed by each candidate is 112. The audit scale uses pairs of forced-choice descriptors, which measures the same dimension in order to directly assess the main and opposing factors of each dimension (e.g. Introversion vs. Extraversion). As individuals tend to be more experienced with Likert-scale work assessments in which they can position themselves in relation to a trait, the response audit facilitates both a pleasant test-taking experience, as well as the face validity of results. Figure 3 shows an example of an item from the response audit used in the Professional Profile 2©.

I am non-conforming
	I respect rules

1.3.	Mode of administration, languages and special requirements
Professional Profile 2© is administered online and works with all modern computer operating systems including Windows, MAC-OS and Linux. A PDF version of the questionnaire is available if required. Like other modern personality questionnaires, the assessment can be administered remotely and without supervision. However, should standardised group assessments be required, this can easily be accommodated. Reports are solely generated online. Therefore, if the test has been administered in paper-format, an administrator must manually enter candidate responses into the online platform of the questionnaire to generate candidate reports.

The questionnaire is currently available in Arabic, English and French and will soon be available in Dutch, German, Portuguese and Spanish. For more information on language availability please contact the Central Test Consulting Service (consulting@centraltest.com).

Professional Profile 2© was designed to be easily used by individuals from diverse backgrounds and those with a range of special needs. All test-takers are able to adjust visual screen specifications based on individual requirements. For example, respondents with visual impairments or those requiring a larger font size for comfort, may increase the font size by enlarging the computer screen’s display (by pressing "CTRL" and "+"). Similarly, respondents are able to access test instructions throughout the course of the assessment. It is also possible to navigate through questions or change answers as desired by selecting on-screen prompts. Alternatively, for blind candidates or those with severe visual disabilities, items may be read aloud by a trained administrator. Individuals with hearing impairments are not excluded from taking the questionnaire since all content is presented visually.

Although the average time for completion is 15 minutes, the Professional Profile 2© has no set time limit. Therefore, individuals with learning disabilities are able to take the time they need to complete the assessment. Administrators are encouraged to consider whether a candidate who needed an unusual amount of time to take the test may have special needs. Please contact the test publisher for guidance on administering the questionnaire to individuals or groups with special requirements.


1.4.	Social desirability

Social desirability is the tendency to present oneself in a manner that will be viewed favourably by others. When done unintentionally, it is referred to as self-deception. When done intentionally it is referred to as impression management (Tett & Simonet, 2011). As people are social by nature, they are generally inclined to seek some degree of social acceptance. Unlike aptitude tests, the results of personality questionnaires like the Professional Profile 2© are affected by impression management. An individual’s attempt to present himself/herself in a favourable light negatively impacts test results, whether the questionnaire is completed online or in paper-based format (Dodou & de Winter, 2014).

Social desirability is a particular concern in the context of recruitment, as a candidate is usually tempted to answer in a way to impress the recruiter even though there are no ‘right’ or ‘wrong’ answers. Candidates may provide responses according to what they feel, recruiters may expect from them to fulfil a role. As a result, social desirability is a significant risk to the validity of assessments (Mueller-Hanson, Heggestad, & Thornton, 2003). If social desirability was equivalent across all candidates, then test administrators would be able to factor it into the scoring and interpretation of results. However as faking differs across individuals, distortions in scores may result in an increased likelihood of the wrong candidate being hired (e.g. Rosse, Stecher, Miller, & Levin, 1998).

The Professional Profile 2© actively guards against social desirability with the use of an ipsative questionnaire format and built-in response audit. Research has found ipsative measures to be more
 
resistant to distorted responding compared to more commonly used Likert-scales (Bowen, Martin, & Hunt, 2002; Kluger, Reilly, & Russell, 1991). Not only does the ipsative format reduce candidates’ ability to improve their scores, items are combined in such a manner that makes it difficult for candidates to conceive a response strategy as items are paired with equally appealing response options.


1.5.	Test-taker requirements and recommendations
The Professional Profile 2© was designed for members of the general population 20 years and older and is suitable for all occupational roles. It is recommended that candidates receive notification of the objective of the assessment, conditions under which it will be administered, how data will be handled and stored, and the nature of any feedback well in advance. Potential candidates should also be asked to inform the test administrator beforehand of any special needs or requirements.

Previous test-taking experience is not required to take the Professional Profile 2©. Similarly, advanced education and work experience are not requirements to successfully take the assessment.


1.6.	Administrator qualifications and recommendations

The assessment was developed to be used by human resource (HR) professionals, training and executive development professionals, counsellors and psychologists. To administer the Professional Profile 2©, practitioners must complete standardised training and/ or possess accredited testing standards qualifications. Training is designed to ensure administrators are able to administer the assessment under standardised conditions; score and norm results; interpret scores and generate written and verbal feedback for a range of organisational needs.

It is recommended that all administrators take the Professional Profile 2©, prior to administering the assessment making sure to comply with all instructions. This will enable practitioners to become familiar with the assessment and therefore anticipate questions or challenges that may be identified by candidates.

Test administration must be in accordance with the organisation’s code of practice, as well as legal assessment guidelines on the use of psychometric assessments for employment purposes. All steps must be taken to ensure test administration is objective and fair. Therefore, it is particularly recommended that administrators are aware of international and country laws governing; a) Data Protection, b) Gender Discrimination, c) Age Discrimination, d) Race and Ethnicity Relations, e) Disabilities Discrimination, and f) Religious Discrimination prior to administration.

Additionally, prior to use administrators must be familiar with Central Test's conditions of use and code of ethics and adhere to its quality charter. Administrators must:

•	Ensure that the appropriate test has been chosen to meet the specified organisational need.
•	Make sure that the candidate is informed about the assessment and its purpose prior to administration.
•	Achieve candidates’ consent prior to administration
•	Use appropriate norms to interpret results
•	Provide candidates with feedback on results.
•	Delete questionnaire results and personal candidate data upon demand.
•	Treat results and candidate data confidentially and obtain candidate consent before transferring personal data or results to third parties.
•	Ensure that only qualified personnel administer assessments and have access to data
•	Ensure that test materials and reports are stored in secure environments

For more detailed information about Central Test's code of ethics, refer to Central Test's quality charter.
 
1.6.1.	Testing conditions
For best results and candidate experience, the Professional Profile 2© should be administered in a quiet, well lit and ventilated environment. Candidates must be informed about the assessment in advance and should have sufficient time to take it. In order to reduce candidates’ anxiety, the assessment should be referred to as a questionnaire and not as a test.

For online assessment, it is important that candidates have comfortable seating, adequate desk space, adequate computer screen size, a keyboard and a mouse. Prior to testing, administrators should ensure that all relevant candidate background information has been collected and verified in order to confirm that the candidate taking the assessment is the right individual.

In cases where individuals take the assessment in the privacy of their home or a convenient location, it is recommended that they do so using a computer they are familiar and comfortable with. For security purposes, all background information and candidate details must be provided to the administrator prior to a candidate taking the assessment. The background information is prefilled by the test administrator but can be corrected by the candidate after the completion of the assessment, when verifying ‘Additional Information’. Candidates receive notification to take the questionnaire via e-mail with an embedded web link and login details. Web links to the questionnaire are password protected and unique to a candidate’s account. It is possible to set expiration dates in order to ensure candidates complete the questionnaire within a specified time-frame. For additional security administrators can also configure the invitation to ensure that the assessment must be completed from the same IP address once the questionnaire has been accessed.

The Professional Profile 2© has no time restriction. However, candidates generally take between 12 and 20 minutes to complete the questionnaire. For monitoring purposes, completion times are recorded to ensure a pleasant candidate test-taking experience. It also allows administrators to see if a candidate rushed through the questionnaire or needed an unusual amount of time to complete the assessment. Candidates are encouraged to answer questions quickly in order to avoid spending too much time thinking about each statement. For personality questionnaires, it is generally recommended that respondents answer spontaneously as there are no right or wrong answers. Candidates should also be urged to answer honestly as the built-in response audit is designed to identify inconsistencies in responses.


1.6.2.	Answering questions
Although instructions for completing the Professional Profile 2© questionnaire are presented on-screen it is important that administrators establish and maintain rapport with candidates. Prior to starting the assessment, instructions should be read aloud by the administrator with candidates given an opportunity to ask questions before taking the assessment. Administrators should verify that candidates understand what they are required to do prior to starting the assessment session.

Guidelines for a few scenarios:

•	If candidates have questions after the assessment session has begun, the administrator should try to answer them without disturbing other candidates.

•		If a candidate does not understand a question, the administrator should answer carefully and avoid explaining the meaning of words or items in his/her own words in order to not lead the candidate toward specific responses. Instead, the administrator should check if the question is part of the lexical guide (contact Central Test for a copy). This guide contains standardised descriptions of items that could be potentially challenging for some individuals.

•	If an administrator suspects that a candidate’s lack of understanding is due to education level, the assessment should be abandoned.
 
•	If an administrator suspects that a candidate’s lack of understanding is due to language proficiency, the administrator should ask the candidate to take the assessment in the language they are most comfortable with (or language s/he dreams in).

•	If an administrator suspects that a candidate’s lack of understanding is due to overcautious responding, the candidate should be encouraged to endorse their most likely response.


1.6.3.	Administration instructions
On screen administration instructions are the same for individual home-based and administrator-based assessment. Standardised instructions ensure that all participants are exposed to the same conditions during assessment; the assessment process is fair and objective and assessment conditions do not directly or indirectly give any individual or group an advantage.

Prior to assessment, administrators should ensure they have the following:

•	This manual
•	Welcome script to inform candidates of the purpose of the assessment, why the assessment is being used as part of the organisational process, and what will happen during the testing session
•	Test log to verify candidate details and record incidents during the session
•	In the case of paper-based assessment, printed PDF copies of the Professional Profile 2©
questionnaire and pens
•	Lexical guide to answer potential candidate questions

Candidates who take the Professional Profile 2© as a home-based assessment from their private computer are guided through the instructions on their screen. Administrators of group administrations may guide participants after the introduction screen for the Professional Profile 2© has been accessed and the candidate is seated in front of the computer or the printed PDF copies by reading out the following on- screen instructions:


The on-screen instructions will take you through the entire questionnaire. Please follow on your screen (paper) whilst I read the instructions out loud:
Welcome!
You are about to take the Professional Profile 2©, a work personality assessment. Please read the
instructions that follow.
The following questionnaire will assess your personality at work.
This questionnaire is composed of 112 questions.
It should take you approximately 15 minutes to complete the assessment.
For each set, indicate which statement best describes your personality or your beliefs by clicking the corresponding box.

It is possible that neither of the statements describes you exactly.

In such instances, kindly choose the statement that describes you the closest.



After a brief pause say:
•	Do you understand what you are expected to do?
•	If you would like to increase the screen size, please press CTRL +, if you would like to decrease the screen size, please press CTRL -. Please try this now (Pause and allow candidates to adjust screens).
•	If you need help, please raise your hand and I will come by to help you.
 
•	All questions have to be answered. If you forget to answer a question, an information box will appear and indicate the questions that you need to answer in order to be able to continue.
•	At the end of the questionnaire you will be presented with some demographic questions. Please answer them honestly.
•	Do you have any questions before we start?
After a paper-pencil administration:
It is important that administrators verify that all questions have been answered as the scoring system does not allow for any questions to be left blank.
At the end of the assessment, administrators must collect all testing materials. Paper-pencil questionnaires need to be stored in a secure place and only be accessible to authorized individuals. Testing materials like questionnaires and reports need to be shredded by administrators to ensure appropriate disposal.



1.7.	Technical and security issues
Below are guidelines governing the security of administering the Professional Profile 2©. Guidelines ensure that every administration of the Professional Profile 2© is a pleasant, objective and fair process. Security specifications ensure that candidate information is protected both during administration and after assessment completion.


1.7.1.	Respecting the ethical charter

As a publisher of online assessment tools, Central Test respects the legal conventions of French labour law and the regulatory provisions resulting from the law of 6 January 1978 pertaining to information technology, files and freedoms: Declaration N°1834067.
Test results may not be forwarded to a third person without the explicit consent of the test candidate. A candidate has a right to access his/her results.
The assessor must grant him/her access automatically or when the candidate requests it.
The candidate has the right to request that all his/her personal data be permanently deleted. To do this, s/he must contact either the organisation directly, who will delete his/her data, or Central Test.


1.7.2.	Confidentiality and Security of user data

Central Test guarantees the security and confidentiality of data generated during the administration of online tests. Access codes are encrypted in the database and cannot be accessed by anyone. Passwords are computationally generated and are not visible to companies. A URL rewriting module (secure link generator) is installed and has transparent parameter passing for the user.
Browsing and data exchange via the Central Test application can be secured and encrypted using SSL. Secure browsing is guaranteed by the https protocol, which is represented by an icon in the form of a padlock in the internet browser. The Central Test application has a « GeoTrust SSL True BusinessID » digital signature, issued and certified by GeoTrust©. This certificate is irrefutable evidence of our serious attitude towards security and protects all Central Test assessments from phishing attacks. Invitations to take tests are made via secured links that are of limited use (in terms of numbers and duration). The tests are timed and IP addresses are tracked. Access to questionnaires may be limited in terms of numbers and duration.
 
1.7.3.	Hardware architecture and server availability

Central Test’s architecture consists of a network of 10 physical dedicated servers providing high-quality service.
Each server is housed in a specialised data centre featuring:
•	dedicated lines
•	a guaranteed bandwidth of 100Mbps (for each server)
•	redundant energy sources
•	4-hour Equipment Restoration Time
•	2-hour Incident Intervention Time
•	monitoring and assistance 24/7/365
Access to server administration is restricted by the IP address and is password-secured. Data exchanges are secure.

The Central Test application has been tested using the following browsers:
•	Internet Explorer 8 or above
•	Firefox 21 or above
•	Google Chrome 30 or above
•	Safari 7 or above


1.7.4.	Computer generated reports
Reports can be generated within minutes of candidates completing the questionnaire. The Professional Profile 2© report is user friendly, customisable and available in several languages. Report content can be tailored depending on the needs of the administrator. Separate reports may be configured for the candidate and the company, recruiter, coach or psychologist. Reports are available in PDF format.
