2.	Conceptual & Practical Scale Development
Professional Profile 2© was developed using robust theoretical concepts, practical and relevant work requirements and advanced scientific analyses. Unlike other work-based personality assessments, the item bank for the questionnaire was primarily derived from recruiter surveys, job board analyses and HR and training practitioner feedback. This empirically–based practitioner approach has resulted in the development of an administrator-friendly, work-relevant, valid assessment that candidates enjoy taking. All items in the item bank were piloted and pre-tested with professionals from four continents (North America, Europe, East Asia and Australasia) in order to ensure assessed work behaviours were contemporary, global and culture-free.



2.1.	Theoretical background
2.1.1.	The history and value of psychometric testing to personnel assessment
Historically, the Latin origin of the word personality (persona) referred to masks worn to represent roles that actors interpreted. Today, the term covers psychological characteristics common to all human beings and is used to explain human nature and individual differences. Analysis of individual differences dates back to 460-377BC, when Hippocrates proposed a biological model of personality arguing that temperament can be used to identify key human behaviours. This generated the first classification of personality as individuals were categorised as sanguine, phlegmatic, melancholic or choleric, that corresponded to traits of optimistic, relaxed, analytical or irritable respectively.

Considered the father of psychometrics, Sir Francis Galton (1884) was the first scientist to analyse the structure of personality using a lexical hypothesis. The lexical hypothesis is one of the most widely used underlying theories of personality and rests on two arguments; a) personality characteristics that are most important in people’s lives will eventually become a part of their language; and b) more important personality characteristics are more likely to be encoded within language as a single word. This lexical hypothesis was used by Gordon Allport (1937) to generate a list of 17,953 unique terms to describe personality. Allport was the first to use the term “personality” in its modern application and produce an understandable classification of human traits that led to the clustering and later factor structure of personality traits by Raymond Cattell (1957)and Costa & McCrae (1985).

In the business world of the 21st century, psychometric measurement tools are increasingly used in the workplace as they help to predict behaviour and performance in a reliable way. Across the globe organisations utilise personality and ability assessments to make personnel decisions for recruitment, selection, promotion and development. Unlike judgements based on subjective perceptions, such as those derived from traditional job interviews, psychometric assessments offer a more objective, fair, reliable, valid and legally-defensible approach to identifying desired work behaviours. The measurement of personality represents a key component in identifying individuals who are best suited for particular situations, contexts and planned objectives as individual values, interests, preferences and abilities represent core composites of personality. Therefore the use of personality assessment is integral to any process where the individual is central to productivity (Cruise, 2012).


2.1.2.	Personality and employee effectiveness
An assessment can be called psychometric insofar as it is standardised, applicable across groups and has proven reliability and validity. Personality testing remains one of the best predictors of both job performance and training potential and is certainly a better measure of ‘fit’ than traditional interviews (Barrick & Mount, 1991; Robertson & Smith, 2001). Approaches to personality developed over the last 30 years have focused on accurately measuring individual behaviour in a professional context. The link between personality and work success has been long established with several studies showing that personality is a predictor of job performance (De Fruyt & Mervielde, 1999; Hurtz & Donovan, 2000; Salgado, 1997).
 
One study that has significantly influenced the use of personality tests in the workplace was conducted by Barrick and Mount (1991). Using the Five Factor Model (FFM) of personality (see page 21 for detailed description of the FFM), they found personality dimensions accurately predicted at least one aspect of performance at work in a variety of jobs including professionals, police, managers, sales staff, and skilled/semi-skilled workers. Another study showed that Extraversion and Conscientiousness predict job performance (De Fruyt & Mervielde, 1999), whilst Extraversion and Openness are positively related to job performance and creativity and higher levels of Neuroticism have a negative effect on individual work success (Rothmann & Coetzer, 2003).

Secondly, evidence has linked personality to social and emotional components of work such as social investment at work (Hudson, Roberts, & Lodi-Smith, 2012); job satisfaction (Christiansen, Sliter, & Frost, 2014a; Heller, Ferris, Brown, & Watson, 2009; Lam & Ozorio, 2012) and job commitment (Panaccio & Vandenberghe, 2012). For example, Christiansen et al. (2014), found individuals with lower levels of Agreeableness and Conscientiousness reported more distress while performing tasks related to those traits, whilst employees with higher levels of Neuroticism perceived tasks related to Extraversion as most stressful. Furthermore Mathieu (2013) found that narcissism was positively related to Extraversion and negatively related to Agreeableness and had a significant influence on job satisfaction. Findings are important for HR professionals in light of the high financial costs associated with employee satisfaction and turnover.

Thirdly, the relationship between personality and leadership has also been confirmed. De Vries (2012) for example found that personality predicts different leadership styles. Using the HEXACO personality model (Lee & Ashton, 2004), results showed direct effects of Honesty-Humility on ethical leadership; Extraversion on charismatic leadership, Agreeableness on supportive leadership and Conscientiousness on task- oriented leadership. Similarly, using the FFM model of personality, Craik et al. (2002) found Conscientiousness and Openness were characteristic of a strategic management style; Extraversion, Openness and lower levels of Agreeableness were typical of an interpersonal management style; and Extraversion and Openness were indicators of managerial potential. Due to its effect on CEO leadership, personality is therefore indirectly related to organisational effectiveness (Colbert, Barrick, & Bradley, 2014).

Fourthly, personality plays an important role in team settings. Interpersonal team-work behaviour is predicted by Agreeableness (Tasa, Sears, & Schat, 2011). However, while people who score high on Agreeableness tend to be great team players, they are less well suited to “fill the role of devil’s advocate” (LePine, Buckman, Crawford, & Methot, 2011). Researchers have also found that team-member- exchange engagement was related to higher levels of Extraversion but lower levels of Neuroticism (Liao, Yang, Wang, Drown, & Shi, 2013). With team fit being a key ingredient in modern organisational success, identifying individuals with personality traits that compliment business agenda is a primary concern for HR professionals.

Finally, personality is also linked to negative employee work behaviours. Brown et al. (2010) for example, found evidence that ethical decision making is not solely based on education but related to personality factors such as narcissism and empathy, whilst Kalshoven, Den Hartog & De Hoogh (2010) found Conscientiousness, Agreeableness and Emotional Stability to be related to ethical leadership. Furthermore, while organisational citizenship behaviours (voluntary extra role behaviours) are related to individual Agreeableness, Emotionality, Openness to Experience and low Honesty-Humility (Bourdage, Lee, Lee, & Shin, 2012); dark personality traits (avoidant and narcissistic) are negative predictors of transformational leadership (Khoo & Burch, 2008). Negative personality traits particularly affect customer service experiences and harm overall performance of organisations. Furthermore, Harris and Ogbonna (2009) proposed that employees who deliberately sabotaged firms were generally Thrill Seekers, Apathetics, Customer Revengers and Money Grabbers. These findings emphasise the financial, social and psychological costs of selecting employees who poorly fit with roles, teams and organisations.


2.1.3.	Different approaches to personality: type versus trait
There are two main approaches to classifying individuals in personality psychology: type versus trait approach. Type theories categorise personality as a set of interrelated elements that make an individual unique. People are assigned to discrete categories of personality that are different to each other (e.g.
 
introversion – extraversion). Types express preferences that allow us to predict behaviour, understand changes in behaviour over time and identify how behaviours may be developed with intervention. Type theories regard opposing traits as two distinct classes and are therefore appropriate assessments for workplace development, career guidance and coaching.

Trait theories on the other hand, regard personality as degrees of expression along a continuum of behaviour. A trait could be defined as the tendency to act in a certain way and could be expressed by the behaviour of the individual in diverse situations (Nadeau, Acier, Miranda, Pervin, & John, 2004). For instance, when someone is described as a kind person it means that s/he tends to behave in a kind way, regardless of the situation. Trait approaches are commonly used in work assessments as they are effective predictors of stable behaviour. It is expected that a rigorous person will be a rigorous employee. For recruitment purposes, trait theories are recommended over type theories as they give a more detailed description of an individual’s potential instead of allocating unique individuals to a limited number of personality types.

The Five Factor Model (FFM) of personality is considered the standard in trait personality measurement as it represents well researched global personality traits. The model is comprised of: Openness - the extent to which an individual is intellectually curious, has insight and is imaginative; Conscientiousness - the extent to which an individual is reliable, persistent and ambitious; Extraversion - the inclination to be sociable, desiring to be among others, thus requiring excitement and stimulation; Agreeableness - the tendency to be humanistic, cooperative and trusting of others; and Neuroticism - the inclination to feel negative emotions including fear, guilt, sadness and anger (John & Srivastava, 1999; Major, Turner, & Fletcher, 2006; Mooradian, Renzl, & Matzler, 2006). The Professional Profile 2© adopts the trait approach and measures stable personality traits in the workplace. The assessment allows practitioners to identify qualities such as ambition, desire to lead, or emotional resilience required for specific roles.


“A musician must make music, an artist must paint, a poet must write, if he is to be ultimately at peace with himself. What a man can be, he must be.”
Abraham Maslow, (1954) pg. 93




2.1.4.	Personality theories used in the Professional Profile 2©
In addition to the Five Factor Model (FFM) of personality, several established theories were used for the conceptual and practical development of the Professional Profile 2©. Other theoretical frameworks include C.G. Jung (1971) Theory of Psychological Types; William Schutz (1958) Fundamental Interpersonal Relations Orientation; Lee and Ashton(2004) HEXACO Model of Personality and J. Holland (1958) Occupational Themes (RIASEC). A combined theoretical approach was adopted in order to ensure conceptual richness, practical pertinence and prevent content restriction that may result from a single model.

2.1.4.a	Theory of Psychological Types – Carl G. Jung (1921)
In 1921, Carl Gustav Jung categorised humans according to two attitude types (Extraversion - Introversion) and four functions. Functions describe how people perceive their environment (Sensation – Intuition) and how they judge what they perceive (Thinking – Feeling). Jung argued that much of behaviour that appears random is actually orderly and consistent due to differences in the way individuals prefer to use perception and judgement (Perceiving – Judging). For example, people who process information through Sensation will pay close attention to details, whereas an individual who perceives through Intuition will rely more on broad patterns. Individuals who tend to make use of the Thinking function base their judgments more on logic, whereas individuals with a preference for the Feeling function tend to make judgments based on personal values (Arnau, Green, Rosen, Gleaves, & Melancon, 2003). The combination of attitude type and functions results in 16 different personality types.
 
For the development of dimensions and items on the Professional Profile 2©, how individuals judge what they see, make decisions and process information are considered key components for success at work.


2.1.4.b	Fundamental Interpersonal Relations Orientation (FIRO) – William Schutz (1958)
Schutz (1958) proposed that humans have three interpersonal needs; a need for inclusion, affection and control. The theory is based on the belief that when people get together to form a group there are three main interpersonal needs that they are looking to obtain. Inclusion – relates to whether an individual tends to include others in interaction or want others to include them and be recognised. Affection – refers to how an individual relates to others and how much someone invests in a relationship or desires another to demonstrate affection towards him/her. Control – differentiates between people who desire to be dominated and guided and those who prefer to be leading and take responsibility. Needs are either wanted or expressed by individuals when interacting with others. This results in six different dimensions namely: Expressed Inclusion (EI), Wanted Inclusion (WI), Expressed Affection (EA), Wanted Affection (WA), Expressed Control (EC) and Wanted Control (WC). The FIRO was included in the development of the Professional Profile 2©, as how much interaction an employee wants in the areas of socialising, leadership and responsibilities will inform organisations about an individual’s potential team fit, culture fit and leadership effectiveness.


2.1.4.c	HEXACO Model of Personality – Lee and Ashton(2004)
Lee and Ashton (2004) proposed a 6-dimension, trait-based model of personality based on evidence of previous research on trait-based personality models. The model includes all dimensions measured by the FFM as well as the additional dimension of Honesty-Humility (H). Within the model dimensions are classified as: Honesty-Humility (H) – measures facets of sincerity, fairness, greed avoidance and modesty. Emotionality (E) – assesses fearfulness, anxiety, dependence and sentimentality. Extraversion (X) – identifies social self-esteem, social boldness, sociability and liveliness. Agreeableness (A) – measures forgiveness, gentleness, flexibility and patience. Conscientiousness (C) – assesses organisation, diligence, perfectionism and prudence. Openness to Experience (O) – examines an individual’s tendency towards aesthetic appreciation, inquisitiveness, creativity and unconventionality. For the development of the Professional Profile 2©, facets measured by the (H) dimension of HEXACO were important as an individual’s values and expression of said values significantly influence role, team and organisational fit as well as his/her approach to making decisions.


2.1.4.d	RIASEC Career Interest Model John Holland (1958)
Psychologist John Holland argued that the choice of a vocation is an expression of personality and the closer the match between an individual’s personality type and chosen profession, the more successful and satisfied the individual will be. Holland therefore demonstrated the link between vocational environment and personality and developed a career interest model called RIASEC – an acronym for six personality types. Types are described as: Realistic (Doers) - those who prefer things to ideas or people. They are generally independent, stable, genuine and practical. Investigative (Thinkers) – individuals who tend to enjoy solving problems and conducting research. They are generally analytical, rational and prioritise intellectual or scientific activities. Artistic (Creators) – those who prefer to work with ideas, abstractions and concepts. They are generally, creative, sensitive, expressive and intuitive. Social (Helpers) – individuals who prefer tasks that help, support or teach others. They are generally caring, cooperative, empathetic and friendly. Enterprising (Persuaders) – those who prefer work that involves leadership, politics, business and public-speaking. They are generally ambitious, assertive, confident and risk-taking. Conventional (Organisers) – individuals who value precision and accuracy. They are generally efficient, thorough, orderly and detail-oriented. The RIASEC model was included in the development of the Professional Profile 2© given documented evidence on the financial, productivity and psychological costs associated with employee turnover, negative work behaviours and poor performance. Understanding employee career preferences is important for HR and recruitment professionals to identify and manage individuals who are poorly suited for specific roles, tasks and environments. Whilst these six
 
categories are not definitive of the types of people that exist, they do allow a simple ordering of vocational personalities. Figure 4 depict the hexagonal model of the RIASEC framework.




Figure 4. Holland’s (1977) RIASEC Model of Occupational Themes


2.1.5.	Motivations and values in the workplace
The relationship between individual values, motivations and work success has been long documented. Several theories point to the role of motivation and values in determining an individual’s life goals, the decisions s/he makes and explains why individuals behave in certain ways on a day to day basis.

Values are principles or standards of behaviour that govern one’s judgement of what is important in life. Sociologist Morris Massey (1979) argued that values are formed during three periods: a) The Imprint Period – until the age of seven our values are blindly absorbed from our parents; b) The Modelling Period – lasts between seven and approximately thirteen years of age, other sources such as teachers and role models influence our values and we try to imitate others; c) The Socialization Period – during adolescence and early adulthood (13 to 21 years) we are mainly influenced by our peers and actively seek others who are like us and share our values. Developed personal values therefore influence our attitudes and behaviours as they determine what we consider appropriate, right or wrong.

Values may be considered as long-term objectives that develop and adapt as a result of life experience. Important values influence our decision-making and the way we experience our surroundings. Finegan (2000) suggests a multidimensional approach based on two sets of factors: Humanity Factors such as courtesy, consideration, cooperation, fairness, forgiveness and moral integrity; and Vision Factors such as development, initiative, creativity and openness. An organisation that values both Vision and Humanity factors is likely to be perceived positively, and accordingly, employees are more likely to be affectively committed. However, this effect only occurs when employees attach importance to these values. Job applicants tend to look for companies that share the same values (Anderson, Fornell, & Mazvancheryl, 2004; Barnard, 1968; Chatman, 1989; Schneider, 1987).

The relationship between employee values and job performance was established from as early as 1956 (Pelz) when it was found that employees working in medical research who had a strong emphasis on science-related values showed higher levels of performance. Current research indicates that employees who report a high value congruence with their organisation are generally more satisfied with their job (Amos & Weathington, 2008). High value congruence also leads to organisational commitment and lower intent to quit (Kristof-Brown, Zimmerman, & Johnson, 2005; Meglino, Ravlin, & Adkins, 1989). However, if
 
there is a mismatch between these values, strain occurs and employees are more likely to become stressed (Edwards, 1996). Thus it is necessary for organisations to be able to communicate their values in order to succeed.

Motivation is the desire or willingness to do something and provides underlying reasons for an individual behaving in a particular way. Motivation influences emotional, social and cognitive forces that lead to behaviour. There are two types of motivation: Extrinsic and Intrinsic. Extrinsic motivation refers to factors external to the individual that influence their beliefs, attitudes, decisions and behaviours. In the context of work, extrinsic motivators include salary, job perks/benefits, status and work conditions. Intrinsic motivation, on the other hand, refers to behaviour that is driven by internal rewards or feelings of personal satisfaction unique to an individual. In the context of work, intrinsic motivators include recognition, challenging work, purposeful work, achievement and opportunities for personal growth. In the Professional Profile 2©, values and motivations are considered essential to determine how individuals are likely to behave at work, the type of tasks that will keep him/her driven and satisfied, and the work environment s/he is likely to flourish in.

Research on the relationship between employee motivation and job and organisational success is extensive. There is clear evidence establishing a link between motivation and employee job performance (Alonso & Lewis, 2001; Bright, 2007; Locke & Latham, 2002; Locke, Shaw, Saari, & Latham, 1981); retention (Rainlall, 2004) and work attitudes (Kovach, 1995; Porter, Bigley, & Steers, 2003). With the recruitment and loss of talented employees associated with high financial and productivity costs, identifying motivated employees remain a primary objective of HR practitioners.


2.1.6.	Motivations and values theories used in the Professional Profile 2©
Several established motivation and values theories were used for the conceptual and practical development of the Professional Profile 2©; these included A. Maslow’s (1943) Theory of Human Motivation; Value-based Decision-making model (VBDM) based on E. Spranger’s (1928) values; and S. Schwartz’s (2012) Value Theory.


2.1.6.a	Theory of Human Motivation – Abraham Maslow (1943)
What makes us choose one activity over another? What causes us to act in a particular way and what makes us go to work? Motivation, consciously or unconsciously, triggers action and is considered as the origin of individual behaviour. In 1943, Abraham Maslow theorised that individuals have universal needs at work, which were sources of motivation. Needs can be classified in a pyramid to visualise the order in which they appear in an individual. Maslow argued that satisfaction of needs at lower levels elicit needs at the next level and concluded that humans can only reach the top of the pyramid if lower-level needs were satisfied. Figure 5 depicts Maslow’s theory of motivation and hierarchy of needs.

There are several theories of need-based motivation, with the majority agreeing on three key points:
i.	The existence of needs. Individuals feel basic needs which they seek to satisfy. These needs induce action.
ii.	The existence of objectives. Individuals are motivated to take action in order to achieve a goal.
iii.	Motivation is a personal phenomenon. Individuals do not have the same needs or problems nor solve them in the same way. Therefore both needs and motivation are individual.
 
 

Figure 5. Maslow’s Theory of Motivation and Hierarchy of Needs


2.1.6.b	Value-Based Decision-Making – Eduard Spranger (1928)
In his seminal book Types of Men, Eduard Spranger (1928) proposed six types of personal values that form part of an individual’s thinking and help us to choose between alternatives during the decision making process. Values, he argued, allow us to analyse and refine judgement. Values in the workplace are important as they reduce conflict between organisational members (Courtney, 2001) and effectively lead to the management of organisational knowledge, particularly during periods of restructuring and change (Hall, Guo, & Davis, 2003).

Values are categorised as follows: Theoretical - refers to comprehension of the truth and knowledge that has been attained in a rational, scientific manner. Social - relates to a philanthropic view as represented by a search for social interaction and being aware of the impact of our decisions on other people, groups, and organisations. Political - concerns a power-oriented view in relation to the prestige and power that could arise at the cost of others. Religious - this value aspires to make the world a better place. It is usually based on philosophical and interpretative views. Aesthetic - views the world through an artistic, interpretative view and seeks to find form and harmony in a given setting. Economic - refers to the search for usability and material goods. It arises from a functional, practical view.


2.1.6.c	Value Theory – Shalom Schwartz (2012)
According to Shalom Schwartz (2012), values are concepts or beliefs that relate to a purpose or desirable behaviour. They transcend specific situations and reflect motivations designed to achieve specific objectives, such as safety, performance and autonomy. Values guide our choices and allow the evaluation of behaviour towards people or events. Schwartz conducted extensive research and identified 10 cross cultural value types. Power relates to the need for a prestigious social status and is related to values of authority, wealth, social power, public image and social recognition. Achievement is characterised by personal success and is related to values of ambition, orientation towards success, competence, influence and self-respect. Stimulation is the desire for variety in order to maintain an optimum level of activity and is related to values of audacity, an exciting and varied life. Hedonism is the need for pleasure and satisfaction and is related to values of pleasure, love life, having fun. Universalism is the understanding, tolerance and protection of the welfare of the whole world and nature. Related values include equality, a world at peace, tolerance and social justice. Benevolence is the maintenance of the welfare of people with whom one is in personal contact and is related to values of helpfulness, loyalty, honesty and responsibility. Tradition is commitment and approval of the customs and ideas advocated by traditional culture or religion. Related values included respect for tradition, humility, spiritual life and moderation. Conformity moderates actions, trends and desires that upset, hurt others or violate
 
social norms. The emphasis here is on self-control in the interactions of everyday life with relatives. Related values are obedience, politeness, self-discipline, and honouring parents and elders. Security is characterized by the pursuit of harmony and stability within society, relationships and the self. Some values of this type are essentially individual interests (such as being healthy) and others are community interests (such as national security). Related values relate to maintaining social order, family safety, and cleanliness. Self-Direction is the independence of thought and action, characterised by choosing, creating and exploring. Related values are creativity, freedom and independence.

2.2.	Development of Professional Profile 2©
The Professional Profile 2© was developed from a thorough multi-stage process. The precursor to the questionnaire was first developed in 2002 – a time in which the structure of work was more defined and targeted work competencies more predictable. However, work in the twenty-first century is characterised by more flexible ways of working, cross-cultural team work, technological innovation and more complex performance indicators. Questionnaire items and dimensions were therefore revamped in order to ensure; a) items reflected contemporary ways of working; b) effective prevention of faking and social bias; c) practitioners had a tool that improved the accuracy of assessment decisions; and d) individuals were clearly guided when assessing fit with desired roles and careers.


2.2.1.	Dimensions and Item Construction
The Professional Profile 2© was developed from the bank of items for the original professional profile questionnaire. The original questionnaire underwent review that resulted from client interviews, training workshop feedback forms and advertised job competences. The original questionnaire assessed 12 dimensions from 98 questions, presented as paired statements (the equivalent of 196 items). Questions were divided into two sections: Part A comprised of 74 paired statements in which respondents chose the statement that best reflected them. Part B consisted of 24 paired adjectives or descriptors that required the respondent to choose a single response from the pair. A practitioner-based review ensured dimensions identified were clearly defined and items targeting each dimension accurately reflected modern and business-relevant behaviours. There were three primary recommendations for changes:

i.	Greater differentiation between personality and motivation dimensions
ii.	Inclusion of dimensions that target organisational and team fit
iii.	Clearer item structure and wording
This practitioner review process was followed by item and dimension review by a team of ten (10) experts comprised of Human Resource managers, recruitment consultants and psychologists. Items were grouped into themes and categorised into global dimensions generated from literature reviews of practitioner and academic research on personality, motivation, values and attitudes in the workplace. This process produced two additional dimensions; free-thinking/ rule following and familiarity seeking/ novelty seeking.

New and amended items for the Professional Profile 2© were written simultaneously in simple French and English to promote ease of response as well as facilitate candidates’ mental connection and understanding with statements. As the questionnaire aimed to capture work-behaviours in multiple contexts, items were checked for cultural bias, clarity, offensiveness and language accuracy. Two examples of changed items resulting from this process are depicted in Table 2 below.


Table 2

Examples of changed items resulting from expert review and practitioner feedback

Dimension	Original Item	Professional Profile 2©

Team Work/ Autonomy	
I find it stimulating to work in a team	
I prefer working closely with others
Flexibility/ Firmness	I try to avoid power struggles	I try to find my way out of disagreements
 
2.2.2.	Ipsative Response Format
Response options for the Items on the Professional Profile 2© are written in binary forced-choice format. In contrast to Likert-scales, respondents choose between descriptors according to the extent to which the descriptor captures their preference. Ipsative formats have been found to reduce response bias (Chan, 2003; Saville & Willson, 1991; ten Holt, van Duijn, & Boomsma, 2010). The fact that respondents cannot endorse all items eliminates acquiescence (the tendency to agree with all items on a questionnaire), reduces susceptibility to impression management and means that it is generally more difficult to fake (Brown & Maydeu-Olivares, 2011a). Ipsative formats make it impossible to provide the same response on all items, thereby resulting in greater differentiation of scores within a profile. Ipsative scales are also beneficial because there is no need to include negatively-worded items in order to balance dimensions. Rather, mirrored items facilitate measurement of behaviours for different traits along the same domains. In Likert scales, it is necessary to include negatively-worded items in order to balance responses and monitor acquiescence (Brown & Maydeu-Olivares, 2013; Edwards & Kenney, 1946). With ipsative formats, all items are worded positively yet both positive and negative behavioural indicators are captured.

Despite such advantages, ipsative questionnaires are underutilised in work-based assessments due to challenges for traditional normative scoring methodology, construct validation and interpretation (Cornwell & Dunlap, 1994; Loo, 1999; Meade, 2004).

In an ipsative test, the respondent must make a comparative judgement between two or more items to give an answer. Conversely, in a normative test, judgement is viewed in absolute terms for each evaluated personality trait and preferences are not compared between different traits. Classic test theory (CTT) scoring does not reflect the psychological process of comparison, as it ignores the fact that respondents indicate a preference not an absolute judgement. As a consequence, ipsative test scores that are generated from CTT scoring approaches might be distorted. The potential solution therefore is to keep the ipsative format but change the way the scores are calculated, in order to reflect realistic comparative judgements.


2.2.3.	Thurstonian Item Response Theory (IRT) and Ipsative Responses
To solve the problem of ipsative scoring, researchers relied on the work of Thurstone (1927, 1931) who proposed the law of comparative judgements, which assigns the result of a comparative judgment to the "relative utility" of objects compared for each individual. Applied to personality assessment, the notion of utility refers to the degree to which there is a match between the item and the personality trait of the respondent. Therefore, an individual will choose the item that corresponds to them the most.

Despite its potential benefits, Thurstonian models were previously not utilised in ipsative test as (Brown, 2010):
i.	There was no efficient estimation method available
ii.	Computer technology was not powerful enough to facilitate analysis
iii.	Previous statistical programmes did not facilitate the calculation of scores because the variance of error for each question is 0

Recent changes were made to the Thurstonian second order model to produce the Thurstonian IRT model (Brown & Maydeu-Olivares, 2011, 2013). This model bypasses the notion of latent utility, and directly connects the choice made by the respondents to the latent personality traits measured by the test. This change simplifies the model and allows for it to be applied to much longer questionnaires.

Brown & Maydeu-Olivares (2011, 2013) showed that when applied to an ipsative questionnaire with blocks of four items, this method allows not only to correct the distortion of the scores, but also to recover the levels of "absolute" personality traits from responses based on comparative judgements. The scores provided were similar to those of a normative test.
The nature of this scoring procedure, based on the Maximum a posteriori estimator (MAP), was completely different from that of the CTT, which "adds" the dimension points for each selected item. MAP is an estimation procedure which has the goal to find a parameter value theta (the value on the latent trait
 
continuum) that best explains the data (the pattern of binary outcome responses) by assuming a joint distribution on both, the data and the theta, with theta governing the distribution on the data (i.e. the posterior distribution). More simply, with the Maximum a posteriori estimator, the overall response pattern of the test is taken into account for the calculation of the score. By comparing the choice and preference indicated by the respondent while taking into account the specific characteristics of the items, the method is able to deduce (or recover) their absolute level of personality trait for each dimension.

For the Professional Profile 2©, Thurstonian IRT was used to revise, rewrite and validate both items and dimensions. Figure 6 displays the model of Thurstonian IRT used to analyse items and dimensions on the questionnaire.




Figure 6. Model of Thurstonian IRT analyses utilised in the development of the Professional Profile 2©


2.2.3. a Item facility
Item facility refers to an item’s ability to differentiate between respondents and identify floor or ceiling effects. An item has floor or ceiling effects when the probability of endorsing a response option is much lower or greater than the probability of endorsing the alternate option. As items for the Professional Profile 2© were binary, item facility was examined from the proportion of responses for each option. The more equivalent the proportion the more balanced the item. For the Professional Profile 2©, items with floor or ceiling effects were discarded or modified. The facility of changed or new items was then examined with subsequent pre-tests.


2.2.3. b Item difficulty & discrimination
Item difficulty is the first parameter of an IRT model. It is defined as the location on the ability continuum, where the probability of endorsing the item is equal to 50% of the participants. In a personality assessment, the difficulty parameter reflects the level of trait needed for participants to have a 50% chance of choosing the item. An item is said to be “easy” when the probability that 50% of individuals who endorsed it only have a low level on the trait measured by the item (e.g. an “easy” item measuring Extraversion might be chosen by 50% of participants having only a low level of trait for Extraversion). On the contrary, an item is said to be “difficult”, when the probability that 50% of individuals who endorsed it have a high level of trait (e.g. a “difficult” item measuring Extraversion might reach the probability of being chosen by 50% of the participants only if they have a high level of trait for Extraversion).

Item difficulty can be visualised on the Item characteristic curves (ICC). The ICC is an S-shaped curve that represents the relationship between ability levels of the latent trait and the probability of endorsing an item. It is obtained by modelling the probability of endorsing the items using logistic model (see figure 7).
 
 


Figure 7. Item characteristic curves of an easy, moderate and difficult item from an ability test; the probability that 50% of the participants chose the correct answer can be observed when the ability level is -1.3 for the easy item, 0 for the moderately difficult item and 1.4 for the difficult item (the ability level is represented as a Z score, 0 being the mean).


Item discrimination is the second parameter of the IRT model and refers to the shape of the curve. It is a measure of an item’s ability to distinguish between respondents on the target construct. A high discrimination parameter value reflects a high ability to differentiate between participants; the probability of choosing the correct answer (or endorsing the item for a personality assessment) increases more rapidly as the ability (or level of trait) increases.

As the Thurstonian IRT model is conceptualised from a factor analysis perspective, item difficulty is referred to as "threshold" instead of "difficulty” and item discrimination parameter as “factor loadings”. Factor loadings and threshold were examined for all Items. Items that were too difficult, too easy or did not discriminate enough were modified or removed.


	c Item and Dimension Reliability
Item reliability is the degree to which items on a questionnaire have no error. The reliability of an item is determined by the internal consistency of the item and its relationship with other items that measure the same dimension. Internal reliability was measured using composite reliability generated from item factor loadings. Composite reliability ranges from 0 to 1 with a recommended coefficient of 0.7. For the Professional Profile 2©, items with the smallest factor loadings were changed or deleted. Modified questionnaires were then trialled with successive pre-tests.


	Pre-tests and Pilots
Pre-tests were conducted with a range of male and female professionals across the globe to ensure items were accurate, relevant, clear, concise, culture-free, bias-free and reflected targeted work behaviours. After 2 French and 2 English pre-tests, the final questionnaire was piloted with 345 English-speaking professionals from the United States and 209 French-speaking professionals from France. Pre-test samples comprised of native French and English speakers from different continents in order to establish the questionnaire as an international measure of work personality and motivation. Unlike other personality
 
measures, the Professional Profile 2© was developed to ensure items were culturally equivalent from the early stages of development, rather than focus on cultural adaption after the questionnaire’s validity has been established within a single context – the typical practice of test developers. Tables 4 and 5 detail sample characteristics of all pre-tests and pilots conducted for French and English working-age samples.

Composite reliabilities for each dimension were calculated from generated item factor loadings. Dimensions were flagged if reliabilities were less than 0.55. Dimensions that did not converge were inspected for poorly-performing items which were then reviewed, modified or changed then subjected to another round of pre-test. The overall scale composite reliability for the Pilot study was .998 (France) and .990 (USA). Composite reliability for personality dimensions ranged from .663 – 1.00 (France) and .768
-1.00 (USA), whilst reliability for motivation dimensions ranged from .832 – 1.00 (France) and .683 - .892 (USA). As shown in Table 3, dimensions not emerging as valid did not produce item loadings therefore reliabilities were not calculated. Detailed information on the validity of each domain for each pre-test can be seen in Chapter 6- Validity.

2.2.5	IRT scoring: Case study
To our knowledge, the Thurstonian IRT model has been previously used only once on an ipsative personality assessment (Brown & Maydeu-Olivares, 2011, 2013). To investigate the benefits of using the scoring procedures based on the Thurstonian IRT model with the maximum a posteriori estimator instead of the classical test theory based scoring, we performed a case study on the final French and English version of the Professional Profile 2© questionnaire. The objective was to compare the impact of the two ways of calculating scores (IRT versus classical) on the face validity of the questionnaire.


2.2.5.a	Participants & Methods
Thirteen participants, 8 French-speaking (5 females) and 5 English-speaking (1 female) took the Professional Profile 2© online.

They were given 3 different reports including personalised comments for each personality trait measured. One report was based on the IRT score, a second one was based on CTT scores. A third report based on a random score was added as a baseline condition in order to control for acquiescence bias (control condition). The random report was different for English speaking versus French speaking individuals, but identical for participants from the same country. It was always presented in the second position, while the position of the IRT or CTT score based report (place 1 versus 3) was counterbalanced between participants.
Task 1: For each report, participants were asked to rate on a scale from 0 (not agree at all) to 10 (completely agree) if they agreed with the comments on each dimension.

Task 2: Then, for each dimension, they were asked to choose the comments which described them best. Sometimes, IRT, CTT or Random based report converged to the same score for one dimension and therefore reports displayed the same personalised comment for one dimension. In that particular case, participants were asked to select both comments.


2.2.5.b	Analyses
Task 1: The median and mode of agreement for each dimension were computed for the IRT, classical and random report (cf. see Figure 8 and Table 6).

Task 2: For each type of report, the frequency and percentage of the preferred comments were calculated for each dimension and each participant. When the preferred comments based on IRT or classical score were identical, the answers of the participant were considered for both of them. However, when the comments were identical across IRT or CTT and the random report, the score was attribute only to the experimental condition and not to the random report.


2.2.5.c	Results
Task 1. Results show that the median score on the satisfaction scale for each comment ranges from 7 to 9 (average 8) for the report based on the CTT scoring method, from 5 to 8 (average 7) for the report based on the IRT scoring method, and from 2 to 8 (average 6) on the random report. Whilst the mode score ranges from 7 to 9 (average 8) for the report based on the CTT scoring method, from 0 to 9 (average 8) for the report based on the IRT scoring method, and from 2 to 8 (average 6.5) on the random report. We also considered the number of times participants rated a score below 5 on the 10 point scale, to determine how many times they disagreed with the report comments. Disagreement scores decreased from the random report (17.6%), then IRT report (15.9%) and then to the CTT report (9.3%).

It was the report based on the CTT scoring methods that raised the best agreement scores and the least disagreement scores (see Figure 8 and Table 6).
 
 
Figure 8. Median score (out of 10) of satisfaction score for the comments of each dimension and each type of report. Report based on CTT score (solid red line, CTT); report based on IRT score (black dashed line, IRT), report based on random score (grey dotted line, random).

Results of this study indicate a preference for reports based on CTT scoring over IRT scoring. Moreover, participants were more satisfied by the report based on CTT scoring compared to the IRT scoring methods. Even if the median scores on the agreement scale are close for both IRT and CTT report, analyses show that IRT scoring led to a higher amount of scores below 5 compared to the CTT report, indicating that from the participants’ perspective the first method generates more inaccurate results than the second one.

Finding that CTT scoring of an ipsative test was more accurate than IRT scoring was surprising, as literature emphasised the issue of distorted results when using a CTT method for the scoring of an ipsative test. However, practitioners already highlighted with empirical evidences that forced-choice and normative questionnaire might produce comparable results, especially when a large number of dimensions are being measured (Baron, 1996; Karpatschof & Elkjaeer, 2000). Each Professional Profile 2© dimension indeed includes 2 opposing factors, which means that the questionnaire assesses 28 constructs, and therefore might fall into the category of a questionnaire measuring a sufficient number of dimensions to retrieve people’s absolute level of trait.

Other factors may explain the superiority of CTT scoring methods for the Professional Profile 2© questionnaire, such as the addition of the Audit Scale, which contains only pairs of items measuring the same traits. The 28 items composing the Audit Scale directly assess the two opposing factors of the 14 dimensions of the questionnaire, and may correct the distortion caused by the forced-choice format measuring two different dimensions. More research is needed to find a scoring methods that works empirically and is coherent from a statistical perspective. In the meantime, as suggested by empirical evidence, the CTT scoring methods will be used in the Professional Profile 2©.
 
3.	Scoring
A raw score is the total number of responses endorsed by a candidate for each trait. In the context of work-based assessment, raw scores mean little as they do not provide effective or appropriate comparisons between candidates across a specific trait. Norms enable HR practitioners, managers and recruiters to evaluate an individual’s score relative to the score of other delegates who completed the same assessment.


3.1.	The relationship between raw scores and norms
The best norm group is one that is most representative of the characteristics of the individuals taking a test. Applied assessment assumes personality traits, motivations, values, attitudes and perceptions are distributed along a normal probability or bell-shaped curve. Therefore, norm groups effectively identify individual scores across this distribution. In any given population, levels of a target attribute may be found. Therefore, for a given attribute or trait, the normal curve represents the norm or average performance of a population and the scores that are above and below the average for that population (Kaplan & Saccuzzo, 2008). For norms to be meaningful, the sample used must reflect the intended population of test takers. If a test is developed for engineers, the norm sample must consist of engineers in the population. Similarly if an assessment was developed for nurses, it is not appropriate for use in the selection of chemists. Norm groups can range in size from a few hundred to a hundred thousand. However the larger the norm group sample size the closer the approximation to a normal population distribution. Figure 9 shows the normal bell curve.



Figure 9. Normal Bell-shaped curve showing the distribution of percentages, standard deviations and standard ten (Sten)


Key to the creation of norms is the conversion of raw scores into derived scores. This process provides a system of common metrics that readily facilitate the interpretation of test scores. For the Professional Profile 2© the forms of score standardization used were: 1) Mean; 2) Median; 3) Standard deviation; 4) Standard score; 5) Standard ten (sten); and 6) Standard error of measurement. Table 8 describes the various types of derived scores used in the validation of the Professional Profile 2©.
 
Table 8

3.2.	Transforming raw scores to standardised scores
For the 112 items on the Professional Profile 2© the minimum and maximum overall score for each trait are
-8 and 8. The conversion of raw scores to standardised scores is achieved using a four-step process.


Step 1	Ipsative scores are calculated for each scale by summing all forced-choice responses for each trait. For each response, 1 is assigned to the response that is endorsed and 0 for the response that is not endorsed.
Step 2	Scores are repositioned on a scale from 0 to 10 by using the following formula:


raw score − (min)
𝑠𝑐𝑜𝑟𝑒 = ( (max) − (min) ) ∗ 10

Whereby max refers to the maximum possible score of each scale and min refers to the minimum possible score of each scale.
Step 3	To enable the comparisons between individuals, scores for each scale are standardized using the mean and standard deviation across respondents. This process produces a z score using the formula:

𝑧 = (𝑠𝑐𝑜𝑟𝑒 − (µ/𝜎)

Step 4	
Standardised scores are then transformed into sten scores from 1 to 10 using the formula:
𝑠𝑡𝑒𝑛 = 𝑧 ∗ 2 + 5.5





The importance of standardized scores can be illustrated with the following example:

For 9 out of 10 times, a test-taker chooses items describing Extraversion over items describing other dimensions (i.e. in 90 % of all cases). However on 6 out of 10 times, the test-taker chooses items corresponding to the dimension of Rule-Following. If the test-taker is compared to a group with a high mean for Extraversion, the 90 % may not be extraordinary. However, if the comparison group is characterised by a very low mean score on Rule-Following, the 60 % is meaningful. Thus a score has to be regarded in relation to the population in order for it to be interpreted appropriately.


3.3.	Standard error of measurement
No test score is perfect. Therefore we need an estimate of the possible amount of error present within a score. The amount of error associated with a score is known as the standard error of measurement (SEM). SEM helps us to understand what is a candidate’s true score and the extent to which it differs from the obtained score (Harvill, 1991). In an ideal world, the SEM would be calculated by having an individual take an assessment on many occasions over a long period of time. The extent to which there was variation across each score obtained by the candidate would be classified as the standard error of measurement.
 
However, as it is not feasible to assess candidates multiple times, the SEM must be calculated using a different approach.

The SEM is an estimate that is added to or subtracted from a candidate’s score in order to establish a level of confidence about a score. From this confidence band, we are able to determine the extent to which a score may vary. Standard error of measurement is related to reliability. Therefore as reliability increases, SEM decreases. For a series of scores, approximately 68% of the time, an observed score will lie within +1.0 and -1.0 SEM of the true score. On 95% of occasions, the observed score will be between +1.96 and -1.96 SEM of the true score. Whilst 99% of the time, the observed score will lie within +2.58 and -2.58 SEM of the true score. The 95% confidence band of a score is calculated using the formula:

𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑓𝑖𝑑𝑒𝑛𝑐𝑒 𝐵𝑎𝑛𝑑 = 𝑠𝑐𝑜𝑟𝑒 ± 1.96 × 𝑆𝐸𝑀

Therefore for the Professional Profile 2©, a dimension with a score of 5 and SEM of 0.2, has a confidence band of 4.608 - 5.392. This means we can be 95% confident that an individual’s true score lies between this range.


3.4.	Choosing an appropriate norm group
Norms give meaning to an individual’s score. The interpretation of the same score may therefore differ depending on whether a score was obtained by a graduate, administrator or senior manager. For accurate interpretation, it is necessary to compare an individual’s score with a group that is most similar to him/her. To choose the appropriate norm, first identify the country of the candidate. Then from that country identify the age, gender, educational level, number of years work experience, social professional status, industry or occupational group most appropriate for the candidate.

Alternatively it is possible to create your own internal norms, based on the candidates that have already been assessed.


3.5.	Creating in-house norms
It is possible to generate internal organisational norms if there is a sample of 50 or more individuals who have worked in the specified role, department or sector. In-house norms ensure that comparisons between individuals and the reference group reflect the target role, tasks and work environment as closely as possible. In-house norms are particularly valuable in situations where standard norms are not available, roles are new, the organisation has a unique client-base or business function and in cases where the applicant pool for a role is significantly higher or lower than expected.

To create in-house norms, first calculate the mean and standard deviation for each dimension on the Professional Profile 2 © and score the results based on the step by step guide outlined in section 3.2. Organisations will then be able to compare individual applicants or employees to a local normal group.


3.6.	Link between scores and Report comments
Personalised comments on Professional Profile 2© reports reflect the scoring process. For each dimension, an individual receives a score of 0 to 10 (as demonstrated in Step 2). Comments provide an extensive interpretation of all dimensions of the assessments and are chosen from six levels according to the score of the candidate. They describe individual preferences and orientation toward traits with higher scores accompanied by stronger descriptors. For example, a candidate with a mean score on a trait may be classified as ‘somewhat’ in relation to behaviours associated with the trait, whilst someone with a high score is described as having a ‘strong preference for’ that trait. Candidates with low scores on dominant traits are described in positive terms in relation to the opposing trait in order to avoid the use of negative terminology in reports.
 
3.7.	Scoring the Profile Fit Percentages
The report presents profile fit percentages computed for an individual’s General Profile (e.g. reflective, cooperative, conscientious, dynamic, devoted), orientation towards desired organisational potentials (e.g. decision-making, conflict resolution, team building, stress management, result orientation) and potential fit with 58 professional careers (e.g. legal, tourism, administrative, business-oriented). Percentages reflect the degree of correspondence between a candidate’s scores on relevant dimensions and the scores of the target profile. A perfect match corresponds to a score of 100%. These sections are additional components that can be added to standard Professional Profile 2© reports.

The percentages should be interpreted in relative terms. This means that the order of the targeted profiles is more important than their absolute score. We consider that beyond a threshold for a specific dimension, such as extraversion, a score equal to or greater than this threshold corresponds to an optimal level of approximation for the Profile Fit (for example, a score of 7, 8, 9 or 10 on extraversion will be equivalent and considered as optimal for a threshold set at 7). The logic remains the same for opposing dimensions. A score equal to or lower than the threshold corresponds to an optimal level of approximation (e.g. a score of 0, 1, 2 or 3 to a threshold of 3).

This method of calculation thus takes into account only the dimensions for which the score for a participant does not match the defined threshold. Calculations are also weighted according to the strength of their threshold. A threshold set at 9 or 10 for example will have more weight than a threshold of 6 or 7.

1.	Introduction

•	What are spatial abilities?
Spatial abilities refer to processes that allow individuals to identify, recognize and differentiate objects by their shape, size or orientation. It is thanks to them that individuals are able to differentiate a soccer ball from a golf ball, a cup from a bowl, or to realize that an image is upside down.
Spatial abilities intervene both in direct perception (when objects are present) and in mental imagery (when individuals create a mental representation of an object that is not physically present) (Mellet, Petit, Mazoyer, Denis, Tzourio, 1999). Through mental imagery, individuals are able to visualize the concepts of space, volume, distance, rotation and reflection. They are able to combine conceptual measures thanks to their projective skills (Bishop, 1978).
•	Skills related to spatial abilities
Individuals with high spatial abilities tend to generate abstract and schematic mental images (Kozhevnikov, Kosslyn, & Shephard, 2005). They proceed analytically, piece by piece, which allows them to visualize the different parts of the structure of an object explicitly. Their mental images are therefore more flexible and transformable (Kozehvnikov, Hegarty & Mayer, 2002).
Thus, people with high spatial skills are more comfortable than others to read diagrams (Höffler, 2010), or to understand and interpret a graph (Kozhevnikov, Motes and Hegarty, 2007). Spatial skills have also been shown to be positively correlated with mathematical problem solving performance (Van Garderen, 2006): spatial thinking appears to be an aid in isolating the important component of a problem (Bodner and Macmillen 1986). This effect is amplified by the judicious use of schemata during reflection (Hegarty and Kozhevnikov, 1999), while individuals with low spatial skills are more likely to draw incorrect diagrams (Pribyl and Bodner 1987). In addition, spatial skills facilitate the use of data analysis and design software (Hamlon Boersma and Sorby 2006; Norman 1994), as well as understanding molecular geometry for chemistry students (Carter, Larussa and Bodner, 1987). They can also be used in a more abstract way, such as creating and manipulating models to test scientific hypotheses, or when a theory originally presented as a text or an equation is transformed into a graph or schema (Trickett & Trafton, 2007).
These skills are very important for the success and completion for STEM (science, technology, engineering, mathematics) studies and careers. Longitudinal studies have shown that spatial skills in adolescence can predict success in STEM domains (Shea, Lubinski and Benbow, 2001, Wai, Lubinski and Benbow, 2009, Webb, Lubinski and Benbow, 2007). Indeed, Wai et al. (2009), showed that participants with the highest STEM diplomas had the highest spatial abilities 11 years earlier.
•	The importance of using a specialized test
Spatial abilities are often neglected in the educational system, entrance examinations, or the selection of candidates. At present, individuals are primarily assessed based on verbal and numerical skills. As a result, individuals with excellent spatial abilities, but lower verbal or numerical abilities are not detected by current measures (Webb, et al., 2007). Humphreys, Lubinski and Yao (1993) report that thereby, engineering and physical sciences are missing many talents each year during the admissions.
It is for these reasons that Central Testing has created REASONING-SPATIAL, providing additional information needed to assess potential candidates.

Questionnaire : 8 multiple choice questions
Time : 16 minutes maximum (It can be completed faster)
Time per question: 2 minutes maximum
Administration : online

Nevertheless, as Sorby (2009) points out, spatial skills are very malleable and sensitive to training, so regular practice can dispel these differences.

•	The indicated central tendency measures (mean and median) are the mathematical mean of all observed scores. They give us an estimate of the difficulty and adaptation of the instrument to the population. Mathematically, they indicate at which level of the scale most scores are found.
•	The standard deviation of the scale is the mathematical mean of all the standard deviations of individuals. It is an indicator of dispersion of scores against the mean. A high standard deviation is an indicator of high dispersion, while a low standard deviation is an indicator of low dispersion.
•	The standard error of the mean gives us an estimate of the error variance that is likely to be found in the population. The lower the error compared to the mean, the more accurate the test is to evaluate this characteristic.



2.3.	Reliability

Reliability measures the extent to which an assessment is free from error and the degree to which items on a scale measure the same construct.
A primary concern, when developing and using assessments, is the extent to which random measurement errors influence test performance. If a test is unreliable, it is impossible for it to be valid, as accuracy of results (validity) depends on the consistency (reliability) with which the same results are produced. Reliability enables test users to be confident that the results accurately reflect the test taker’s attitude, aptitude or behavior in the workplace.
Internal reliability measures the extent to which items on a scale are internally consistent and all measure the same construct. Internal reliability is measured by calculating Cronbach’s Alpha (α) for each factor,
 
which computes the average of all possible inter-item correlations without relying on a single indicator of reliability, which may contain large amounts of error.


Factor	Cronbach’s alpha
STEM (n=72)	0.74
Non-STEM (n=72)	0.30
Overall	0.57

The results indicate a difference in reliability between the STEM and non-STEM groups. The most probable interpretation is that SPATIAL-REASONING is a specific tool that is well suited for evaluating a STEM population but is less suitable for evaluating a non-STEM population.


2.4.	Validity

Validity is the extent to which an assessment measures that which it is supposed to measure. This is a subjective judgment made on the basis of experience and empirical indicators. The validity of this assessment was assessed using two methods:


•	Construct Validity: If an assessment has construct validity, it implies that it measures the domain that it seeks to measure. In order to establish construct validity, this domain needs to be carefully defined, then items that are relevant to that domain must be carefully chosen and incorporated, and finally their relevance must be evaluated.
The items on this assessment were chosen by a team of psychologists, senior corporate executives, university academicians, statisticians, and other relevant personnel after numerous consultations. These items are continuously re-evaluated and updated in relation to relevant statistical data.
•	Consequential Validity : DIF – Differential Item Functioning
An item functions differently, or displays differential item functioning (DIF), if respondents from different groups have different probabilities of endorsing the item, despite having the same level of the trait. The data analysis showed no DIF for gender nor study level.

1.	Introduction
Emotional intelligence can be defined as the ability to understand one's own, as well as other people's emotions, and to manage them in an appropriate way. Emotional intelligence is essential for personal fulfilment and professional success. High levels of emotional intelligence are beneficial to an individual's personal and professional life and are fundamental for serving as a contributing member of an organisation.

EMOTION is a tool designed to assess a person's level of emotional intelligence. This includes the ability to perceive, understand, and manage one’s own emotions, as well as those of others. The corporate version of the assessment is designed to effectively meet the demands of recruitment.
The origins of emotional intelligence can be traced back to 1990, with the work of American researchers Peter Salovey and John Mayer. They gathered the expressions of many behavioural and cognitive abilities that are strongly intertwined, such as:
•	The ability to recognise one’s emotions and to understand them
•	The ability to manage one’s emotions, especially those that have a depressive or
disruptive effect
•	The ability to use one’s emotions to serve a goal
•	The ability to identify the emotions of others and to share one's own emotions
•	The ability to manage other people's emotions

General intelligence is important for problem solving; however, it is most beneficial to a person's professional and personal life when coupled with emotional intelligence. A certain level of emotional intelligence is crucial during instances when people must work together towards a shared goal, particularly in the corporate world. Furthermore, emotional intelligence is necessary for a person's efficacious adaptation to new situations.
Salovey and Mayer (1990) described emotional intelligence as “a form of social intelligence that involves the ability to monitor one’s own and others' feelings and emotions, to discriminate among them, and to use this information to guide one’s thinking and action". People with a high emotional intelligence score react better to social changes, can perceive things accurately, can understand other people and asses their emotions, and are able to build supportive social networks. A high level of emotional intelligence also provides good judgment when it comes to selecting work that is emotionally rewarding. In this way, emotionally intelligent individuals avoid procrastination, self-doubt, and low levels of achievement, and strive to reach a balance between their personal and professional life, to be self-motivated, and to manage their goals well.
 
1.1	Main Areas of Application
Applications of EMOTION can vary, as it is suitable for individuals, teams, and organisational assessment. It can be applied to employee recruitment, selection, and development processes, as well as for team and individual development, and for culture and employee commitment audits.
After completing EMOTION a report is provided detailing the results of the assessment, which provides a normative emotional intelligence score with a graphical representation of the person’s emotional intelligence. The test-taker is also provided with a summary of his/her emotional profile and the areas that he/she can work on to develop higher emotional intelligence. The report also includes a social desirability indicator to assess the validity of the candidate’s responses.

1.2	Questionnaire Structure
EMOTION consists of 15 factors which all measure a different aspect of emotional intelligence. There is an additional factor that serves as a social desirability scale. The final version of the questionnaire is comprised of 96 questions, with each factor containing 6 items. All items are measured on a 4-point Likert scale, where respondents need to specify their level of agreement or disagreement with each item (1 = This does not describe me at all, 2 = This describes me a little, 3 = This describes me fairly well, 4 = This describes me very well). This is the most common form of scoring in normative questionnaires and covers the full range of possible responses.


1.3	Mode of Administration, Languages and Special Requirements
EMOTION is administered online and is compatible with all computer systems including Windows, MAC-OS, and Linux. The assessment can be administered remotely and without supervision. Reports are solely generated online; therefore, if the test has been administered on paper, the administrator must manually enter candidates’ responses into the Central Test client platform to generate a report.
The questionnaire is available in English. For more information on other language availability, please contact the Central Test Consulting Service at consulting@centraltest.com.
EMOTION was designed to be used by individuals from diverse backgrounds and can be adapted for those with special needs. It is possible to adjust visual screen specifications, such as increasing the font size and screen display. Test instructions are accessible throughout the duration of the assessment for those who require it. Questions can be revisited and changed as desired. For candidates who are blind or have severe visual impairments, items can be read aloud by a trained administrator.
 
The average time for completion is 15 minutes; however, EMOTION does not have a time limit and therefore candidates who need more time to answer the questions can do so freely. Please contact the test publisher for guidance on administering the questionnaire to individuals or groups with special requirements.

1.4	Social Desirability
People are sociable by nature and so are inclined to seek social acceptance. Social desirability is the tendency to present oneself in a manner that will be viewed favourably by others. An individual’s need to present themselves in a favourable light negatively impacts test results (Dodou & de Winter, 2014); therefore it is of utmost importance to control for social desirability in respondents. This is especially true for recruitment purposes, as a candidate will be highly tempted to answer in a way that will impress the recruiter.
In order to control for social desirability, a social desirability scale was constructed for EMOTION. The questions pertaining to the social desirability scale were similar to the other test items in terms of vocabulary and subject; this was done to ensure they were not easily detected. The scale is composed of 6 items, the same as in all other factors.
1.5	Test-taker Requirements and Recommendations
EMOTION was designed for members of the general population over 18 years of age that are currently working or looking for work. It is recommended that candidates receive notification of the objective of the assessment, the conditions it will be administered in, how data will be handled and stored, and how the feedback will be delivered. Potential candidates should also be asked beforehand whether they have any special needs or requirements.
1.6	Administrator Qualifications and Recommendations
In order to administer EMOTION, practitioners must complete a standardised training course and/or possess accredited testing qualifications.
The aim of the training is to ensure that the test user knows how to:
-	Administer the assessment in standardised conditions
-	Interpret scores and norms
-	Generate written and verbal feedback from the results
-	Match the results to a range of organisational needs
Prior to any administration, it is recommended that administrators take EMOTION
themselves. This will enable them to become familiar with it.
Test administration must be in accordance with the organisation’s code of practice and legal regulations on the use of psychometric assessments for employment purposes. It is recommended that international and country laws, such as data protection, discrimination, race and ethnicity relations etc., are taken into account prior to administration. This is to help ensure that the test administration is objective and fair.
Additionally, prior to use, administrators must be familiar with, and adhere to, Central Test’s conditions of use, Code of Ethics, and Quality Charter. Administrators must ensure that:
•	The appropriate test(s) has been chosen to meet the organisational needs
•	The candidate is informed about the assessment and its purpose prior to administration
•	The candidate has given consent prior to administration
 
•	Appropriate norms are used to interpret results
•	The candidate is given feedback on the results
•	The results and personal information will be deleted on request by the candidate
•	The candidate has given consent prior to personal data or results being transferred to third parties
•	Only qualified personnel administer assessments and have access to the data
•	Assessment materials and reports are stored securely
For more information about Central Test’s Code of Ethics, refer to Central Test’s Quality
Charter.
1.6.1	Testing Conditions
EMOTION should be administered in a quiet, well-lit, and ventilated environment, so as to ensure that the candidate is undistracted and has a pleasant experience, thereby increasing the probability that their responses are accurate. Candidates should be allowed sufficient time to take the assessment and should thus be informed about the length of the questionnaire prior to administration. It is best to refer to the assessment as a questionnaire and not a test to reduce candidates’ anxiety levels.
Prior to testing, administrators should ensure that all relevant candidate background information has been collected and verify that the correct candidate is taking the assessment. Candidates receive an email invitation to take the questionnaire, with an embedded link and login details. All web links and login details are unique to a candidate’s account. Expiration dates can be set in order to ensure candidates complete the questionnaire within a specific time-frame.
EMOTION does not have a time restriction; however, time is monitored to allow administrators to know whether a candidate has taken an overly long or short time to complete the questionnaire. Candidates are encouraged to answer questions quickly and spontaneously, in order to provide more accurate answers.
1.7	Technical and Security Issues
The following points serve as guidelines for securely administering EMOTION. These are useful for ensuring that EMOTION is administered in an objective and fair manner. Security specifications are also important for protecting the candidate’s information, both during administration and after the completion of the assessment.
1.7.1	Respecting the Ethical Charter
As a publisher of online assessment tools, Central Test respects the legal conventions of French labour law and the regulatory provisions resulting from the law of 6 January 1978 pertaining to information technology, files and freedoms: Declaration N°1834067.
Test results and personal data cannot be shared with third parties without explicit consent from the candidate. Candidates have the right to access their results and the assessor must grant them access when requested. Furthermore, if the candidate requests it, all data must be permanently deleted; to do so the candidate must contact either the organisation or Central Test directly.
1.7.2	Confidentiality and Security
Central Test guarantees the security and confidentiality of data generated during the administration of online tests: access codes are encrypted in the database and cannot be accessed by anyone. Passwords are computationally generated and not visible to companies. A URL rewriting module (secure link generator) has been installed and has transparent parameter passing for the user.
Browsing and data exchanged via the Central Test application can be secured and represented by an icon in the form of a padlock in the internet browser. The Central Test
 
application has a <<GeoTrust SSL TRUE BusinessID>> digital signature, issued and certified by GeoTrust®. This certificate protects all Central Test assessments from phishing attacks, and serves as proof of our dedication to providing a secure platform.
Invitations to take tests are made via secured links that are of limited use (in terms of numbers and duration). The tests are timed and IP addresses are tracked.
1.7.3	Hardware Architecture and Server Availability
Central Test has a network of ten servers to provide high-quality service. Each server is housed in a specialised data centre featuring:
•	Dedicated lines

•	4-hour Equipment Restoration time
•	2-hour Incident Intervention time
•	Monitoring and assistance 24/7/365
Access to the server administration is restricted to an IP address and is password-secured. Data exchanges are secure. Central Test’s application has been tested on and is compatible with the following browsers: Internet Explorer 8 or newer, Firefox 21 or newer, Google Chrome 30 or newer, and Safari 7 or newer.
