The confirmatory factor analysis (CFA) confirmed that the 6 factors are independent of each other in measuring the construct of counterproductive work behaviour (CWB). CFA also confirmed that the 12 facets are independent of each other while measuring CWB. CFA on the two facets, Self and Others, separately revealed that they are independent of one another and both measure the construct effectively. As all four CFAs are significant (p<.00), it can be concluded that there is no relationship between the variables investigated.

CFA converged on all four analyses, meaning that the construct measured in ETIX is therefore applicable in ‘real world’ situations. The fact that the analyses converged also demonstrates that ETIX does not measure external factors or other related constructs, and instead it only measures what it intended to.

6.3.	Consequential Validity
Consequential validity refers to what happens to assessment results when used and interpreted in a ‘real-life context’. It is of particular importance to take this into account due to the potentially negative consequences and misinterpretations that can arise from an assessment of this nature. Consequential validity was established by running various analyses to measure the invariance of the questionnaire’s applications across gender, age and study level. Scores should be interpreted with care when there is a significant difference between groups (p<.05).

6.3.1.	Measurement Equivalence

Measurement equivalence (ME) refers to the consistency of measurement across specified group demarcation, such as nationality, ethnicity and gender. The focus of ME is to ensure that individuals from different groups with the same score on a trait have the same set of observations on an assessment (Drasgow, 1984; McLeary & Cruise, 2012). Understanding ME is important in an assessment to establish confidence, validity and equivalence across groups. ME is obtained when candidates from different cultural backgrounds, age groups, ethnicities, genders and occupational groups with the same level on a trait, have an equal chance of being selected for a role that requires the desired level on this trait. ME helps to determine the extent to which respondents are interpreting items similarly, and identified differences across groups reflect actual differences in the trait being measured (Drasgow, 1984). One approach to assessing measurement equivalence is differential item functioning analysis.

6.3.2.	Differential Item Functioning

An item functions differently, or displays differential item functioning (DIF), if respondents from different groups have different probabilities of endorsing the item, despite having the same level of the trait. When a different probability of endorsing an item remains constant across different levels of the trait, the DIF is said to be uniform (Figure 2). Here, the item of interest consistently gives one group an advantage across all levels of the trait, even when the groups are matched on ability. Conversely, when a different probability of endorsing an item varies along the level of the trait, the DIF is characterised as non-uniform (Clauser & Mazor, 1998; Hanson, 1998). Rather than a consistent
 
advantage being given to the reference group across the ability continuum, an advantage to one group over another exists at different levels of the trait. For instance, an item may give the reference group an advantage at the higher end of the continuum, while a disadvantage at the lower end (Figure 3).



Figure 2. Item characteristic curve with uniform
DIF.	Figure 3. Item characteristic curve with non-
uniform DIF.


Traditionally testing for the presence of DIF under the logistic regression framework is based on likelihood ratio χ2 test (Swaminathan & Rogers, 1990). Initially used for dichotomous items, this technique was then extended for polytomous items1, in which case the proportional-odds logistic regression model (Agresti, 1990) is used.

The logistic regression procedure, as used in ETIX to assess DIF, focuses on one item after the other, looking for difference between groups when participants are matched on similar level of trait. The matching criterion is the IRT score of each factor.

For each item, different nested models are fit to the data to test the null hypotheses (no difference between the groups) and the alternative hypotheses (a: there is a difference between groups or b: there is a difference between groups, and this difference is not constant across all trait levels):

Model 0: logit P(ui ≥ k) = αk
Model 1: logit P(ui ≥ k) = αk + β1 * trait level
Model 2: logit P(ui ≥ k) = αk + β1 * trait level + β2 *group
Model 3: logit P(ui ≥ k) = αk + β1 * trait level + β2 *group + β2 * trait level * group

Where P(ui ≥ k) is the cumulative probabilities that the item response falls in response option k or higher, and αk are the intercepts.

The log-likelihood of each of the 3 models are recorded. Uniform DIF (degrees of freedom (df) = 1) is tested by comparing the log likelihood values for Model 1 and Model 2, non-uniform DIF (df = 1) by comparing Model 2 and Model 3, and the global overall DIF (df = 2) effect by comparing Model 1 and Model 3. These comparisons are performed with the likelihood ratio X2 statistic.

1 Data is polytomous when item levels are available in multiple categories (ordinal and interval), whereas dichotomous assessments have items with only two levels (binary).
 
Criteria for detecting DIF:

Items were flagged as DIF if any of the three likelihood ratio X2 statistics was significant. Significance level was fixed at alpha =.01 (due to multiple comparison). Moreover, Cox and Snell (1989), Nagelkerke (1991), and McFadden (Menard, 2000) pseudo R2 were reported as a magnitude effects. The level of the effect size provided by the difference between the R2 coefficients of the nested models were classified as "negligible" (<.13), "moderate" (.13 to .26) or "large" (>.26) based on Zumbo and Thomas’ (1997) scale of DIF effect.

Purification process:

Ordinal logistic regression uses IRT factor scores as a matching criterion for detecting DIF, assuming that all items except the item of interest are DIF free. Iterative purification is a process used in order to determine whether the IRT factor score was already biased prior to running an ordinal logistic regression analysis, due to items with DIF being included in the scale. It iteratively recalculates the matching criterion and the DIF, by using just the items which are identified as non-DIF. Iterative purification therefore reduces the effect of DIF on the factor estimates which are used for matching criterion (Choi, Gibbons & Crane, 2011).

Gender, study level and age differences were separately subjected to Dif analyses for each factor. Analyses were performed using R software version 3.1.3 and the lordif package for R (Choi, Gibbons & Crane, 2011).

6.3.3.	DIF Analysis: Gender

In order to discover if items performed differently across gender, we first defined two groups of professionals by gender (males and females). These analyses were conducted to ensure that all of the items were interpreted in the same way by participants of either gender, and that there was not a bias towards either males or females.

6.3.3.a.	DIF Analysis: Participants

Analyses were conducted with a total of 320 participants all from the UK norm sample. Participants were separated in two groups according to their gender. 168 women (53%) aged from 21 to 65 years (M=43.80; SD=10.8) and 152 men (47%) aged from 23 to 65 years (M=43.62; SD=10.3) were included in the analysis.

6.3.3.b.	DIF Analysis: Results

Analyses identified 2 items relating to Respect for Commitments, 3 for Equality, 1 for Goods and Property and 2 items for Laws and Procedures displaying a general DIF effect for gender, due to the presence of non-uniform DIF for all items, and the presence of uniform and non-uniform DIF in the Goods and Property and Laws and Procedures items. However, the size of the effects, as indicated by the pseudo R2, were negligible: <.02, which is far below Zumbo’s (1999) threshold of .13 indicating a negligible size effect.

No DIF was flagged for the factors Facts and Honesty, and Other People. The results of these analyses show that the majority of items (132 out of 140) on ETIX do not function differently across genders. Table 27 shows the Likelihood Ratio X2 Statistics for this analysis; all items with demonstrated DIF have been included in this table. Uniform DIF is displayed as χ212, while non-uniform DIF is displayed as χ223, and global DIF is displayed as χ213.

One item displaying DIF was chosen to graphically illustrate DIF in ETIX with relation to gender. Figure 4 displays analyses of the one item for Commitments, which has negligible gender differences. The reference group (males) is more likely to endorse the item (Figure 4) than the focal group (females).
 
 

Figure 4. Graphical Display of an Item “If I was offered a better opportunity elsewhere, I would not
feel I had to give much advance warning.” for the Factor Commitments which Illustrates Non-Uniform DIF with Respect to Gender. Reference Group = Men, Focal Group = Women.

The top left graph displays the item characteristic curves (ICCs) for the item for male (solid curve) vs. female (dotted curve) professionals based on item true-score functions. The Logistic Regression χ2 test for uniform DIF (χ212) was not significant (p=.813), whereas non uniform DIF (χ223) was significant (p=.0154). The slope of the function for males was slightly higher than that for females at a high theta level, whereas it was higher for females than males at a lower theta level, further indicating a non- uniform DIF.

The top right and the bottom right graphs both show the absolute difference between the ICCs for male and female professionals. The top right graph illustrates the difference in scores between groups along the continuum of trait level (theta score), and shows that the small difference in item true score function is present only at low and high level of the trait Commitments. The bottom right graph shows this same difference in score between groups but weighted by the score distribution of the focal group, thus indicating that the impact of the difference between the item score of the two groups is actually minimal.

The bottom left graph shows the item response functions for the two groups separately for each response options. Slope (discrimination parameter) and category threshold values (difficulty parameters at the intersection of categories response options) are printed on the left of the graph. Difference between groups on the slope parameters (1.47 for men, .74 for women) is an indicator of non-uniform DIF, which is confirmed by the visual inspection of the graph “item true score function” (top left figure). The category threshold values, calculated for each group, indicate at which point of the response option the difference between groups occurs. For this item, category threshold value
 
differs between groups at the intersection of response option “Strongly agree” and “Somewhat agree” (-1.42 for men and -2.42 for women): as the items in ETIX have reversed coding (“Strongly disagree” with the item results in a higher ethical score than “Strongly agree” meaning that the curve from left to right of the graph corresponds to “Strongly agree”, “Somewhat agree”, “Somewhat disagree” and “Strongly disagree” respectively), the probability of endorsing the “Strongly agree” over the “Somewhat agree” option for men starts at lower level of trait compared to women. Category threshold value also differs between groups at the intersection of response option “Somewhat disagree” and “Strongly disagree” (1.58 for men, 3.14 for women): the probability of endorsing the “Strongly disagree” over the “Somewhat disagree” option for men starts at lower level of trait compared to women.

Conclusion: Overall, the results of DIF analyses for gender showed that only 8 items out of 140 displayed a DIF effect with only a negligible size effect. Moreover, the impact of the 8 items with DIF on the IRT factor score of their respective dimension was non-significant. Therefore, we can conclude that ETIX questionnaire is appropriate for use for both male and female professionals without causing any discrimination, and practitioners can be confident of obtaining valid, representative and fair results from its use.

6.3.4.	DIF Analysis: Age

In order to discover if items performed differently across gender, we first defined two groups of professionals by age; one group consisted of those aged under 50 and the other of those aged 50 and over.

6.3.4.a.	DIF Analysis: Participants

Younger professionals were used as the reference group and comprised of 218 participants (51% women) aged from 21 to 49 years (M=37.9; SD=7.1). The focal group included 102 participants (56% women) aged from 50 to 65 years (M=56.05; SD=4.3) and were classified as older professionals.

6.3.4.b.	DIF Analysis: Results

Analyses flagged 4 items relating to Equality, 3 for Facts and Honesty, 1 for Goods and Property and 1 item for Laws and Procedures with a DIF effect for age. 1 item for Equality displayed a non-uniform DIF effect, while the other items displayed both a uniform and a non-uniform DIF (Table 28). No DIF was identified for the factors Commitments and Other People. Table 28 shows the Likelihood Ratio DIF statistics for this analysis; all items with demonstrated DIF have been included in this table. The results of these analyses show that the majority of items (131 out of 140) on ETIX do not function differently across age groups.

6.3.5.	DIF Analysis: Study Level

In order to discover if items performed differently across study level, we first defined two groups of professionals; one group consisted of those with a Bachelor’s, Master’s or PhD degree and the other of those with trades training or no study, a high school diploma and those with 2 years vocational study.

6.3.5.a.	DIF Analysis: Participants

University level professionals were used as the reference group and comprised of 140 participants (54% men) aged from 21 to 44 years (M=40.8; SD=10.3). The focal group included 180 participants (57% women) aged from 25 to 63 years (M=46; SD=10.2) and were classified as working professionals.

6.3.5.b.	DIF Analysis: Results

Analyses flagged 8 items with DIF out of the 140 items included in the ETIX questionnaire: 1 item relating to Facts and Honesty, 2 items from Equality and 3 items from Laws and Procedures displayed both a uniform and a non-uniform DIF for educational level, while 1 item from Equality and 1 item for Laws and Procedures displayed a non-uniform DIF (Table 29). No DIF was identified for the factors Commitments, Goods and Property, and Other People.

Therefore, we can conclude that the ETIX questionnaire is appropriate for use with professionals regardless of their educational level, as long as they have had at least a secondary school education (high school level), and regardless of their age or gender, without causing any discrimination. Practitioners can be confident of obtaining valid, representative and fair results from its use. ETIX provides candidates from different genders, age groups and study levels with the same level on a factor, the same chance of being selected for a role that requires the desired level on this factor.

6.3.6.	T-tests and ANOVAs

The DIF analyses demonstrate that ETIX has high consequential validity and, although some items were flagged with DIF, there was a negligible impact on the overall assessment meaning that it does not have bias towards any age group, gender or study level. Therefore, any significant differences found between genders, age groups, or study levels are due to real differences and not biases of the questionnaire. To further explore the differences between the groups, t-tests and ANOVAS were performed between genders, age groups and study levels (see Tables 31-33).

Gender: In order to check for differences in scores between genders, an independent-samples t-test was conducted to compare all ETIX factors and facets between females (n=161) and males (n=145).

Females scored significantly higher than males on all ETIX factors and facets (see Table 31). These differences might be due to the deeply rooted societal gender roles wherein women are expected to act nicely, making them more prone to follow the norms, avoid conflicts and respect others, while men are expected to be ambitious and successful, hence making them more prone to bending the norms in order to achieve their goals. However, some studies have shown that gender differences on ethical judgement may not be as significant (Loo, 2003). These results should therefore be interpreted with caution and further investigated.

As illustrated in Table 31, a one-way ANOVA was conducted to explore the differences between the age groups on ETIX factors and facets. Significant differences were found for Factors Respect for Goods and Property, Respect for Facts and Honesty and Respect for Laws and Procedures as well as all of their corresponding facets. A similar trend can be observed in the aforementioned factors, with the older age group (44 or more) obtaining the highest scores on all factors and facets, while the youngest age group (28 or less) obtained the lowest scores. A possible explanation for this might be that older people tend to lead a financially more secure life than younger people (Miron-Shatz, 2009); consequently they might have a stricter stance on stealing, lying and corruption as they have “more to loose and less to gain” from such behaviours. Another explanation for the present findings could be that as people age, their brain’s cognitive control system changes, improving their capacity for self-regulation, which in turn decreases the impulse towards sensation seeking and risk-taking behaviour (Steinberg, 2008). This might in turn affect the way older people view the counterproductive work behaviours attached to these factors, making them less likely to engage in them.

However, no significant differences were found for the factors Respect for Others, Respect for Equality and Respect for Commitments. This could be due to the fact that these factors are more indirectly
 
related to breaking the rules or the law, and constitute more modern preoccupations, which might balance the differences discussed in the previous paragraph.

   MOTIVATION+ is a psychometric evaluation tool, designed to analyse an individual’s motivations, values and satisfaction at work. Consisting of 41 questions, it is a useful and relevant tool for recruitment, performance review, career and self-development, training or establishing management policies.

Another version of MOTIVATION+ is MOTIVATION. The difference between the two versions is that MOTIVATION+ has an additional scale to measure an individual’s job satisfaction. While MOTIVATION+ consists of 40 questions assessing an individual’s motivations plus an additional question to assess the individual’s job satisfaction, MOTIVATION consists of only 40 questions evaluating an individual’s motivations.

MOTIVATION+ is especially useful for different processes of companies and organisations such as performance evaluation, personal and professional development, support in intervention programmes and / or for the review of policies. MOTIVATION, on the other hand, is exclusively suitable for the selection and hiring processes.

The assessment provides an in-depth analysis of a candidate's preference for certain values in a working environment, based on 15 fundamental values. The scale was specifically developed to evaluate values at work because a person's behaviours, choices and attitudes are driven by values. Thus, the profiles generated by MOTIVATION+ will help decision-makers optimise the match between individuals and their work, team and organisation.

MOTIVATION+ is validated via a sample of 915 professionals from a variety of occupations, educational levels and age groups. Due to its robust psychological approach and practitioner-based results, the assessment can be utilised by decision-makers in the field of HR, management, work psychology, coaching, training and consulting, worldwide.

There are several features that make MOTIVATION+ especially interesting. These include:

•	Contemporary and business relevant items
•	Compositional / integrative response format with 3 levels
•	Short questionnaire length with only 41 questions
•	Average completion time of 12 minutes
•	Practical and easy-to understand results
•	User-friendly and customisable reports that can be easily tailored
•	Developed in multiple languages simultaneously
•	Valid and reliable, with studies conducted on professionals from multiple countries

In this technical guide & user manual, you will find useful information about the assessment, its development, theoretical background, psychometric properties and practical applications. Chapters 1, 2, 3 and 8 are specifically tailored for HR practitioners whilst chapters 4, 5, 6 and 7 consist of detailed technical and psychometric information.
 
1.	General Description and Applicability
MOTIVATION+ provides a comprehensive account of an individual's motivational profile, with insights into what drives them to be more committed, involved, and efficient in their job. The assessment is based on basic individual values, or more precisely, work values, to provide psychologists and HR professionals with an accurate tool to identify what drives and moves an individual at work.

The underlying logic in using work values to understand an individual’s motivation is that they offer a solid theoretical background, low volatility in time and evident inter-individual variations.

The list of the motivators or factors measured in MOTIVATION+ is provided below, along with their definitions. These factors are categorised into their relevant work value groups.

Social Motivators

The work value group 'Social Motivators' encompasses those values that relate to an individual's social surroundings. Consisting of 'Social Environment,' 'Social Utility', 'Idea Sharing', 'Influence' and 'Developing Others,' these values enable a person to build upon and strengthen the social relationships that they have at work, and to help and support others.

1.	Social Environment - People that are motivated by the social environment at work appreciate working in a friendly workplace where they can count on and turn to their colleagues. They see interacting with others around them as an important way to help maintain office harmony. Working in a company that promotes such an environment will allow them to feel enthusiastic and driven about their work.
2.	Social Utility - People that are motivated by social utility at work place high importance on being able to benefit others with their work. They feel driven when they can take part in tasks that they know will help others.
3.	Idea Sharing - People who are motivated by this value are driven by an environment where their ideas are appreciated and listened to. They feel motivated when they are encouraged to share their thoughts and when attention is paid to their suggestions.
4.	Influence - People who are motivated by influence like to be able to impact work outcomes through their involvement in the reaching of important conclusions. They feel driven when they can have a say in important decisions that will affect projects.
5.	Developing Others - Individuals that are motivated by developing others feel driven when they are able to pass on their skills and knowledge. They value the opportunity to transmit their capabilities and expertise to others.
 
Personal-development Motivators

The work value group 'Personal-development Motivators' relates to the values that contribute to an individual's professional self development and growth. Consisting of 'Surpassing', 'Competition', 'Intellectual Curiosity', 'Career Opportunities' and 'Appraisal', these values enable employees to advance in their job, and achieve success.

1.	Surpassing - People that are motivated by the value surpassing like to have opportunities to exceed themselves. They feel driven when their work provides them with opportunities that push them out of their comfort zone and test their limits.
2.	Competition - Individuals who feel driven by competition at work will feel stimulated by an environment that enables them to try to outperform others. They will be motivated by a workplace that encourages friendly contests between co- workers to reach targets or goals.
3.	Intellectual Curiosity - This factor refers to feeling motivated at work when acquiring new knowledge. People who are driven by this value like to work on tasks where they are able to discover new topics in order to broaden their horizons. They feel fuelled when they are able to satisfy their desire to learn.
4.	Career Opportunities - Individuals who are driven by career opportunities seek to climb up the career ladder, and appreciate chances for promotion. As a result, they will feel motivated when their job gives them the possibility to advance the status of their career.
5.	Appraisal - People that are motivated by appraisal like to have regular evaluations from their co-workers, or superiors, regarding the quality of their work. Being informed on how they are doing and about how others perceive their work will enable them to feel driven and on the right track in the projects that they undertake.

Contextual Motivators

The 'Contextual Motivators' work value group relates to the extrinsic motivations of an individual provided by the company, and encompasses 'Mobility', 'Remuneration', 'Well- being', 'Security' and 'Autonomous Working Conditions'. These can be defined as the values which enable a person to gain some form of external reward, and tend to be additional aspects to a person's work itself.

1.	Mobility - Individuals that are motivated by mobility like to have a job that allows them to move from place to place, rather than remaining at a desk for most of their schedule. Leaving the office on a regular basis will allow them to feel driven in the work they are undertaking.
2.	Remuneration - Those that are driven by remuneration feel motivated by jobs with factors such as high salary and other material rewards. Bonuses and additional benefits are important to them in order to feel enthusiastic about the work that they undertake.
3.	Well-being - This factor refers to those who feel motivated in their job when their
 
company places importance on their well-being. Factors such as a good balance between work and private life are crucial to them, and they will feel driven if their employer recognises the importance of occupational health.
4.	Security - Those that feel driven by security like to be assured that they have a long- term, permanent position that is not likely to change anytime soon, unless they wish it to. If their position is secured in such a way and they know that they are able to remain within the company for the long haul, they will feel driven and enthusiastic in their role."
5.	Autonomous Working Conditions - People who are motivated by autonomous working conditions value liberty in their working approach. They are motivated when their company offers flexibility, such as having independence in deciding how they proceed on their projects and tasks, and the ways in which they work.

1.1.	Main Areas of Application
MOTIVATION+ was specifically designed to help companies achieve a better understanding of what motivates people to succeed and in what type of position, job, and environment they will be the most productive. It may be used throughout an employee's career-span, or to optimise a company’s internal processes such as:

●	Recruitment - With MOTIVATION+, you can assess whether a candidate’s values match the culture of your organisation.
●	Career Management - You can find out whether an individual’s motivations allow them to succeed in the function and the position they strive for.
●	Internal Mobility - By assessing your employee’s motives and values, you will be able to implement changes that will lead your employees to reinforce their involvement and be more enthusiastic and engaged at work.
●	Prevention of Psychosocial Risks - With MOTIVATION+, you can identify sources of employee dissatisfaction and pinpoint areas for improvement. You will thus be better equipped to prepare courses of action to reduce employee stress as well as undesirable behaviours at work.

The questionnaire can also be used in conjunction with other occupational-based assessments to acquire a more comprehensive profile of individuals in specific roles. For example, combining MOTIVATION+ with EMOTION 2© provides in-depth insight into whether an individual is well-suited to obtain a leading position at work, by providing information on whether the individual’s values are a good fit for their job as well as their ability to motivate themselves to achieve goals and maintain a good relationship with their team.

1.2.	Questionnaire Structure
MOTIVATION+ consists of 41 questions and is divided into two parts. The first part assesses an individual’s motivational factors and comprises 40 questions that are
 
presented in a compositional or integrative format. Each question consists of three statements that require the test-taker to indicate the extent to which the statements are preferred to each other. In order to do so, the candidate selects two: the most motivating statement by clicking   and the least motivating statement by clicking . The third statement is considered as neutral. Each of the 15 factors in MOTIVATION+ is measured by 8 items. The questionnaire therefore measures 120 motivational components whilst appearing significantly shorter. This item structure also reduces the likelihood of respondent fatigue and response bias. Furthermore, forcing respondents to allocate their preferences to the three options of a question safeguards against impression management and the test-taker’s attempts to fake results.



Figure 1. Example of a Compositional Format Item on MOTIVATION+.

The second part of the questionnaire consists of 1 question with 15 sub-questions that concern the test-taker’s satisfaction with their current or previous job. A score of 1 indicates that the test-taker is not at all satisfied with this aspect in his / her job and a score of 10 indicates that the test-taker is very satisfied. Each of the 15 factors measured in MOTIVATION+ is represented by one global satisfaction question.

Figure 2. Example of Satisfaction Scale Items on MOTIVATION+.

Thus, MOTIVATION+’s questionnaire structure allows to compare an individual’s
 
motivation with their level of satisfaction. However, it is worthwhile to note that it is possible that an individual who may not be motivated by a certain factor can still be satisfied with the amount or role that this factor plays in their job.

1.3.	Mode of Administration, Languages and Special Requirements
MOTIVATION+ is administered online and works with all computer systems including Windows, MAC-OS and Linux. The assessment can be administered remotely and without supervision. A PDF version of the questionnaire is also available, if required. As reports are always generated online, if the test has been administered in paper format, then the scores must manually be entered onto the online platform to generate the candidate reports.

The questionnaire is currently available in English, French and Spanish. For more information on language availability, please contact Central Test’s Client or Consulting Service (customer@centraltest.com or consulting@centraltest.com).

MOTIVATION+ was designed to be easily used by individuals from diverse backgrounds and for those with a range of special needs. All test-takers are able to adjust visual screen specifications based on individual requirements. Similarly, respondents are able to access test instructions throughout the assessment. It is also possible to navigate through questions or change answers as desired. Alternatively, for blind candidates or those with severe visual disabilities, items may be read aloud by a trained administrator. Individuals with hearing impairments are not excluded from taking the questionnaire, since all content is presented visually.

Although the average time for completion is 15 minutes, MOTIVATION+ has no set time limit. Therefore, individuals with learning disabilities can take the time they need to complete the assessment. Administrators are thus encouraged to consider whether a test-taker who needed an unusual amount of time to take the assessment may have special needs. Administrators may contact Central Test for guidance on administering the questionnaire to individuals or groups with special requirements.

1.4.	Social Desirability
Social desirability is the tendency to present oneself in a manner that will be viewed as favourable by others. When done unintentionally, it is referred to as self-deception. When done intentionally, it is referred to as impression management (Tett & Simonet, 2011). As people are social by nature, they are generally inclined to seek some degree of social acceptance. Unlike aptitude tests, the results of questionnaires like MOTIVATION+ are affected by impression management. An individual’s attempt to present himself / herself in a favourable light thus negatively impacts test results, whether the questionnaire is completed online or in paper-based format (Dodou & de Winter, 2014).
MOTIVATION+ actively guards against social desirability with the use of an ipsative (forced
 
choice) questionnaire format. Research has found ipsative measures to be more resistant to distorted responding compared to more commonly used Likert-scales (Bowen, Martin, & Hunt, 2002; Kluger, Reilly, & Russell, 1991). Not only does the ipsative format reduce the test-taker’s ability to inflate their scores, but items are also combined in such a manner that makes it difficult for them to conceive a response strategy, as items are paired with equally appealing response options.

1.5.	Test-taker Requirements and Recommendations
MOTIVATION+ was designed for members of the general population, 18 years and older and is suitable for all occupational roles. It is recommended that test-takers receive notification of the objective of the assessment, conditions under which it will be administered, how data will be handled and stored, and the nature of any feedback well in advance. Potential candidates should also be asked to inform the test administrator beforehand of any special needs or requirements.

Previous test-taking experience is not required to take MOTIVATION+. Similarly, advanced education and work experience are not requirements to successfully take the assessment.

1.6.	Administrator Qualifications and Recommendations
The assessment was developed to be used by human resource (HR) professionals, training and executive development professionals, counsellors and psychologists. To administer MOTIVATION+, practitioners must complete standardised training and / or possess accredited testing standards qualifications. Training modules designed by Central Test ensure that administrators are able to administer the assessment under standardised conditions; score and norm results; interpret scores and generate written and verbal feedback for a range of organisational needs.

It is recommended that all administrators take MOTIVATION+, prior to administering the assessment, making sure to comply with all instructions. This will enable practitioners to become familiar with the assessment and therefore anticipate questions or challenges that may be identified by the test-takers.

Test administration must be in accordance with the organisation’s code of practice, as well as legal assessment guidelines on the use of psychometric assessments for employment purposes. All steps must be taken to ensure test administration is objective and fair. Therefore, it is particularly recommended that administrators are aware of international and country laws governing; a) Data Protection, b) Gender Discrimination, c) Age Discrimination, d) Race and Ethnicity Relations, e) Disabilities Discrimination, and f) Religious Discrimination prior to administration.
 
Additionally, prior to use, administrators must be familiar with Central Test's conditions of use and code of ethics and adhere to its quality charter. Administrators must:

•	Ensure that the appropriate test has been chosen to meet the specified organisational need
•	Make sure that the test-taker is informed about the assessment and its purpose prior to administration.
•	Obtain test-taker’s consent prior to administration
•	Use appropriate norms to interpret results
•	Provide the test-taker with feedback on results
•	Delete questionnaire results and personal candidate data upon demand
•	Treat results and candidate data confidentially and obtain candidate consent before transferring personal data or results to third parties.
•	Ensure that only qualified personnel administer assessments and have access to data
•	Ensure that test materials and reports are stored in secure environments

For more detailed information about Central Test's code of ethics, refer to Central Test's quality charter.

1.6.1.	Testing Conditions

For best results and candidate experience, MOTIVATION+ should be administered in a quiet, well-lit and ventilated environment. The test-taker must be informed about the assessment in advance and should have sufficient time to take it. In order to reduce their anxiety, the assessment should be referred to as a questionnaire and not as a test.

For an online administration, it is important that test-takers have comfortable seating, adequate desk space, adequate computer screen size, a keyboard and a mouse. Prior to testing, administrators should ensure that all relevant candidate background information has been collected and verified in order to confirm that the candidate taking the assessment is the right individual.

In cases where individuals take the assessment in the privacy of their home or a convenient location, it is recommended that they do so using a computer they are familiar and comfortable with. For security purposes, all background information and candidate details must be provided to the administrator prior to a candidate taking the assessment. The background information is pre-filled by the test administrator but can be corrected by the candidate after the completion of the assessment, when verifying the ‘Additional Information’ page post questionnaire completion. Candidates receive notifications to take the questionnaire via email with an embedded web link and login details. Web links to the questionnaire are password-protected and unique to a candidate’s account. It is possible to set expiration dates in order to ensure candidates complete the questionnaire within a specified time-frame. For additional security, administrators can also configure the invitation to ensure that the assessment must be completed from the same IP address
 
once the questionnaire has been accessed.

For monitoring purposes, completion times are recorded to ensure a pleasant test-taking experience. It also allows administrators to see if a candidate rushed through the questionnaire or needed an unusual amount of time to complete the assessment. Test- takers are encouraged to answer questions quickly in order to avoid spending too much time thinking about each statement. It is also recommended that they answer spontaneously as there are no right or wrong answers.

1.6.2.	Answering Questions

Although instructions for completing MOTIVATION+ are presented on-screen, it is important that administrators establish and maintain rapport with candidates. In the case where MOTIVATION+ is administered offline, instructions should be read aloud by the administrator with candidates given an opportunity to ask questions before taking the assessment. Administrators should also verify that candidates understand what they are required to do prior to starting the assessment session.

Guidelines for a few scenarios:

•	If a test-taker does not understand a question, the administrator should answer carefully and avoid explaining the meaning of words or items in his / her own words in order to not lead them toward specific responses.
•	If an administrator suspects that a test-taker’s lack of understanding is due to language proficiency, the administrator should ask them to take the assessment
in the language they are most comfortable with.
•	If an administrator suspects that a test-taker’s lack of understanding is due to overcautious responding, they should be encouraged to endorse their most likely response.

1.7.	Technical and Security Issues
This chapter details guidelines governing the security of administering MOTIVATION+. These guidelines ensure that every administration of MOTIVATION+ is a pleasant, objective and fair process. Security specifications ensure that candidate information is protected both during administration and after assessment completion.

1.7.1.	Respecting the Ethical Charter

As a publisher of online assessment tools, Central Test respects the legal conventions of French labour law and the regulatory provisions resulting from the law of 6 January 1978 pertaining to information technology, files and freedoms: Declaration N°1834067. Additionally, as of 25th May, 2018, Central Test complies with the GDPR (General Data Protection Regulation) made by the European Parliament & Council.
 
Test results are not to be forwarded to a third person without the explicit consent of the test candidate. As the test-taker has a right to access his / her results, the administrator must grant the test-taker access automatically or when he / she requests it.

The test-taker also has the right to request that all of his / her personal data be permanently deleted. To do this, they must contact either the organisation directly, who will delete his / her data, or Central Test.
1.7.2.	Confidentiality and Security of User Data

Central Test guarantees the security and confidentiality of data generated during the administration of online tests. Access codes are encrypted in the database and cannot be accessed by anyone. Passwords are computationally generated and are not visible to companies. A URL rewriting module (secure link generator) is installed and has transparent parameter passing for the user.

Browsing and data exchange via the Central Test application can be secured and encrypted using SSL. Secure browsing is guaranteed by the https protocol, which is represented by an icon in the form of a padlock in the internet browser. The Central Test application has a “GeoTrust SSL True BusinessID” digital signature, issued and certified by GeoTrust©. This certificate is irrefutable evidence of our serious attitude towards security and protects all of Central Test’s assessments from phishing attacks. Invitations to take tests are made via secured links that are of limited use (in terms of numbers and duration). The tests are timed and IP addresses are tracked. Access to questionnaires may also be limited in terms of numbers and duration.

1.7.3.	Hardware Architecture and Server Availability

Central Test’s architecture consists of a network of 10 physical dedicated servers providing high-quality service.

Each server is housed in a specialised data centre featuring:

•	dedicated lines
•	a guaranteed bandwidth of 100Mbps (for each server)
•	redundant energy sources
•	4-hour Equipment Restoration Time
•	2-hour Incident Intervention Time
•	monitoring and assistance 24/7/365

Access to server administration is restricted by the IP address and is password-secured. Data exchanges are also secure.
 
The Central Test application has been tested using the following browsers:

•	Internet Explorer 8 or above
•	Firefox 21 or above
•	Google Chrome 30 or above
•	Safari 7 or above

1.7.4.	Computer-generated Reports

Reports can be generated within minutes of candidates completing the questionnaire. The MOTIVATION+ report is user friendly, customisable and available in several languages. Report content can be tailored depending on the needs of the administrator. Separate reports may also be configured for the candidate and the company, recruiter, coach or psychologist. Reports can also be generated in PDF format.

1.8.	Supply, Conditions and Availability
Conditions of supply and availability for MOTIVATION+ vary from country to country. However, the following points generally apply:
•	MOTIVATION+ is only available for psychologists, HR practitioners and coaches.
•	Training is compulsory prior to use and distribution.
•	This manual is available to individuals who have been trained to use MOTIVATION+.
•	MOTIVATION+ can be ordered online, or by contacting any Central Test customer service representative or local partner worldwide (customer@centraltest.com).
•	For information on MOTIVATION+’s prices and related products and services, contact any Central Test customer service representative or local partner worldwide.
•	For more information on the training, kindly contact Central Test's customer service or one of Central Test's partners.
 
2.	Conceptual and Practical Scale Development
MOTIVATION+ was developed using robust theoretical concepts, text analysis of practical and relevant work requirements and advanced scientific analyses. All items in the item bank were piloted and pretested with professionals from multiple continents in order to ensure that the work behaviours assessed were contemporary, global and culture-free.

2.1.	Theoretical Background
For over a century, the working world has faced major transformations (Broeck, Vansteenkiste, Witte, Soenens, & Lens, 2010). Closely related to the changing economic market, these changes have been moderated by globalisation, and facilitated by new information and communication technologies (ICT). Technological, economic and social advances have brought forward new challenges to the workplace which highlight the importance of the role of the human being in the work environment, and expose the need to question how performance should be defined in technical and human terms. The post- war period was a turning point for human capital management in modern companies. Production methods were improved to achieve a maximum level of efficiency. From this, work psychology emerged, providing insights on key issues such as decision-making, recruitment, performance evaluation, management of organisations and welfare at work, etc. (Lévy-Leboyer, 2006).

Since the beginning of the twentieth century, economists have tried to answer one specific question: “how are employees motivated?” The first answer to this question established the belief that employees are generally lazy and motivated by monetary incentives. This notion was institutionalised by Frederick Winslow Taylor via his Scientific Management theory, and is now well known as the principle of the “economic man”. However, psychologists quickly realised that the concept of motivation is centred on factors that are much more complex than simple financial rewards, and several experiments have shown that money is not the primary interest of employees.

Over the last few decades, scientists have attended to this matter (G. P. Latham & Budworth, 2006) and proposed various models of motivation. And after seventy years of scientific research, the modern perception of motivation appears to be explained through a complex and multifactorial process. Central to this process are individual characteristics (affect, cognition and social), environmental aspects, and the interaction of these two elements (Lévy-Leboyer, 2006). Motivation is therefore defined as a “physiological and psychological process, responsible for the initiation, maintenance and termination of behaviour and the appetitive or aversive value of the situation in which the specific behaviour occurs” (Bloch et al., 1991). Vallerand and Thill (1993) defined motivation as “the hypothetical construct that describes the internal and/or external forces that produce the initiation, direction, intensity and persistence of behaviour”. Put simply, this definition states that motivation is a hypothetical, and therefore abstract, construct of mental processes which can only be measured indirectly by observing people’s behaviour or behavioural intentions.
 
The theories of Maslow and Herzberg appear to be the cornerstones of the many theories of motivation proposed during the twentieth century. Despite many limitations to their hypotheses, their approach was hugely popular within human resources, and their names are still present to this day. They claimed that motivation is a process that occurs when a humans act in a specific way to meet their demands. Their great contribution was to then incorporate into their theory, originally considered solely as an exchange system of effort and financial reward, a spiritual dimension (Lévy-Leboyer, 2006). The stage of the “economic man” was then followed by the stage of the “social man,” or in other words the “human relations movement,” which has its roots in the work of Elton Mayo and the discovery of the Hawthorne effect. Mayo discovered that the performance of workers can be improved simply by observing them, through their awareness of this observation. (Mayo, 1949). This work helped to initiate a wide range of research in the social psychology field. Thus, motivation became a multi-dimensional concept, addressed through human needs, personality traits, context, human-environment suitability, cognition affects and emotions (Latham & Pinder, 2005).

Nowadays, because of the evolution of research on motivation, Lévy-Leboyer (2006) emphasises that: "the language itself has changed: we no longer talk of "needs" but rather values and patterns." (P. 35). The central challenge for organizations is to know and understand the priorities set by stakeholders, in order to construct a company policy based on the hierarchy of values (Lévy-Leboyer, 2006). But what exactly are values? According to Bilsky & Schwartz (1994), values are commonly composed of five basic characteristics: they are concepts or beliefs, refer to desired behaviours or conditions, are not confined to a particular situation, serve as a guideline in the selection or evaluation of behaviour and events, and are classified according to the relative importance that we give them. Similarly, these characteristics may apply to a particular context, for example, “work values” is an expression for general values in a work context (Ros, Schwartz & Surkiss, 1999). It is necessary to differentiate values from attitudes; an attitude is limited to a factor such as a person, location or event, while values, in contrast, are more general and more entrenched. It is thought that values are developed through social interactions, and may be passed culturally through generations. Theories also suggest that in early developmental stages, individuals begin with a wide pool of values, but over time these become refined and individuals learn to direct their behaviour based on the values they prioritise the most. Now that the concept of values has been explored, it is necessary to establish the link between work values and motivation.

Note that in the literature the terms "needs", "motives" and "values" are often used as synonyms, but ‘values’ can be considered an approach to motivation in themselves, specifically as secondary engines that drive actions determined by needs, socialisation, cognition and experience (Deci & Ryan, 2000; Kooij, De Lange, Jansen, Kanfer & Dikkers, 2011; Krumm, Grube & Hertel, 2013). Values are therefore rooted in needs, with the main difference being that needs are innate, while values are acquired through cognition and experience. They are indirectly included in most theories (Latham & Pinder, 2005), and can be divided into two areas; principles (involving an idea of how behaviour should be) and preferences (expressing cravings and/or a hierarchy of values) (Parks & Guay, 2009). Schwartz (2006) argues that values have played a central role in various social science
 
disciplines, including psychology. They can be used to characterise individuals or companies, measure changes across a time axis, and explain behaviours and attitudes.

In addition, personal goals serve as a mediator between values and behaviour, in the sense that values influence the formation of goals, which then lead to certain behavioural choices (Kanfer, 1990). However, Parks & Guay (2009) raise a fundamental issue concerning the measurement of this concept; studies have not been able to empirically identify the link between motivation and values. However, many studies show that values are involved in decision-making processes, the pursuit of goals and are above all influential on the behaviour of individuals.

Based on these explanations, we can still assume that values adequately meet the definition of motivation, as (a) they are primarily a hypothetical construct, (b) they describe the internal or external forces behind action, and (c) they allow a global definition of the initiation, direction, intensity and persistence of behaviour. For example: "status" is a hypothetical construct (i.e. an abstract notion) that comes from an external force (i.e. social recognition) and that will determine the behaviour of the individual who attaches great importance to this value.

Further proof for these assumptions has been found during questionnaire validation studies. Research suggests that performance at work has a higher correlation with motivation that stems from values rather than motivation that stems directly from the task itself (Gagné et al, 2015; Gagne and Deci, 2005).

The theory of basic human values proposed by Schwartz (1992, 1994) contains 10 universal values which differ in terms of motivation. These may be opposite or complementary and can be organised into four higher-order groups. Schwartz (2006) describes six key features that all values have in common:
•	"Values are beliefs"
•	"Values refer to desirable goals"
•	"Values guide the selection of actions, people or events"
•	"Values are used as standard or criteria"
•	"Values are ordered by importance"
•	"The relative importance of multiple values guides action"

The distinction between them is therefore based on the different types of induced motivation. However, this universal model is not specifically designed for the working environment.

The work of Ros et al. (1999) was the first empirical attempt to understand the link between values and attitudes, or behaviour, and to apply the basic human values to the workplace. His pioneering research has many advantages, including the multicultural character of the theory and the importance of distinguishing several general values.
 
Since the publication of this study by Ros et al., over a decade of research has focused on values at work. In 2013, the "Munster Work Value Measure (MWVM)" emerged (Krumm et al., 2013), based on the inventory of values by Schwartz (1992, 1994), which brings together 56 motivational values, represented in a circumplex model and ordered by four meta-values. The MWVN is similar to the basic human values of Schwartz, introducing 21 values ordered by five meta-values. They are also represented in a circumplex and are titled "Intrinsic Growth Values"; “Affective Values”; "Generativity Values"; "Context-related Values"; and "Extrinsic Growth Values". The Intrinsic Growth Values encompass values that achieve personal fulfillment through work itself, such as "learning", "autonomy" and "achievements". Affective Values refer to emotions and feelings that can be experienced at work, for example, values like "enjoyment" or “appreciation". Context-related Values refer to elements around work with values such as "security" or "health". Extrinsic Growth Values refer to the acquisition of resources related to success at work and are composed of values such as "money", "status" or "influence". Finally, Generativity Values refer to all social values that have a concept of transmission and mutual assistance, such as "generativity", "helping" or "legacy". Many studies have shown in the past that a distinction between intrinsic and extrinsic motivation is relevant, and the four-factor model of Schwartz has been replicated many times. The MWVM is an extension of this structure, adding the concept of generativity, defined as a measure of sensitivity by age. (Krumm et al., 2013; Ros et al., 1999; Schwartz, 1992, 1994).

The model of MOTIVATION+ utilises Schwartz’s four-dimensional work value structure - measuring the four work values Self-Advancement, Internal, External and Social - as a theoretical framework to categorise the 15 factors measured in MOTIVATION+ into three motivational value groups - Social Motivators, Personal-development Motivators and Contextual Motivators. This categorisation into the three groups facilitates ease in understanding the 15 motivational factors that encompass MOTIVATION+. Figure 3 shows the model of MOTIVATION+ with its 3 motivational value groups and 15 factors.
 
 

Figure 3. Model of MOTIVATION+.

Age

Values are particularly interesting when measuring motivation because they are relatively stable over time. They may change under special circumstances, for reasons such as the desire to maintain a good image of oneself, the entry into the world of work or a workplace change. Furthermore, there are certain changes in the evolution of values throughout the lifespan of a person. For example, the older one gets, the greater importance given to targets with an emotional component rather than future-oriented goals. This reversal can be explained by a change in the temporal perception of individuals.

Literature states that there are several tools which indirectly measure the differences between individuals based on age. These differences may be relevant for certain companies, and especially career management services (Krumm et al., 2013). For example, generativity values should be of greater importance for people who have advanced in their careers (values such as legacy and social utility), while on the contrary, those at the start of their career will emphasise extrinsic-growth values (such as status and career development opportunities) (Schwartz, 2006).

Gender

Besides age, there are also gender differences in the motivation theories discussed in the literature. Indeed, Schwartz and Rubel (2005) pointed out that many studies deal with a difference between men and women regarding the importance attached to certain values. The research suggests that these differences do exist, even if they are small. For example, men are more likely to have values that refer to action (such as influence or power), whereas women are oriented more towards social values (such as benevolence and universalism) (Schwartz, 2006). A small number of studies have shown that there is even a difference between men and women concerning work values. Specifically, it was shown that women consider social values as more important (e.g. helping others) while men give more importance to extrinsic values (e.g. advancement) (Schwartz & Rubel, 2005).
 
2.2.	Development of MOTIVATION+
MOTIVATION+ was developed via a thorough, multistage process in a comprehensive and scientific manner. The development of the test started in 2016, as a result of accrued demand from companies using psychometric assessments in order to provide a “supplementary aid” in HR processes. Extensive empirical and theoretical research was conducted to create the groups, factors and items of MOTIVATION+. The questionnaire was also pretested several times to ensure that it met the criteria for reliability and validity that is established by internationally recognised psychological institutions. Special attention was also given to guard against social desirability as well as to simplify the ergonomics of the assessment.

2.2.1.	Groups, Factors and Item Construction

MOTIVATION+ is a complete and comprehensive revision of Central Test’s motivational assessment, PRISM ©, which exists only in French. MOTIVATION+ is a result of a review of PRISM’s theoretical background, question format, and item bank, to match contemporary literature on the subject of work motivation. MOTIVATION+ measures 15 motivational factors (detailed in chapter 1). These factors are grouped into three work value dimensions; Social Motivators, Personal-development Motivators and Contextual Motivators.

Multiple reviews were conducted to ensure that the factors are clearly defined, and that items targeting each factor accurately reflect modern and business-relevant behaviours, thus resulting in the following:

1.	Greater differentiation between each motivation-based factor
2.	Inclusion of factors that target occupational and organisational fit
3.	Clearer item structure and wording
4.	Updated use of terms and relevance of items to match the current working world
5.	Simple and easily comprehensible use of language

Thus, items for MOTIVATION+ were written to promote ease of response as well as facilitate candidates’ mental connection and understanding of it. As the questionnaire aimed to capture work behaviours in multiple contexts, items were checked for cultural bias, clarity, offensiveness and language accuracy.

2.2.2.	Ipsative Response Format

Response items for the 40 core questions of MOTIVATION+ are written in an ipsative (forced-choice) format. This means that respondents choose between descriptors according to their preference (in this case, choosing a descriptor which captures their preference most, and which captures their preference least).

Ipsative formats have been found to reduce response bias (Chan, 2003; Saville & Willson,
 
1991; Ten Holt, van Duijn & Boomsma, 2010). The fact that respondents cannot endorse all items eliminates acquiescence (the tendency to agree with all items on a questionnaire), reduces susceptibility to impression management and means that it is generally more difficult to fake (Brown & Maydeu-Olivares, 2011a). Ipsative formats make it impossible to provide the same response on all items, thereby resulting in greater differentiation of scores within a profile.

2.2.3.	Satisfaction Scale’s Likert Format

Question 41 requires the candidate to indicate their level of satisfaction of work, on each of the 15 factors measured in MOTIVATION+. This is presented as fifteen likert scale questions, one for each factor, ranging from scores of 1 to 10. A score of 1 means not at all satisfied,’ a score of 5 means ‘neither satisfied nor dissatisfied,’ and a score of 10 means ‘very satisfied.’

2.2.4.	Pre-tests and Pilot Study

MOTIVATION+ was constructed in a manner that ensured items were culturally equivalent from the early stages of development, rather than focusing on the cultural adaption after the questionnaire validity had been established within a single context – the typical practice of test developers.

Pre-tests were conducted with a range of male and female professionals across the globe to ensure that items were accurate, relevant, clear, concise, culture-free, bias-free and that they reflected targeted work behaviours. After four pretests and a pilot, the final questionnaire was validated with a sample of 915 English-speaking professionals from various countries. The sample consisted of English speakers from different continents in order to establish the questionnaire as an international measure of motivation at work.
 
3.	Scoring
A raw score is the total number of responses endorsed by a candidate for each factor measured. In the context of an occupational motivation assessment, raw scores mean little as they do not provide effective or appropriate comparisons between candidates across a specific motivator. Norms enable Human Resource practitioners, managers and recruiters to evaluate an individual’s score relative to the score of other delegates who have completed the same assessment.

3.1.	The Relationship between Raw Scores and Norms
The best norm group is one that is most representative of the characteristics of the individuals taking a test. Applied assessment assumes personality traits, interests, motivations, values, attitudes and perceptions are distributed along a normal probability or bell-shaped curve. Therefore, norm groups effectively identify individual scores across this distribution. In any given population, levels of a target attribute may be found. Therefore, for a given attribute or trait, the normal curve represents the norm or average performance of a population and the scores that are above and below the average for that population (Kaplan & Saccuzzo, 2008). For norms to be meaningful, the sample used must reflect the intended population of test-takers. If a test is developed for engineers, the norm sample must consist of engineers in the population. Similarly, if an assessment was developed for nurses, it is not appropriate for use in the selection of chemists. Norm groups can range in size from a few hundred to a hundred thousand. However, the larger the norm group sample size, the closer the approximation to a normal population distribution. Figure 4 shows the normal bell curve.



Figure 4. Normal Bell-shaped Curve Showing the Distribution of Percentages, Standard Deviations and Standard Ten (Sten).
 
Key to the creation of norms is the conversion of raw scores into derived scores. This process provides a system of common metrics that readily facilitates the interpretation of test scores. For MOTIVATION+, the forms of score standardization used were: 1) Mean; 2) Median; 3) Standard deviation; 4) Standard score; 5) Standard ten (sten); and 6) Standard error of measurement. Table 2 describes the various types of derived scores used in the validation of MOTIVATION+.

Table 2. Types of Derived Scores used to Standardise MOTIVATION+.


Sr.
No.	
Derived Score	
Symbol	
Definition and Characteristics




1	



Mean	


µ	
Mean is the average score of all participants who complete an assessment. The mean is calculated by dividing the sum of all scores by the number of participants. The formula for calculating the Mean is:

μ = ( Σ X ) / N,
where ΣX is the sum of all the population observations, and N is the number of population observations.





2	




Median	





x͂	
Median is the score that separates the highest scores from the lowest scores in equal numbers.

The observed values are first ranked in ascending order. The median is then the value that divides the study population into two subsets of equal size (if the number of observations n is even, the median is the half-sum of the rank terms n and n + 1).

Like Mean, Median is a measure of the general tendency of a group of scores.





3	




Standard Deviation	




σ	
Standard Deviation is a measure of the amount of variation across scores for candidates who take an assessment. A small standard deviation indicates scores are close to the mean, while a large value tells us there is much variability across individuals. The formula for calculating Standard Deviation is:

σ = sqrt [ Σ ( Xi - μ ) 2 / N ],
where μ is the population mean, Xi is the ith element from the population, and N is the number of elements in the
population.
 






4	




Standard Score	




Z	Standard Score is an estimate of whether an individual’s score is above average, average or below average compared to peers. Scored on a scale from -4 to 4 with zero in the middle representing average.
Positive scores are above average while negative scores are below average. Z scores are calculated using the formula:

z = (x - μx) / σx,
where x is a normal random variable, μ is the mean of x, and σ is the standard deviation of x.







5	






Standard Ten	






Sten	
For a Standard Score with scores ranging from 1 – 10, here Mean is 5.5 and Standard Deviation equals 2. The formula for calculating Sten Score is:

Sten = ( X− µ/σ ) 2+5.5,
where X is the candidate’s raw score, µ is the mean of raw scores for all candidates, σ is the standard deviation of raw scores on the standardisation sample, multiplied by 2 + 5.5.

Sten Scores are rounded to the nearest whole number with values less than 1 or greater than 10 rounded up or down respectively.




6	


Standard Error of Measurement	



SEM	
SEM is related to test reliability and provides an indication of the dispersion of measurement errors associated with test scores from a group of candidates. It helps us to identify the level of errors when estimating a candidate's true scores from their observed scores. The smaller the SEM, the more accurate are the assessments that are being made. The formula for calculating SEM is:

SEM = 𝜎 S √(1 - reliability)


3.2.	Transforming Raw Scores into Standardised Scores
The 40 questions that measure an individual's motivational values are transformed from raw scores to standardised scores as each value is measured by 8 items. For the 120 components/items measuring motivation, the minimum and maximum overall score for
 
each attribute is 0 and 10. For the satisfaction score, the report reflects the score that was chosen by the participant on the scale from 0 to 10 for each of the 15 areas.

The conversion of raw scores to standardised scores is achieved using a four-step process, as below.

Step 1 Ipsative scores are calculated for each scale by summing all forced-choice responses for each attribute. For each item, 1 is assigned to the endorsed response which is the most preferred, 0 for the response that is not endorsed & -1 to the endorsed response which is the least preferred.

Step 2 Scores are repositioned on a scale from 0 to 10 by using the following formula:

Whereby max refers to the maximum possible score of each scale and min refers to the minimum possible score of each scale.

Step 3 To enable the comparisons between individuals, scores for each scale are standardised using the mean and standard deviation across respondents. This process produces a z score using the formula:

z = (score – (μ/)

Step 4 Standardised scores are then transformed into sten scores from 1 to 10 using the formula:

sten = z * 2 + 5.5

3.3.	Standard Error of Measurement
As no test score is perfect, an estimation of the possible amount of error present within a score is required. This amount of error associated with a score is known as the standard error of measurement (SEM). SEM helps to understand what is a candidate’s true score and the extent to which it differs from the obtained score (Harvill, 1991). In an ideal world, SEM would be calculated by having an individual take an assessment on many occasions over a long period of time. The extent to which there is variation across each score obtained by the candidate would be classified as the standard error of measurement. However, as it is not feasible to assess candidates multiple times, SEM must be calculated using a different approach via the formula as stated below.


 
where,
rxx is the reliability of the test, or the reliability of a factor measured in the test S is the standard deviation

For the factor Idea Sharing in MOTIVATION+, SEM = 1.84 * (sqrt.1- 0.78) i.e. 0.86.

SEM is usually accompanied by a confidence interval (CI), or a range around the estimated “true” score. From this confidence band, we are able to determine the extent to which a score may vary. However, it is important to note that SEM is related to reliability. Therefore, as reliability increases, SEM decreases. Below are some common SEM confidence intervals and their formulas:

68% CI = Score ± SEM
95% CI = Score ± (1.96 * SEM)
99% CI = Score ± (2.58 * SEM)

Thus, at a 95% confidence interval, the factor Idea Sharing with an obtained score of 6 and SEM of 1.59, has a confidence band of 4.31 to 7.69. This means we can be 95% confident that an individual’s true score lies in this range.

3.4.	Choosing an Appropriate Norm Group
Norms make it possible to give meaning to an individual’s score. The interpretation of the same score may therefore differ depending on individual differences, such as study level. For accurate interpretation, it is necessary to compare an individual’s score with a group that is most similar to him/her. To choose the appropriate norm, first identify the country of the candidate. Then from that country, identify the age, gender, level of education, number of years’ work experience, socio-professional status, industry or occupational group that is most appropriate for the candidate. Alternatively, it is possible to create your own internal norms, based on the candidates that have already been assessed.

3.5.	Creating In-house Norms
It is possible to generate internal organisational norms if there is a relevant sample of 100 or more individuals. In-house norms ensure that comparisons between individuals and the reference group reflect the target role, tasks and work environment as closely as possible. In-house norms are particularly valuable in situations where standard norms are not available, roles are new, the organisation has a unique client base or business function, and in cases where the applicant pool for a role is significantly higher or lower than expected.

To create in-house norms, first calculate the mean and standard deviation for each factor on MOTIVATION+ and score the results based on the step-by-step guide outlined in Chapter 3.2. Organisations will then be able to compare individual applicants or employees to a local normal group.
 
3.6.	Link between Scores and Report Content
Personalised Motivation Comments

Comments on the MOTIVATION+ report reflect the scoring process. An individual receives a score of 0 to 10 (as demonstrated in Chapter 3.2.). The comments provide an extensive interpretation of all the factors that are within the three work value groups of the test. They are chosen from six levels according to the score of the candidate, and detail the individual’s motivations according to his/her score. Higher scores are accompanied by stronger descriptors, weaker scores are accompanied by weaker descriptors, and so on. For example, a candidate with a low score on a factor may be described as ‘not appearing to be motivated’ by the relevant factor. A high score on the other hand may describe an individual as being ‘highly motivated’ by the relevant factor. It is important to note that scores should be interpreted in relative terms as the order of the targeted factors is more important than their absolute score.

Personalised Satisfaction and Advice Comments

For each MOTIVATION+ factor, the report also provides an interpretation of the candidate’s current satisfaction level with each factor in their current or most recent role, based on their responses to the satisfaction questions within the questionnaire. The satisfaction comments are chosen from three levels according to the candidate’s score (between 0 to 10). The level of strength and detail within the satisfaction comments corresponds to the combined motivation and satisfaction scores for each factor. In instances where motivation factor scores are high but satisfaction scores are low, additional advice comments indicate actions the candidate could take in order to improve their level of satisfaction. No advice comment will be shown when satisfaction scores are low and motivation factor scores are also low, as the candidate’s responses indicate that this is not a significant motivator for them, so increasing their level of satisfaction in these areas is not relevant.
 
4.	Sample Descriptives
The final sample used to validate MOTIVATION+ consisted of 864 participants (465 females and 399 males), from a large range of occupational groups. Data was collected from the internet crowdsourcing marketplace, Amazon Mechanical Turk, the Research and Innovation Testing Centre of Central Test, as well as HR / Client data in order to ensure that the reported data is objective, non-discriminatory and relevant to the intended population.

5.	Norms
Norms provide information that allow comparisons to be made between individual candidate scores and the scores of a particular reference group.

Norms are standardised scores. Scores on psychological tests like MOTIVATION+ are most commonly interpreted by reference to the norm group scores.

There are two purposes of norms:

1.	Norms indicate an individual’s relative standing in the normative sample to allow the evaluation of his/her performance in relation to others.
2.	Norms provide compared measures that enable a direct comparison of an individual’s performance on a difference test (Anastasi, 1982).

6.	Reliability
Reliability refers to the extent to which an assessment is free from error and the degree to which items on a scale are measuring the same construct. For a measure to be reliable, the same results must be consistently generated over time. Reliability is important for an assessment as it enables us to have confidence that scores on a test are really typical of an individual’s behaviour or attitude. A primary concern, when developing and using tests, is the extent to which random measurement errors influence test performance. If a test is unreliable, it is impossible for it to be valid as accuracy of results (validity) depends on the consistency with which the same results are produced. High reliability of an assessment helps to guard against biases that may be caused from errors in human judgment during the recruitment and selection process.

6.1.	Empirical Reliability
Reliability indicates the degree of precision of the measurement of a test and is, in general, defined as the proportion of the variance of the true score and the variance of the observed test score (i.e. the amount of variance in the measured test score reflecting true differences of the participants). Per definition, reliability takes a value between 0 (only error variance) to 1 (only true variance).

As recommended by Brown & Maydeu-Olivares (2012) and du Trois (2013), reliability for MOTIVATION+ was estimated as empirical reliability. In this approach, the error variance is approximated by the measurement error observed in the posterior distribution of the estimated trait scores. Consequently, the reliability estimates are directly dependent on the observed credibility of the estimated trait scores. It is important to keep in mind that this credibility is not uniform across the whole scale but - depending on the item difficulties - specific to a certain trait estimate (more extreme traits are usually measured with less precision). The reliability estimates below can accordingly be seen as an average of reliabilities over the range of all observed trait scores of a specific trait.

The reliability for MOTIVATION+ was computed on the English-speaking sample (n = 915), and can be seen in Table 12. The median reliability over all subscales of MOTIVATION+ is 0.79.

Table 12. Empirical Reliability for All Factors.

Factor	Reliability
Idea Sharing	0.57
Appraisal	0.61
